Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
12 min
Impossibility of Combining Literal and Metaphorical Meanings
Usul rule with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on combining haqiqah and majaz and evaluate the Shafi'i counterargument.

Core Idea
A single word cannot simultaneously be literal and metaphorical — like a garment cannot be owned and borrowed at once.

This passage explains a fundamental rule in Hanafi usul: it is impossible to intend both the literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings of a word at the same time. Using a vivid analogy, the author illustrates the logical impossibility, then engages with a scholarly dispute. Understanding this rule sharpens your ability to interpret legal texts precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the claim: it is impossible for a single word to simultaneously denote both its literal and metaphorical meanings when both are essentially intended.
The passage asserts mumtani' (impossible).
2
Understand the analogy
Analyse the garment analogy: a garment cannot be both owned and borrowed at the same time. Similarly, a word cannot hold two distinct meanings simultaneously.
The analogy is from usage, not underlying cause.
3
Explore the dispute
Contrast the Hanafi (impossible) and Shafi'i (possible with conditions) positions. Note the proponents.
Al-Shafi'i, al-Jubba'i, 'Abd al-Jabbar hold permissibility.
4
Examine the opposing proof
Evaluate the Shafi'i citation of Q. 4:43 'or you have touched women' as evidence that both literal (touch) and metaphorical (intercourse) can be intended together.
Identify the verse and its claimed dual meaning.
5
See the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi counter: the verse does not require both meanings essentially; one is primary and the other understood secondarily or contextually.
If combination leads to contradiction (e.g., command and threat), it is universally rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحقيقة والمجاز لا يجتمعان] ومن حكم الحقيقة والمجاز استحالة اجتماعهما مرادين بلفظ واحد، كما استحال أن يكون الثوب الواحد على اللابس ملكاً وعارية في زمان واحد، ولهذا قال محمد في "الجامع": · ف والحديث الذي أورده المصنّف أخرجه الزيلعي، وقد وردت بمعناه أحاديث صحيحة، منها ما أخرج مسلم عن أبي سعيد الخدري له قال: كنا نرزق تمر الجمع فكنا نبيع الصاعين بالصاع، فبلغ ذلك رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال: "لا تبيعوا صاعي تمر بصاع ولا صاعي حنطة بصاع ولا درهما بدر همین [مسلم، رقم: ٤٠٨٥] حكم الحقيقة والمجاز الخ: اختلف أهل الأصول في صحة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز من لفظ واحد في الإرادة حال كونهما مقصودين بالحكم بالذات في وقت واحد، بأن يطلق اللفظ ويراد المعنى المجازي والحقيقي في وقت واحد، بحيث يتعلق بكل واحد منهما الحكم بالذات كما تقول: "لا تقتل الأسد، وتريد به الأسد والرجل الشجاع معا، فذهب أصحابنا وعامة أهل الأدب وأهل التحقيق من أصحاب الشافعي وعامة المتكلمين إلى امتناعه لغة وذهب الشافعي وعامة أصحابه والجبائي وعبد الجبار من المعتزلة إلى أنه يصح إن لم يمتنع الجمع بينهما، كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿أَوْ لا مَسْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ﴾ (النساء:٤٣)، فإنه يراد منه المس باليد والجماع. وأما لو امتنع كالأمر ، مثل: "افعل" فإنه حقيقة في الوجوب ومجاز في التهديد، فلا يصح الجمع بينهما، وإلا يلزم كون الشيء مأموراً به مثاباً على فعله ومهدوداً عليه معاقباً على فعله كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاعْمَلُوا مَا شِئْتُمْ (فصلت: ٤٠ ) ، ولا نزاع في جواز اجتماعهما بحسب احتمال اللفظ إياهما، أو بحسب التناول الظاهري بشبهة من غير إرادة كما سيأتي، وكذلك لا نزاع في جواز استعمال اللفظ في معنى مجازى تكون الحقيقة من أفراده على سبيل عموم المجاز؛ لأن المراد هنا المعنى المجازى فقط، وأما الحقيقي فيفهم تبعاً، وإليهما أشار بقوله: "مرادين" ولا نزاع في جواز الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز بلفظين وإليه أشار بقوله: "بلفظ واحد"، واستدل المصنّف على عدم جواز الجمع بقوله: كما استحال إلخ : المراد منه التشبيه من حيث الاستعمال، فكما أن استعمال الثوب الواحد في حالة واحدة على سبيل العارية والملك جميعا محال، سواء كان بنسبة شخص أو شخصين، كذلك استعمال اللفظ الواحد في حالة واحدة بطريق الحقيقة والمجاز معاً ممتنع، سواء كان بنسبة معنى أو معنيين، فاللفظ بمنزلة اللباس والمعنى بمنزلة الشخص، والحقيقة كالثوب المملوك، والمجاز كالثوب المستعار، وهذا نظير الاستحالة لا مناطه، فالمناقشة فيه غير مفيد، وهذا الاستدلال على رأي المصنّف من أنه ذهب إلى استحالة الاجتماع عقلاً، وذهب أكثر المحققين إلى أن استحالة لغةً كما أشرنا إليه سابقاً. ولهذا : أي لأجل استحالة الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز. * الجمع والجمع الدقل أو ضعيف عن التمر.
[Literal and metaphorical meanings cannot be combined] Among the rulings of literal and metaphorical is the impossibility of them being intended together by a single word, just as it is impossible for a single garment worn by a person to be both owned and borrowed at the same time. For this, Muhammad said in al-Jami': ... And the hadith that the author cited was extracted by al-Zayla'i, and there are sound hadiths with the same meaning, among them what Muslim transmitted from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri who said: We were given dates of mixed quality, so we used to sell two sa''s for one sa''. That reached the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and he said: 'Do not sell two sa''s of dates for one sa'', nor two sa''s of wheat for one sa'', nor a dirham for two dirhams.' [Muslim, no. 4085] The ruling of literal and metaphorical etc.: The scholars of usul differed on the validity of combining literal and metaphorical meanings from one word in intention when both are intended essentially for the ruling at the same time. That is, the word is uttered and both the metaphorical and literal meanings are intended simultaneously such that the ruling applies to each essentially. For example, if you say: 'Do not kill the lion,' intending both the animal and the brave man. Our companions (Hanafis) and most literary scholars and the researchers among al-Shafi'i's followers and most theologians hold its linguistic impossibility. Al-Shafi'i and most of his followers, al-Jubba'i and 'Abd al-Jabbar among the Mu'tazila, hold that it is permissible if combining the two is not impossible, as in the verse 'or you have touched women' (4:43), where it intends both touching with the hand and intercourse. But if it is impossible, like the imperative 'do' which is literal for obligation and metaphorical for threat, then combining them is invalid; otherwise, the thing would be both commanded (rewarded for doing) and threatened (punished for doing), as in the verse 'Do whatever you wish' (41:40). There is no disagreement about the permissibility of them coming together in terms of the word's probability or in terms of apparent understanding due to a resemblance without intention, as will come. Also, there is no disagreement about the permissibility of using the word in a metaphorical sense where the literal meaning is among its individuals by way of the generality of metaphor, because the intended here is only the metaphorical, while the literal is understood secondarily. The author indicated these with his saying 'intended'. And there is no disagreement about the permissibility of combining literal and metaphorical with two words; the author indicated this with his saying 'with one word'. The author argued for the impermissibility of combination with his saying: 'just as it is impossible' etc. The intended is analogy in terms of usage. Just as using one garment in one state both as a loan and ownership is impossible, whether attributed to one person or two, similarly using one word in one state both literally and metaphorically is impossible, whether attributed to one meaning or two. The word is like the garment, the meaning like the person; literal is like owned garment, metaphorical like borrowed garment. This is an analogy of impossibility, not its underlying cause, so disputing it is not useful. This argument is based on the author's view that the impossibility is rational, while most investigators hold it is linguistic, as we indicated earlier. And for this: meaning because of the impossibility of combining literal and metaphorical. * The combination and the mixed or weak dates.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the impossibility of combining literal (haqiqah) and metaphorical (majaz) meanings in a single word when both are intended essentially for the ruling. The author begins by stating the rule, using the analogy of a garment that cannot be both owned and borrowed simultaneously. He then cites a hadith from al-Jami' (of Imam Muhammad) as evidence. The passage then presents the ikhtilaf among scholars: the Hanafis, most literary scholars, and most theologians hold it is linguistically impossible; al-Shafi'i and his followers, along with some Mu'tazilites, hold it is possible unless combining leads to contradiction. Examples include Q. 4:43 ('or you have touched women') where both meanings are possible, versus Q. 41:40 ('Do whatever you wish') where combining obligation and threat is impossible. The author clarifies that there is no dispute about cases where the word can be interpreted either way without intention, or where the literal meaning is subsumed under a general metaphor. The analogical argument (qiyas) is presented as a rational demonstration. The passage ends with a note that the author (presumably the matn author) holds it as rationally impossible, while most investigators view it as linguistically impossible.

Key Terms
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): A word used in its original meaning as placed by language.
المجاز (al-majāz): A word used in other than its original meaning due to a relation and contextual clue.
الاجتماع (al-ijtimāʿ): Combining both literal and metaphorical meanings in a single use of a word intending both essentially.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is it impossible to combine literal and metaphorical meanings in one word?
A) Because it would require the word to be in two places at once
B) By analogy with a single garment that cannot be both owned and borrowed
C) Because the Quran forbids it
D) Because it would cause contradiction in all cases

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, a single word cannot simultaneously carry both its literal and metaphorical meanings when both are intended essentially; the garment analogy illustrates this.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
8 min
The Nature of a Vow as an Oath by Consequence: A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Analyzing the relationship between vow and oath in a single utterance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key usul principles involved in the dispute over 'lillahi 'alayya' and apply them to distinguish between literal and consequential meanings in Hanafi legal analysis.

Core Idea
The utterance 'lillahi 'alayya' is a vow literally but an oath by legal consequence, avoiding forbidden combination of haqiqah and majaz in one word.

When someone says 'lillahi 'alayya', does the utterance combine a literal vow and a figurative oath? This question triggered a significant usul debate in the Hanafi school. In this lesson, we will dissect the arguments, examining the role of intention and the Quranic proof that making permissible things obligatory resembles making permissible things prohibited."

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What is the problem? The utterance 'lillahi 'alayya' appears to combine a literal meaning (vow) and a figurative meaning (oath). Hanafi usul forbids combining haqiqah and majaz in one word.
This is the questioner's understanding that the author seeks to refute.
2
Examine the first answer
The author initially presents: the utterance is a vow by wording (literal) and an oath by consequence (mu'jab), so no combination in one word. The consequence (obligating a permissible act) makes it an oath, similar to how prohibiting permissible things is an oath (Quran 66:1-2). An analogy with purchasing a relative is given.
Note the analogy: purchase gives ownership, ownership of a relative gives emancipation—both by consequence.
3
Analyze the critique
The author objects: if the oath were by consequence alone, intention would not be needed, but Hanafis require intention for oaths. This reveals a tension.
The critique is that consequences are independent of intention, yet the school conditions intention for the utterance to be an oath.
4
Explore Shams al-A'imma's resolution
He argues that 'lillah' is an oath formula (like 'wallah') and 'alayya' is a vow formula; thus they are two separate words, avoiding combination entirely.
This resolution preserves the Hanafi requirement of intention: 'lillah' as an oath formula requires intention.
5
Determine the practical outcome (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the utterance is considered an oath by wording, intention is required; if considered a vow by wording with oath by consequence, intention may still be needed per some views. The mufta bihi is to treat it as an oath by wording due to 'lillah' being an oath formula.
This affects whether a person incurs the obligation of expiation for breaking the implied oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما مسألة النذر فليس بجمع أيضاً بل هو نذر بصيغته يمين بموجبه وهو الإيجاب؛ واليمين أي بحكمه لأن إيجاب المباح يصلح يميناً كتحريم المباح، وهذا كشراء القريب، فإنه تملك بصيغته وتحرير بموجبه. ولما فرغ عن الجواب عن مسألة: لايضع قدمه في دار فلان شرع في الجواب عن مسألة النذر واليمين. فليس بجمع: يعني ليس في تلك المسئلة جمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز كما فهم السائل؛ لأن المراد من الجمع الممتنع أن يكون اجتماعهما من صيغة واحدة، وهنا ليس كذلك بل هو أي الكلام المذكور نذر بصيغته؛ لأن قوله: "الله علي" صيغة نذر وضع له في الشرع، وهو معناه الموضوع له، فلم يثبت من الصيغة إلا المعنى الحقيقي وهو النذر، لا المجازي وهو اليمين، فلم يلزم الجمع بينهما من صيغته لكن هذا الكلام يمين بموجبه . وهو الإيجاب: إذ المقصود من صيغة النذر إيجاب المنذور الذي كان مباح الترك قبله؛ إذ لو كان واجباً قبله لم يصح النذر على ما عرف، فلمّا صار واجباً من النذر كان يمينا. يصلح يمينا : لأن الشيء إذا صار واجباً كان تركه حراماً، وقد كان قبل ذلك مباحاً، فصار إيجاب المباح كتحريم المباح، وتحريم المباح أي الحلال يمين؛ لأن النبي ﷺ لو كان حرم على نفسه مارية أو العسل ، قال : وَلِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللَّهُ لَكَ (التحريم:۱)، فسمّى الله ذلك يميناً. قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَقَدْ فَرَضَ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ تَحِلَّةَ أَيْمَانِكُمْ﴾ (التحريم: ۲)، فثبت أن اليمين إنما حصلت من موجب الكلام لا هي مراد بطريق المجاز. بصيغته إذ قوله : "اشتريت" إنما وضع شرعاً لإفادة الملك بموجبه: إذ يحصل من الشراء التملك، والملك في القريب يوجب العتق شرعاً، فكان الشراء إعتاقاً بواسطة حكمه لا بصيغته أقول: هذا الجواب لا يستقيم؛ لأن اليمين لو كانت موجب الكلام لثبتت بدون النية أيضاً؛ لأن موجب الشيء لايحتاج إلى النية، كشراء القريب يثبت العتق فيه بغير النية أيضاً، والحال أنكم تشترطون النية لثبوت اليمين؟ اللهم إلا أن يقال: إنها كالحقيقة المحجورة، فلذا يحتاج إلى النية، وقد تكلّف الشراح في الجواب، وأجاب شمس الأئمة أن قوله: "لله" بمعنى "والله" صيغة يمين. وقوله: "علي" صيغة نذر، فلا يجتمعان في لفظ واحد، فلم يحصل الجمع في كلمة واحدة في كلمتين، وذلك غير مستبعد فتأمل.
As for the issue of the vow, it is not a combination either, but it is a vow by its wording, an oath by its consequence, which is obligation; and the oath is by its ruling because making permissible things obligatory is like making permissible things prohibited. And this is like the purchase of a relative, for it is ownership by its wording and emancipation by its consequence. When the answer to the issue 'He does not place his foot in so-and-so's house' was completed, he began the answer to the issue of the vow and the oath. 'It is not a combination' means there is no combination between literal and figurative in that issue as the questioner understood; because what is meant by the forbidden combination is that they meet from a single wording, and here it is not so. Rather, that speech is a vow by its wording because his saying 'lillahi 'alayya' is a vow formula established in the Shariah, and it is its literal meaning. So from the wording only the literal meaning (the vow) is established, not the figurative (the oath). Thus combination from one wording does not occur. But this speech is an oath by its consequence, which is obligation. For the purpose of the vow formula is to obligate the vowed thing which was permissible to leave before; since if it had been obligatory before, the vow would not be valid as known. So when it becomes obligatory from the vow, it becomes an oath. It is valid as an oath because when something becomes obligatory, leaving it becomes prohibited, while before it was permissible. So making permissible obligatory is like making permissible prohibited, and making permissible (i.e., lawful) prohibited is an oath. For the Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited Mariyah or honey for himself; Allah said: 'Why do you prohibit what Allah has made lawful for you?' (66:1), and Allah called that an oath. Allah said: 'Allah has already ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths' (66:2). So it is established that the oath arises from the consequence of the speech, not from the figurative meaning. By its wording: his saying 'I bought' is only established in Shariah to convey ownership by its consequence, for from purchase comes ownership, and ownership of a relative necessitates emancipation by Shariah. Thus purchase is emancipation by intermediary of its ruling, not by its wording. I say: This answer is not sound, because if the oath were the consequence of the speech, it would be established without intention as well; for the consequence of something does not need intention, as purchase of a relative establishes emancipation even without intention. Yet you (the proponents of the first answer) stipulate intention for establishing the oath? Unless it is said that it is like a restricted literal meaning, hence it needs intention. The commentators have exerted themselves in answering. Shams al-A'imma answered that his saying 'lillah' is an oath formula like 'wallah', and his saying 'alayya' is a vow formula. So they do not combine in one word; combination in two words is not far-fetched. So ponder.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute over the nature of the utterance 'lillahi 'alayya'. The author first presents the view that this utterance is a vow (nidhr) by its wording and an oath (yamin) by its consequence (mu'jab). The reasoning is that the formula is literally a vow, but since it obligates something that was previously permissible, it legally becomes an oath, similar to how making permissible things prohibited is an oath (Quran 66:1-2). An analogy to buying a relative is given: the wording 'I bought' means ownership literally, but the consequence is emancipation. Then the author critiques this view, arguing that if the oath were merely a consequence, it would not require intention, whereas the Hanafi school stipulates intention for an oath. He presents Shams al-A'imma's resolution: the words 'lillah' and 'alayya' are from separate wordings—'lillah' is an oath and 'alayya' is a vow—so no combination of literal and figurative meanings occurs in a single word. This avoids the forbidden combination. The passage uses Quranic proofs and analogy to clarify the madhhab position.

Key Terms
نذر (nidhr): A vow: obligating an act upon oneself that was previously permissible, through a specific formula.
يمين (yamin): An oath: binding oneself to do or refrain from something, with expiation for breaking.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning intended by the word as originally placed.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative meaning derived from the word through usage or context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for considering the utterance 'lillahi 'alayya' an oath?
A) The literal meaning of the word 'lillah' is an oath.
B) The figurative meaning derived from the context.
C) The legal consequence of obligating a permissible act.
D) The mere presence of the word 'alayya'.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul resolves the apparent combination of literal and figurative by distinguishing wording from consequence, or by separating the utterance into two words, thereby preserving the rule that intention is required for oaths.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Hanafi Refutation of Mafhum al-Mukhalafah
Critique of Shafi'i classification of mantuq and mafhum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects mafhum al-mukhalafah as a valid proof and distinguish it from mafhum al-muwafaqah (dalalah).

Core Idea
Mafhum al-mukhalafah is invalid in Hanafi usul because it relies on speculative implication, not direct indication.

This passage from Muntakhab al-Husami addresses a fundamental methodological difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: the validity of implied meanings not directly stated. The Shafi'i school classifies textual indications into explicit (mantuq) and implicit (mafhum), and further divides mafhum into muwafaqah (consistent) and mukhalafah (opposite). While the Hanafi school accepts mafhum al-muwafaqah as a valid proof (calling it dalalah al-nass), it firmly rejects mafhum al-mukhalafah. Understanding this distinction is crucial for deriving rulings correctly according to Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i classification of textual indications
Read the passage carefully to understand how Shafi'i usul divides indications into mantuq (what is directly stated) and mafhum (what is inferred). Note that mantuq includes ibarah, isharah, and iqtida—categories the Hanafis also accept.
The passage states that mantuq is 'what the word indicates in the place of utterance'.
2
Locate the Hanafi acceptance of mafhum al-muwafaqah (dalalah)
Mafhum al-muwafaqah is the implicit meaning that agrees with the explicit ruling. The Hanafis accept this as a valid proof under the term 'dalalah al-nass'. Identify where the passage equates this with Hanafi terminology.
The text says: 'This is what we have called dalalah al-nass.'
3
Understand the rejection of mafhum al-mukhalafah and its subtypes
Mafhum al-mukhalafah infers the opposite ruling for unmentioned cases from mention of conditions, descriptions, or proper nouns. The passage lists subtypes: mafhum al-laqab, al-shart, and al-wasf. Grasp why the Hanafis consider these invalid: because the implication is not linguistically necessary.
The text says: 'And these types of mafhum are invalid according to us; citing them as proofs is not sound.'
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Consider a practical example: the statement 'If water is used, purification is valid.' Shafi'i might use mafhum al-shart to rule that without water, purification is invalid. Hanafis reject this as a decisive proof, though they may consider it a circumstantial indicator (qarīna). This step solidifies the methodological difference.
The thamarah al-khilaf affects how evidence is weighted in istinbat.

فصل واعلم أن عامة الأصوليين من أصحاب الشافعي الله قسموا الدلالة إلى منطوق ومفهوم، وقالوا: المنطوق ما دلّ عليه اللفظ في محلّ النطق، وهذا يشمل ما سميناه عبارةً أو إشارة أو اقتضاء، والمفهوم ما دلّ عليه اللفظ، لا في محلّ النطق. ثم المفهوم عندهم نوعان: مفهوم موافقة، وهو أن يفهم من اللفظ حال المسكوت عنه على وفق المنطوق، وهذا ما سميناه دلالة النص، ومفهوم مخالفة، وهو أن يفهم منه حاله خلاف ما فهم من المنطوق. ثم المفهوم المخالف على أنواع؛ لأنه إن فهم من اسم العلم فهو مفهوم اللقب، وإن فهم من الشرط أو الوصف فهو مفهوم الشرط أو الوصف، وتلك الأقسام من المفهوم فاسدة عندنا، لا يصح الاحتجاج بها كما قال المصنف.
Section: Know that most usul scholars from the followers of al-Shafi'i divided indication into mantuq and mafhum. They said: mantuq is what the word indicates in the place of utterance, and this includes what we have called ibarah, isharah, and iqtida. Mafhum is what the word indicates but not in the place of utterance. Then mafhum according to them is of two types: mafhum al-muwafaqah, which is understanding from the word the condition of the unmentioned case in agreement with the mentioned case—and this is what we have called dalalah al-nass. And mafhum al-mukhalafah, which is understanding from it the condition contrary to what is understood from the mentioned case. Then mafhum al-mukhalafah has types: if understood from a proper noun, it is mafhum al-laqab; if from a condition or description, it is mafhum al-shart or mafhum al-wasf. And these types of mafhum are invalid according to us; citing them as proofs is not sound, as the author said.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents the Shafi'i classification of textual indication into mantuq (explicit/uttered) and mafhum (implicit/inferred). The Hanafi author acknowledges that mantuq includes what Hanafis call ibarah, isharah, and iqtida—so part of the Shafi'i classification aligns with Hanafi categories. However, mafhum is divided into two: mafhum al-muwafaqah (which coincides with Hanafi dalalah) and mafhum al-mukhalafah (which includes subcategories like mafhum al-laqab, al-shart, al-wasf). The text asserts that according to Hanafi usul, these subcategories of mafhum al-mukhalafah are invalid as proofs. The reason, as known from elsewhere, is that the indication of such mafhum is based on the implication of the opposite, which is not a reliable method in Hanafi usul. The passage reinforces that the only valid types of textual indication are the four: ibarah, isharah, dalalah, and iqtida. The author (Muntakhab) clearly sides with the Hanafi position, marking a major methodological difference between the schools.

Key Terms
المنطوق (al-mantuq): What the word indicates in the place of utterance, including ibarah, isharah, and iqtida.
المفهوم (al-mafhum): What the word indicates but not in the place of utterance, divided into muwafaqah and mukhalafah.
مفهوم المخالفة (mafhum al-mukhalafah): Understanding the opposite ruling for the unmentioned case from a mention of a condition, description, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a valid proof in Hanafi usul?
A) Mantuq (explicit indication)
B) Mafhum al-muwafaqah (dalalah)
C) Mafhum al-mukhalafah (opposite implication)
D) All of the above are valid

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul rejects mafhum al-mukhalafah as a standalone proof, accepting only mantuq and mafhum al-muwafaqah (dalalah) as valid textual indications.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Cause, Condition, and Attribute in Pre-Breach Expiation
Dispute on offering expiation before breaking an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on pre-breach expiation by analyzing the concepts of attribute, condition, and cause, and apply the Hanafi usul principle that condition attaches to cause, not ruling.

Core Idea
Absence of condition negates cause, not ruling; attributes are not always conditions.

This passage tackles a core usul dispute: can one expiate an oath before breaking it? The Hanafi school answers 'yes' for financial expiations but 'no' for bodily ones, while al-Shafi'i permits both. The disagreement hinges on how we understand the relationship between a condition (shart), a cause (sabab), and an attribute (wasf). By working through this passage, you will learn the Hanafi taxonomy of attributes and the principle that conditions suspend the cause, not the ruling itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Read the passage to find the central legal question: can a person offer expiation (kaffarah) before actually breaking an oath (hanth)? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ on this.
The issue is not whether expiation is required, but whether it can be performed preemptively.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafi school holds that pre-breach expiation is valid only for financial obligations (e.g., feeding, clothing) and not for bodily ones (e.g., fasting). The reason: financial obligations allow separation between the obligation itself and its performance (like deferred payment), while bodily obligations are inseparable from performance.
The key principle: 'wajib mali' can be separated from its performance; 'wajib badani' cannot.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i argument
Al-Shafi'i argues that attributes describing the cause (e.g., 'adulterer', 'thief') function as conditions for the ruling. Therefore, the absence of the attribute (i.e., not yet breaking the oath) negates the ruling, making pre-breach expiation invalid for both types. He also claims that conditions attach directly to the ruling, not the cause.
The Shafi'i view treats 'sin' (hanth) as a condition that must exist before expiation is valid.
4
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi author refutes the Shafi'i position on two fronts: (1) Attributes are not always conditions; they have three degrees (incidental, conditional, causal). Only the middle degree functions like a condition. (2) Even if an attribute were a condition, in Hanafi usul the condition attaches to the cause, not the ruling. Using the divorce example ('If you enter, you are divorced'), the condition suspends the cause (the pronouncement) from taking effect, so its absence means the cause is not yet effective, not that the ruling is negated.
This response shows that the Hanafi view is more nuanced: absence of condition does not automatically negate the ruling; it only prevents the cause from operating.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Under Hanafi law, financial expiation before breaking an oath is valid; bodily expiation is not. Under Shafi'i law, both are valid. This difference affects how a mufti advises someone who wishes to expiate in advance.
The thamarah al-khilaf is the actual ruling that follows from the usul disagreement.

الفرق بين الواجب المالي والبدني
وجوز التكفير بالمال قبل الحنث؛ لأن الوجوب حاصل بالسبب على أصله، أي وجوب الكفارة ووجوب الأداء متراخ عنه بالشرط، والمالي يحتمل الفصل بين وجوبه ووجوب أدائه أما البدني فلا يحتمل الفصل، فلما تأخر الأداء لم يبق الوجوب، وإنا نقول: بأن أقصى درجات الوصف إذا كان مؤثرا أن يكون علةً للحكم، وجوّز التكفير إلخ هذا تفريع آخر على ما ذهب إليه الشافعي له، فإذا حلف: والله لا أفعل كذا، و لم يحنث بعد ولكنه كفّر قبل الحنث، بأن أعتق رقبة أو أطعم عشرة مساكين أو كساهم جاز عنده. حاصل بالسبب وهو اليمين، ولهذ يضاف الكفارة إليها، فيقال: كفارة اليمين إلا أن الحنث شرط لوجوب أدائها، والتعليق بالشرط مقدّر، فكأنه قال الحالف إن حنثت فعلي كفارة يمين فإذا وجد السبب يصح الحكم مرتباً عليه. ووجوب الأداء إلخ: يعني أن نفس وجوب الكفارة إنما هو بالسبب، وهو اليمين، فإذا وجد السبب ترتب الحكم عليه، وأما وجوب أداء الكفارة فهو يتأخّر عن السبب ويحصل بالشرط وهو الحنث، فلايكون وجوب الأداء قبل الحنث . ثم أشار إلى الفرق بين الكفارة بالمال وبين الكفارة بالصوم، حيث لايجوز تعجيلها قبل الحنث عنده، بخلاف الأولى. يحتمل الفصل إلخ فينفصل نفس وجوبه عن وجوب أدائه. ألا ترى أن من اشترى شيئاً إلى شهرين مثلاً، فيثبت الوجوب بنفس العقد، ولا يثبت وجوب الأداء قبل حلول الأجل. فلا يحتمل الفصل: يعني ليس الفصل بين نفس وجوبه ووجوب أدائه، بل نفس وجوبه ليس إلا أدائه . ألا ترى أن الصلاة ليست إلا فعلا معلوماً، وكذا الصوم، فوجوب الصلاة والصوم لا يكون إلا الأداء. وجوب فلما تأخر إلخ ونحن نقول: هذا الفرق ساقط عن أصله؛ لأن ذات الأموال إنما تقصد في حقوق العباد، فيصح هذا الفرق في حقوق العباد، وأما في حقوق الله تعالى فالمقصود هو الأداء، فيكون كالبدني في عدم وجوبه عن وجوب أدائه.
وجوب انفكاك نفس ثم أجاب المصنّف عن استدلال الشافعي حله بقوله: وإنا نقول إلخ هذا جواب عن أصل الشافعي له الذي كان منوطا بأمرين: الأول أنه ألحق الوصف بالشرط، والثاني اعتبر التعليق بالشرط عاملاً في منع الحكم دون السبب كما مرّ تشريحه آنفاً تحت قوله : "وحاصله"، فقوله: بأن أقصى درجات الوصف إلخ" منع على الأمر الأول، وحاصله أنا لانسلّم أن الوصف ملحق بالشرط على الإطلاق، بل للوصف ثلاث درجات، أدناها أن يكون اتفاقياً كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَرَبَائِبُكُمُ اللَّاتِي فِي حُجُورِكُمْ ﴾ (النساء: ٢٣)، وهذا القسم لايوجب العدم عند عدمه بلا خلاف، وأعلاها أن يكون بمعنى العلة بأن يكون مؤثراً في الحكم، كما في قوله تعالى: الزانية والزاني والسارق والسارقة فإن وصف الزنا هو المؤثر في وجوب الجلد، ووصف السرقة هو المؤثر في وجوب القطع، وذلك لأن الحكم متى رتب على الاسم المشتق كان مأخذه علة للحكم على ما عرف، وإذا كانت أعلى درجات الوصف أن يكون علة لايدلّ عدمها على عدم الحكم؛ لأن عدم العلة لايدلّ على عدم الحكم بلا خلاف ما لم يثبت اختصاصه بها؛ لجواز ثبوت الحكم حينئذ بعلل شتى، فأدناها أولى أن لا يدلّ عدمه على عدم الحكم، وأوسطها أن يكون بمعنى الشرط، فثبت أن الوصف ليس على الإطلاق ملحقاً بالشرط. ولو كان شرطا إلخ: هذا منع على الأمر الثاني ووجوب عن مفهوم الشرط أيضاً. وحاصله وإن سلّمنا أن الوصف قد يكون ملحقاً بالشرط ولكن لانسلّم أن عدمه يدلّ على عدم الحكم؛ لأن عدم الشرط لايدلّ عندنا على عدم الحكم ، فكذا عدم الوصف الملحق به وذلك لأن الشرط وهو قوله: إن دخلت الدار" إنما دخل على السبب، وهو قوله: أنت طالق؛ لأنه هو المذكور دون غيره من الحكم، وهو وقوع الطلاق، فعدم الشرط إنما يدلّ على عدم المشروط، وهو السبب دون الحكم. ففي قوله: "إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق" علّق قوله "فأنت طالق" وهو السبب بالشرط، وهو قوله: "إن دخلت الدار، فلو لم يقل: إن دخلت الدار، لوقع الطلاق بالفعل، فقوله: "إن دخلت "الدار" منع السبب من اتصاله بمحله وهو المرأة، فقوله: "أنت طالق" لم يتصل ولم يقع على محله لأجل قوله: إن دخلت الدار. لا ينعقد سبباً فلم يكن السبب موجودا قبل وجود الشرط، فكان عدم الحكم لعدم السبب، لا لعدم الشرط.
ولهذا لوحلف إلخ: تائيد وإلزام على الخصم بما هو مجمع عليه أي ولأجل أن المعلّق لا ينعقد سبباً، حتى لو حلف أحد والله لا يطلق امرأته فقال: إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق لا يحنث بهذا القول ما لم يوجد الشرط وهو دخول الدار، وذلك لأن قوله: "إن دخلت الدار" منع قوله : أنت طالق" عن اتصاله بمحله، فكأنه لم يقل هذا القول ما لم يوجد الدخول، فحين يوجد دخول الدار يوجد التكلم بقوله: "أنت طالق" فيحنث. وهذا بخلاف إلخ جواب عن استدلال الشافعي تقرير الاستدلال أن الشرط إنما يردّ على الحكم دون السبب؛ إذ لا معنى لجعل قوله: "أنت طالق" معدوماً، مع كونه موجوداً حسّا، فلا محالة هو داخل على الحكم دون السبب،
The corrupt aspects. The difference between financial and bodily obligations. He permitted expiation with wealth before the breach because obligation is established by the cause according to his principle, meaning the obligation of expiation, and the obligation of performance is deferred by the condition. Financial obligation allows separation between its obligation and the obligation of its performance, while bodily does not allow separation. So when performance is delayed, the obligation ceases. And we say: The highest degree of an attribute, if effective, is to be a cause of the ruling. 'And he permitted expiation etc.' This is another branch based on what al-Shafi'i adopted. If one swears: 'By Allah, I will not do such-and-such' and has not yet broken the oath but expiates before breaking it, by freeing a slave or feeding ten poor persons or clothing them, it is valid according to him. It is established by the cause, which is the oath, and therefore expiation is attributed to it, and it is said: 'Expiation of the oath', except that breaking the oath is a condition for the obligation of its performance, and the suspension by condition is hypothetical, as if the swearer said: 'If I break the oath, then upon me is the expiation of an oath.' So when the cause exists, the ruling is properly based on it. 'And the obligation of performance etc.' means: The very obligation of expiation is by the cause, i.e., the oath; when the cause exists, the ruling is based on it. As for the obligation of performing the expiation, it is delayed from the cause and occurs by the condition, i.e., the breach. So the obligation of performance does not exist before the breach. Then he pointed to the difference between expiation with wealth and expiation with fasting, as he does not permit its acceleration before the breach, unlike the first. 'Allows separation etc.' so the obligation itself is separated from the obligation of its performance. Do you not see that if one buys something with deferred payment of two months, for example, the obligation is established by the contract itself, and the obligation of performance is not established before the term? 'Does not allow separation': meaning there is no separation between the obligation itself and the obligation of its performance; rather the obligation itself is nothing but performance. Do you not see that prayer is nothing but a specific action, and likewise fasting, so the obligation of prayer and fasting is only performance. 'So when it was delayed etc.' And we say: This distinction is invalid from its root because the essence of wealth is sought in rights of people, so this distinction is valid in rights of people. As for the rights of Allah, the purpose is performance, so it is like bodily acts in that there is no obligation apart from its performance. 'Obligation of disconnection of the self' Then the author responded to al-Shafi'i's argument with his saying: 'And we say etc.' This is a response to al-Shafi'i's argument which was based on two matters: first, he equated the attribute to the condition; second, he considered the suspension by condition as a factor preventing the ruling without the cause, as explained previously under 'And its summary'. So his saying: 'Because the highest degrees of the attribute etc.' is a refutation of the first matter. Its summary: We do not concede that the attribute is equivalent to the condition absolutely. Rather the attribute has three degrees: the lowest is that it is incidental, as in the verse 'and your stepdaughters who are in your guardianship' (an-Nisa' 23), and this category does not necessitate non-existence when absent without dispute. The highest is that it is in the sense of cause, i.e., effective in the ruling, as in the verse 'the adulteress and the adulterer, the thief and the thiefess', for the description of adultery is the effective factor in the obligation of flogging, and the description of theft is the effective factor in the obligation of amputation. This is because when the ruling is attached to a derived noun, its origin is a cause for the ruling, as is known. And when the highest degree of attribute is to be a cause, its absence does not indicate the absence of the ruling, because the absence of the cause does not indicate the absence of the ruling without dispute unless its exclusivity is established, because the ruling could be established by various causes. So the lowest degree is more fitting that its absence does not indicate the absence of the ruling. The middle degree is that it is in the sense of condition. Thus it is established that the attribute is not absolutely equated to the condition. 'And if it were a condition etc.': This is a refutation of the second matter and an objection to the implication of condition also. Its summary: Even if we concede that the attribute might be equated to the condition, we do not concede that its absence indicates the absence of the ruling, because the absence of the condition does not indicate in our view the absence of the ruling. So likewise the absence of the attribute equated to it. This is because the condition—i.e., 'If you enter the house'—only enters upon the cause, i.e., 'You are divorced', because it is the mentioned [part] without the ruling, i.e., the occurrence of divorce. So the absence of the condition only indicates the absence of the conditioned, i.e., the cause, not the ruling. So in his saying: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', he suspended his saying 'you are divorced'—which is the cause—by the condition, i.e., 'If you enter the house'. If he did not say 'If you enter the house', divorce would occur by the statement. So his saying 'If you enter the house' prevented the cause from connecting to its subject, i.e., the woman. So his saying 'You are divorced' did not connect and did not occur on its subject due to his saying 'If you enter the house'. It does not become a cause, so the cause did not exist before the condition existed. Thus the absence of the ruling is due to the absence of the cause, not due to the absence of the condition. 'For this reason, if one swears etc.': A confirmation and binding of the opponent by what is agreed upon. That is, because the suspended does not become a cause, so if one swears 'By Allah, I will not divorce my wife' and says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', he does not violate his oath by this statement until the condition—entering the house—exists. This is because his saying 'If you enter the house' prevented his saying 'You are divorced' from connecting to its subject, as if he had not said this statement until the entry occurs. So when entry occurs, the utterance of 'You are divorced' occurs, and he violates his oath. 'And this is contrary to etc.': A response to al-Shafi'i's argument. The presentation of the argument is that the condition only refers to the ruling, not the cause, because it is meaningless to consider his saying 'You are divorced' as non-existent while it is physically existent. So inevitably it is directed at the ruling, not the cause.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul dispute with al-Shafi'i regarding the permissibility of offering expiation (kaffarah) before breaking an oath. The discussion involves the distinction between financial and bodily obligations, and the nature of conditions (shart) versus causes (sabab) and attributes (wasf). The author first states the Hanafi position that financial expiation (e.g., feeding or clothing) can be performed before the oath is broken because the obligation of expiation is established by the oath (the cause) while the obligation to perform it is conditional upon the breach. Financial obligations allow a separation between the obligation itself and its performance (like a deferred payment), but bodily acts (like fasting or prayer) do not allow such separation—their obligation is tied to performance. The Shafi'i view permits pre-breach expiation for both types, but the Hanafi limits it to financial. Then the Hanafi author refutes al-Shafi'i's argument that an attribute (like 'adulterer') functions like a condition. The author explains three degrees of attributes: lowest (incidental), middle (conditional), highest (causative). Only the middle is like a condition. Even if an attribute were a condition, the absence of a condition does not necessarily negate the ruling in Hanafi usul—rather it negates the cause. The condition attaches to the cause, not the ruling directly. The author illustrates with divorce: in 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', the condition 'If you enter' attaches to the cause (the pronouncement of divorce) preventing it from taking effect, not to the ruling itself. Therefore, the absence of the condition merely means the cause is not yet effective, not that the ruling is negated. This supports the Hanafi distinction that financial expiation can be pre-breach because the cause (oath) already exists, even if the condition (breach) does not, whereas for bodily acts the 'obligation' is inseparable from performance, so no pre-breach expiation.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition that suspends the effect of a cause; attached to the cause, not directly to the ruling.
سبب (sabab): The cause that establishes the obligation (e.g., the oath establishes obligation of expiation).
وصف (wasf): An attribute or description that can be incidental, conditional, or causal.
واجب مالي (wajib mali): An obligation involving wealth (e.g., giving money, food, clothing).
واجب بدني (wajib badani): An obligation involving bodily action (e.g., prayer, fasting).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, can expiation by fasting be offered before breaking an oath?
A) Yes, because the obligation is established by the oath.
B) No, because bodily obligations do not allow separation of obligation and performance.
C) Yes, because the condition does not negate the ruling.
D) No, because fasting is not a type of expiation.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the absence of a condition does not negate the ruling; it only prevents the cause from being effective, and attributes are not always conditions—only the middle degree is.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
6 min
The Invalid Argument of Conjunction in Wording Necessitating Conjunction in Ruling
Refuting the claim that grammatical conjunction implies legal conjunction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and refute the claim that grammatical conjunction (al-qiran fi al-nazm) necessitates conjunction in legal ruling (al-qiran fi al-hukm), and apply the Hanafi principle that only need (iftiqar) warrants shared rulings.

Core Idea
Conjunction in wording does not imply conjunction in ruling; only linguistic deficiency creates shared rulings.

In this lesson, we examine a frequently misunderstood argument: that when two phrases are joined by 'and' (waw), they must share the same legal ruling. Some scholars used this to claim that since children are exempt from prayer, they must also be exempt from zakat, because the verse joins them. The Hanafi school refutes this by distinguishing complete from incomplete sentences and showing that shared rulings arise only from need, not from mere conjunction. We will walk through the reasoning using oath scenarios and the key verse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Invalid Face
The passage refutes the claim that grammatical conjunction forces legal conjunction. Locate this claim and the Quranic proof cited (Al-Baqarah 2:43).
2
Analyze the Oath Sections
The passage divides oaths into four categories. Focus on the fourth where an additional word ('today') changes the ruling. Understand why the Hanafi school treats it as an independent oath, not a response.
This illustrates that explicit wording overrides context.
3
Examine the Analogy to Incomplete Sentences
Opponents analogize to cases like 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind' where the second part needs the predicate. Understand why this analogy is flawed for complete sentences like the verse on prayer and zakat.
The flaw: complete sentences do not need sharing; only incomplete ones do.
4
Derive the Principle
The key Hanafi principle: sharing in ruling occurs only due to need (iftiqar), not mere conjunction. See how the author proves this using the conditional divorce example.
5
Apply the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Recognize the practical consequences: children may still owe zakat despite exemption from prayer, and oaths with added words are not restricted by context.
Contrast with the Shafi'i/Maliki/Zufar position.

Source Text

The invalid faces: Like the one invited to lunch who says: 'By Allah, I will not have lunch.' But if he added beyond the amount of the response and said: 'By Allah, I will not have lunch today' – and that is the place of disagreement. According to us, it becomes an independent oath to avoid nullifying the addition. The author clarified that 'conjunction in wording necessitates conjunction in ruling.' Among them is what some said: that conjunction in wording necessitates conjunction in ruling, like the claim of some among them regarding the verse: 'Establish prayer and give zakat' – that the conjunction necessitates that zakat is not obligatory on the child. They said: because the conjunction requires sharing, and they analogized to the incomplete sentence when it is conjoined to the complete, and this is invalid. So his statement 'I will not have lunch' is general, including lunch of that day and other days, lunch with the addressee and others, lunch in his home and elsewhere. But since he uttered the words as a response to him, it indicates that lunch to which he invited him, as if he said: 'If I have the lunch to which you invited me, then [I swear]...' So in these three cases, the general is restricted by its cause by agreement, and it never admits being an independent statement. As for when he added – and this is the fourth division – he becomes an independent oath where it does not relate to the first speech to avoid nullifying the addition: because if we made it as a response, then the addition would be nullified. And his saying 'today' becomes such that without it the response is complete, and the context indicates it is a response, but we do not consider context over explicit wording. So we preferred the wording and made it independent, so it is not restricted by its cause, and he breaks the oath by having lunch, whether on that day with the host or alone or with others. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Zufar said: It is interpreted as a response by context, so it is restricted by its cause, meaning that lunch specifically is that lunch. 'Indeed the conjunction' means the sharing between two phrases with the letter waw. ... on the child and the insane as prayer is not obligatory on them due to its conjunction with prayer in this statement, so they are two complete sentences conjoined with waw, requiring equality between them. The participation and the requirement of participation is that if one is obligatory, the other is obligatory; otherwise, the other is not obligatory. And they analogized to the incomplete sentence: i.e., these people analogized the complete sentence conjoined to a complete sentence, like his saying 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind is divorced', to the incomplete sentence, i.e., the single word conjoined to the complete sentence, like his saying 'Zaynab is divorced and Hind'. Just as the conjoined single word and the complete sentence share the predicate – namely 'divorced' inevitably – similarly, the first two share in the ruling. Because the sharing only applies to the incomplete sentence due to its need for what completes it. So if the conjoined speech is complete by itself, sharing is not required except in what it needs. For this reason, we said regarding a man's statement to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and my slave is free' – that the emancipation is conditional, because it is deficient in terms of suspension. It is 'entering the house' from the first division of what we mentioned from the divisions. The incomplete sentence: his saying 'and Hind'. What completes the speech is the predicate, for his saying 'and Hind' needed 'divorced', so for this need, sharing became established between them, unlike the complete conjoined sentence like the suspension. So it is established that the cause of sharing is need, not the conjunction itself. And for this, i.e., because sharing is established by need. Because his saying 'and my slave is free', although it is complete in terms of effect, is deficient in terms of suspension – because context indicates that his purpose from saying 'and my slave is free' is suspension, not immediate execution, and he did not mention a condition separately, so it became deficient in terms of suspension, so its need for the first in terms of the condition is established, unlike if he said: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and Zaynab is divorced' – he does not suspend Zaynab's divorce on entering the house; because if his purpose was suspension, he would have said 'and Zaynab' without mentioning the predicate, due to the unity of the two sentences in the predicate. So when he repeated the predicate, it is known that his purpose is immediate execution.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on an invalid face (wajh fasid) in usul al-fiqh: the argument that grammatical conjunction (al-qiran fi al-nazm) necessarily implies conjunction in legal ruling (al-qiran fi al-hukm). The author refutes this claim through detailed analysis of oath scenarios and the famous verse on prayer and zakat. He begins by discussing four divisions of oaths, particularly the fourth division where the respondent adds the word 'today' to an oath made in response to an invitation. The Hanafi position is that such an addition makes the oath an independent statement (ibtida'), not a response, so it is not restricted by the cause (the invitation). The context (dalaalat al-haal) is not sufficient to override the explicit wording. Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Zufar argue that the context indicates it is a response, thus it is restricted to that specific lunch. Moving to the conjunction argument, some claimed that the conjunction in the verse 'Establish prayer and give zakat' implies that zakat is not obligatory on children, just as prayer is not, because conjunction requires sharing in the ruling. The author labels this argument invalid (fasid). The refutation is that sharing in the ruling only occurs due to need (iftiqar) in incomplete sentences; complete sentences do not automatically share rulings. This is illustrated by the example of conditional divorce and emancipation: if a man says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced, and my slave is free', the emancipation becomes conditional because it is deficient in the context of suspension. But if he says 'and Zaynab is divorced', the divorce is immediate because he repeated the predicate, showing his intention. Thus, the Hanafi position is that conjunction alone does not force legal sharing; only need does.

Key Terms
القران في النظم (al-qiran fi al-nazm): Joining two linguistic units (words or sentences) with the letter waw (and).
الجملة الناقصة (al-jumla al-naqisa): An incomplete sentence that requires a predicate to be meaningful, like 'Zaynab and Hind' (missing 'divorced').
الافتقار (al-iftiqar): The need or dependency of one part of speech on another to be complete.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi usul, when does conjunction (al-qiran) between two sentences necessitate a shared ruling?
A) Whenever two sentences are joined by waw
B) Only when the second sentence is incomplete and needs the predicate of the first
C) Only when the ruling is explicitly mentioned in both
D) Only when the sentences are in the imperative mood

Key Takeaway
Grammatical conjunction does not force legal conjunction; only linguistic need (iftiqar) creates shared rulings.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Resolving Conflicting Hadith: Prayer in Disliked Times
Usul dispute: reconciling hadith via qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply qiyas to reconcile conflicting hadiths regarding prayer in disliked times and derive the Hanafi position on validity.

Core Idea
Qiyas resolves conflict between hadith: prohibition prevails for Fajr, rak'a hadith for Asr.

This passage addresses a key usul question: when two authentic hadiths seem to conflict, how does the Hanafi school reconcile them? The specific issue is whether an obligatory prayer can be validly performed in a time that is ordinarily prohibited (sunrise, sunset, zenith) if a person catches a rak'a within the valid time. The Hanafi school uses qiyas (analogical reasoning) to resolve the apparent contradiction, while al-Shafi'i relies on the apparent meaning of one hadith. Understanding this method is essential for mastering tarjih (preference) between textual evidences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting texts
The first text is the hadith: 'Whoever catches a rak'a of Fajr before sunrise has caught Fajr, and whoever catches a rak'a of Asr before sunset has caught Asr.' The second text is the authentic prohibition of prayer at sunrise, sunset, and zenith.
The conflict is apparent: one text seems to permit prayer at these times if a rak'a is caught, the other prohibits it absolutely.
2
State the Hanafi principle for resolving conflict
According to Hanafi usul, when two texts of equal authority conflict and cannot be harmonized otherwise, qiyas is applied to determine which text takes precedence in each specific case.
This is a rule of tarjih (preference) based on the strength of the underlying rationale.
3
Apply qiyas to Fajr
For Fajr, the prohibition hadith is preferred over the rak'a hadith because the underlying cause (illa) of the prohibition (sunrise being the end time of Fajr) is stronger. Thus, if the entire Fajr prayer falls after sunrise, it is invalid.
Sunrise is the terminal time for Fajr; the prayer cannot be validly performed after it.
4
Apply qiyas to Asr
For Asr, the rak'a hadith is preferred because the time for Asr extends until sunset, and the prohibition at sunset is specific to that moment. Catching a rak'a before sunset fulfills the obligation, and the prayer is valid even if it extends into the sunset time.
The prohibition at sunset applies to initiating prayer at that time, not to continuing a prayer started earlier.
5
Conclude the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
The dispute manifests in practice: Shafi'i holds that catching a rak'a within the valid time makes the prayer valid even if the entire prayer is in the disliked time. Hanafi holds that for Fajr, the prayer must be completed before sunrise; for Asr, the prayer is valid if started in valid time even if it extends into sunset.
The Hanafi position respects both hadiths by applying qiyas contextually.

العصر". البخاري: ٥٧٩ ، ومسلم: ١٣٧٤] ونحن نقول : وقع التعارض بين هذا الحديث وبين النهي الوارد عن الصلاة في وقت الطلوع والغروب وفي الاستواء، كما هو مروي في الصحاح فرجعنا إلى القياس، كما هو حكم التعارض، But if one begins Asr at its earliest time and prolongs it until sunset, it does not become invalid because the Lawgiver granted him the right to occupy the entire time with performance, so the defect connected to continuation is pardoned due to difficulty of avoidance. When time is free from performance, obligation attaches to the entire time due to removal of necessity that limited it to part, so it becomes obligatory in perfect form and cannot be fulfilled deficiently in the three disliked times, like other obligations. The obligation becomes invalid: according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, because the prayer was obligatory in perfect form due to perfect cause, so it cannot be performed deficiently; he must restart. Al-Shafi'i's proof is what the two Shaykhs narrated from Abu Huraira: 'Whoever catches a rak'a of Fajr before sunrise has caught Fajr, and whoever catches a rak'a of Asr before sunset has caught Asr.' (Bukhari 579, Muslim 1374). We say: There is conflict between this hadith and the prohibition of prayer at sunrise, sunset, and zenith, as narrated in authentic collections. So we resort to qiyas, as is the rule in conflict, and qiyas gives preference to this hadith for Asr and to the prohibition hadith for Fajr. There are other answers mentioned in lengthy works. 'Invalid' means deficient, because it is attributed to the devil. As in Asr at the time of redness, i.e., like the time of yellowness, it is performed that day in deficient form, i.e., with this commencement occurring in deficient time, so if corruption occurs by sunset, the prayer does not become invalid because it was performed as required. The objection raised is that you have established that what becomes obligatory perfectly cannot be performed deficiently, so if one begins Asr at its earliest time and prolongs it until sunset, his prayer should become invalid. The answer is that invalidity only occurs due to the necessity of determination, for the Lawgiver gave the obligated person the determination to occupy the entire time for prayer, so this amount of defect is pardoned for the sake of determination. 'Difficult': thus we said his prayer does not become invalid. 'And when the time is empty' means if the prayer is missed from the time. 'Due to removal of necessity' means obligation attaches to the whole time because the barrier to making the entire time a cause has been removed, namely the time being both a container and a cause; then it is not a container. 'In perfect form' due to perfect cause, which is the entire time. 'Like other obligations' means when qada becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi ruling on the validity of an obligatory prayer when performed deficiently due to time constraints. The author explains that if a person begins Asr prayer in its valid time (e.g., during the time of redness before sunset) and performs it deficiently (i.e., with shortcomings), the prayer is valid because it was performed as required given the circumstances. However, if the time expires after beginning the prayer correctly, the prayer does not become invalid because the Lawgiver has allowed the worshipper to occupy the entire time for prayer, and any defect that arises from continuing is pardoned. The passage then presents a dispute with al-Shafi'i, who cites a hadith that catching a rak'a before sunrise or sunset means one has caught the prayer. The Hanafi response resolves the apparent conflict between this hadith and the authentic prohibitions against praying at sunrise, sunset, and zenith. They apply qiyas (analogy) to prefer the hadith of prohibition for Fajr (since sunrise is the end time) and the hadith of 'one rak'a' for Asr (since sunset is the end time, but prayer at sunset is prohibited). The commentary further clarifies that 'invalid' means deficient, and that the prayer is valid if performed in the disliked time due to necessity, but if the prayer is missed entirely, qada (make-up) is required in perfect form and cannot be done in the disliked times.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): Obligatory religious duty, specifically the prayer that is wajib
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to resolve conflict between two hadiths by preferring one over the other

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The prayer becomes invalid because it entered the disliked time.
B) The prayer remains valid because it was started in valid time and the defect is pardoned.
C) The prayer is valid only if the person intended to finish before sunset.
D) The prayer must be repeated after sunset.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is used to resolve the conflict between the hadith of 'one rak'a' and the prohibition hadiths, leading to different rulings for Fajr and Asr prayer in disliked times.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Hajj as a Time-Bound Obligation with Problematic Expansion
Explanation of Hajj's time as problematic and the dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the views of Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the timing of the Hajj obligation, and explain the practical consequence of this ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
For Hajj, delay is permissible without sin until death — that is the relied-upon Hanafi position of Imam Muhammad.

This lesson explores a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about whether Hajj must be performed in the first year of obligation or can be delayed. The passage introduces the concept of 'time-bound obligation with problematic expansion' (mu'aqqat bi-waqt mushkil al-tawassu'), a distinct category in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to identify when a command's timing is ambiguous and how different mujtahids weigh precaution versus practical scope.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal nature
Hajj is fard al-umr, tied to both the months of Hajj and the entire lifetime. The time is problematic because it is unclear whether the time is a strict container (like for prayer) or a standard (like for fasting).
2
State the two positions
Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) holds it is permissible to delay performance beyond the first year as long as it is not missed by death. Imam Abu Yusuf holds it must be performed in the first year as a precaution.
Both are within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the basis of each view
Muhammad sees the time as expansive because a person's life can hold multiple Hajjs; delay is allowed. Abu Yusuf prioritizes precaution against the risk of missing the obligation.
The reasoning is aqli (rational).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The only practical difference is sin: according to Abu Yusuf, delaying past the first year without performing is sinful (fisq) and affects witness acceptance. According to Muhammad, there is no sin until death or its signs.
Both agree that a Hajj performed later is valid (ada').
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The author (Muntakhab al-Husami) considers Muhammad's view as raajih and mentions expansion only. The relied-upon position for fatwa is that delay is permissible without sin.
This is consistent with later Hanafi works (e.g., al-Hidayah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث في الأمر المقيد المؤقت بوقت مشکل توسعه والنوع الثالث المؤقت بوقت مشكل توسعه وهو الحج، فإنه فرض العمر، ووقته أشهر الحج وحياته مدة يفضل بعضها لحجة أخرى مشكل، ومن حكمه أن عند محمد الله يسعه التأخير لكن بشرط أن لايفوته في عمره، وعند أبي يوسف محله يتعين عليه الأداء في أشهر الحج من العام الأول احتياطاً احترازاً عن الفوات، فظهر ذلك في حق المأثم لا غير. ثم كلما أدّى يكون أداء عند الفريقين.
The discussion on the restricted command that is time-bound with problematic expansion: And the third type is the time-bound with problematic expansion, which is Hajj. For it is the obligation of a lifetime, and its time is the months of Hajj and his lifetime, a period in which some of it is surplus for another Hajj, problematic. And from its ruling is that according to Muhammad—may Allah have mercy on him—it is permissible to delay it, but on condition that he does not miss it in his lifetime. And according to Abu Yusuf—may Allah have mercy on him—it is obligatory upon him to perform it in the months of Hajj of the first year as a precaution against missing it. So that appears only in the matter of sin. Then whenever he performs it, it is a performance (ada') according to both parties.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage from Muntakhab al-Husami discusses the third type of time-bound obligation (mu'aqqat) which is 'problematic in its expansion' (mushkil al-tawassu'), exemplified by Hajj. The passage type is a madhhab dispute within the Hanafi school. The author explains that Hajj is obligatory once in a lifetime (fard al-umr), and its time is both the designated months (Shawwal, Dhu al-Qi'dah, first ten of Dhu al-Hijjah) and the entire lifetime of the person, creating an ambiguity: is the time a container (zarf) like for prayer, or a standard (mi'yar) like for fasting? This leads to a disagreement between Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and Imam Abu Yusuf. Muhammad holds that it is permissible to delay performing Hajj beyond the first year, as long as it is performed eventually before death, so no sin for delay. Abu Yusuf holds that it must be performed in the first year of obligation as a precaution, and delaying incurs sin (fisq) until performed. The practical difference is only in the matter of sin (ithm) and witness acceptance; both agree that performance in later years is valid (ada'). The author follows the preferred view of Muhammad, noting it as raajih.

Key Terms
مؤقت بوقت مشكل توسعه (mu'aqqat bi-waqt mushkil tawassu'ihi): A time-bound obligation where the time is problematic regarding expansion, i.e., it is unclear whether the time is a container or a standard
أشهر الحج (ashhur al-hajj): The months of Hajj: Shawwal, Dhu al-Qi'dah, and the first ten days of Dhu al-Hijjah
فرض العمر (fard al-umr): An obligation that is required once in a lifetime

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, when does a person become sinful for delaying Hajj?
A) Immediately after the first Hajj season passes
B) Only at the time of death or its signs if he has not performed it
C) After three years
D) He is never sinful for delaying

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, in its relied-upon view, permits delaying Hajj without sin until the time of death, as per Imam Muhammad's understanding of the time's expansion.
