Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Dispute on the Basis of Qada': Same Command or New Cause?
Explains the dispute over whether qada' is obligated by the same command as ada' or requires a new cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two positions on the basis of qada', apply the Hanafi position to deduce rulings, and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf between the views.

Core Idea
The same command that obligates ada' also obligates qada'; a new text is not needed.

This lesson explores a foundational usul dispute: does the obligation to make up a missed act (qada') arise from the same divine command that originally obligated the act (ada'), or does it require a new cause? Understanding this distinction is crucial for deriving rulings for obligatory acts and vows according to the Hanafi method.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine what exactly is being disputed: the cause of obligation for qada' (make-up) of missed obligatory acts.
The dispute is about the source of the obligation, not the obligation itself.
2
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The majority of Hanafis hold that the original command (e.g., 'Establish prayer') obligates both ada' and qada'. Cite the Quranic verses and explain that the obligation does not fall away with time.
The hadith about forgetfulness is seen as confirmation, not a new cause.
3
State the opposing view and its proof
Iraqi Hanafis, most Shafi'is, and Mu'tazila argue that qada' requires a new explicit text (like the hadith) or an indication like tafwit (causing loss).
They point to the i'tikaf vow case as evidence.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the vow objection
The author explains that the i'tikaf case does not require a new cause; the shift to a specified fast is due to the lost honor of Ramadan, not a new obligation.
The original command to fulfill vows still binds.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, both sides agree on the obligation of qada' in most cases; the difference lies in the method of derivation (takhrij).
For vows, both views still result in qada', but the reasoning differs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الله تعالى
بيان صفة حكم الأمر
الاختلاف في وجوب القضاء]
واختلف المشايخ في أن القضاء يجب بنص مقصود أم بالسبب الذي يوجب الأداء؟ قال عامتهم بأنه يجب بذلك السبب وهو الخطاب؛ لأن بقاء أصل الواجب للقدرة أي القضاء عنده قربة وسقوط فضل الوقت، لا إلى مثل وضمان للعجز أمر معقول على مثل من في المنصوص عليه وهو قضاء الصوم والصلاة. فاحترز بقوله: "من عنده" عن أداء ظهر اليوم قضاء عن ظهر أمسه؛ لأنه ليس من عنده بل هو حق الغير؛ لأنه فرض، والقضاء إنما يكون بصرف النفل الذي هو حق المكلّف إلى ما كان واجباً عليه. وقوله: "وهو حقه" تأكيد له وتوضيح له بأن المراد من قوله من عنده أن يكون حقه لا مجرد الحضرة. وقد يستعمل الأداء في موضع القضاء مجازاً، كما يقال: نويتُ أن أؤدّي ظهر الأمس أي أقضي؛ لأن أداء ظهر الأمس بعد مضيه محال، وكذا قد يستعمل القضاء في موضع الأداء مجازاً كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِذَا قَضَيْتُمْ مَنَاسِكَكُمْ﴾ (البقرة: 200) أي أديتم، وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِذَا قَضَيْتُمُ الصَّلاةَ﴾ (النساء:103) أي أديتم؛ لأن الجمعة لا قضاء لها، وإذا ثبت هذا فيجوز الأداء بنية القضاء وبالعكس. أم بالسبب الذي إلخ: وهو الأمر، فالمراد بالسبب هو الأمر لا السبب المعروف أعني الوقت؛ لأن الأداء إنما يضاف إلى الأمر لا إلى الوقت، فإن نفس الوجوب يضاف إلى الوقت فالحاصل أن القضاء يجب بالسبب الذي يجب به الأداء عند عامة المحققين من أكثر الحنفية وبعض أصحاب الشافعي وأحمد بن حنبل وعامة أهل الحديث، وإليه ذهب القاضي الإمام أبو زيد وشمس الأئمة وفخر الإسلام والمصنف، وهذا معنى قوله. وهو الخطاب أي: الأمر الذي يوجب الأداء، وعند العراقيين من مشايخنا وعامة أصحاب الشافعي وعامة المعتزلة: لا بد للقضاء من سبب جديد سوى سبب الأداء، فعند الفريق الأول النّص الموجب للأداء وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿أَقِيمُوا الصَّلاةَ﴾ (الأنعام: 72)، وقوله: ﴿كُتِبَ عَلَيْكُمُ الصِّيَامُ﴾ (البقرة: 183) دالّ بعينه على وجوب القضاء لا حاجة إلى نص جديد يوجب القضاء، مثل قوله عليه: "فإذا نسي أحدكم صلاة أو نام عنها فليصلها إذا ذكرها". رواه الترمذي [رقم: 177]، وزاد بعضهم: "فإن ذلك وقتها". وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ كَانَ مِنْكُمْ مَرِيضاً أَوْ عَلَى سَفَرٍ فَعِدَّةٌ مِنْ أَيَّامٍ أُخَرَ﴾ (البقرة: 184)، بل ورد هذين النصين للتنبيه على أن الأداء بأن في ذمتكم بالنصين السابقين لم يسقط بالفوات. أصل الواجب أي نفس الصلاة والصوم دون وصفه وهو فضل الوقت. الصوم والصلاة حيث ورد فيه النصان المذكوران سابقاً بقاء نفس الصلاة والصوم على ذمة المكلّف؛ لكونه قادراً على إتيان فضل الوقت، ولا مثل له ولا ضمان حتى يأتي بهما، بخلاف نفس الصلاة والصوم؛ لأنه على إتيان مثلهما قربة من عنده قادر، وكون بقاء نفس الصلاة والصوم وسقوط فضل الوقت أمراً معقولاً في المنصوص عليه يدلّ على أن الواجب لم يسقط بخروج الوقت، وأن هذا النص طلب تفريغ الذمة عن ذلك الواجب بالمثل ولهذا سمي قضاء، ولو وجب به ابتداءً لما صح تسميته قضاء حقيقة، فثبت في المنصوص عليه أن الموجب للقضاء هو ما يوجب الأداء كما عرفت آنفاً، وذلك أمر معقول كما عرفته أيضا. فيتعدّى ذلك الحكم وهو بقاء الواجب المقدّرة على المثل المنذورات المتعينة إلخ مما لم يرد فيه نص، فقلنا كما في المنصوص عليه بقاء نفس الواجب على الذمة بالنص الأول كذلك فيما لم يرد فيه نص جديد وهو المنذور من الصلاة والصيام والاعتكاف بقاء نفس الواجب بالنص الأول، وعند الفريق الثاني لا بد للقضاء من نص جديد، إما صريحا كما في الصلاة والصوم، وهو قوله : "فإذا نسي أحدكم " [الترمذي رقم: 177]، وقوله تعالى: ﴿فَمَنْ كَانَ مِنْكُمْ مَرِيضان﴾ (الآية)، أو دلالة وهو التفويت أو الفوات، ففيما لم يرد النص الصريح فيه للقضاء كالمنذور من الصلاة والصيام والاعتكاف تفويت المكلّف ذلك بمنزلة نص جديد لقضائه، وكذا فواته عند البعض فلا يظهر ثمرة الخلاف بين الفريقين إلا في التخريج، فعند الفريق الأول يجب القضاء في الكل بالنص السابق، وعند الفريق الثاني يجب القضاء بالنص الجديد أو بالتفويت. ولم يعتكف لعذر مانع من الاعتكاف لأنه لما انفصل إلخ: دفع سؤال يرد على الفريق الأول؛ وهو أنه إذا نذر أحد أن يعتكف شهر رمضان فصام ولم يعتكف لعذر منع من الاعتكاف لا يقضي لا يقضي اعتكافه في ضمن رمضان آخر، بل يقضي بصوم مقصود وهو صوم النفل، فعلم أن السبب للقضاء ليس السبب الذي أوجب الأداء بل سبب آخر؛ إذ لو كان السبب للقضاء هو السبب للأداء، وهو قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلْيُوفُوا نُذُورَهُمْ﴾ (الحج: 29)، لوجب أن يصح القضاء في ضمن رمضان الثاني، كما صح الأداء في ضمن رمضان الأول، كما هو مذهب أبي يوسف له، أو يسقط القضاء أصلا لعدم القدرة على شرطه الذي هو صيام رمضان الذي مضى. فعلم أن السبب للقضاء هو سبب آخر وهو التفويت وهو مطلق عن الوقت، فينصرف إلى الكامل وهو الصوم المقصود. تقرير الدفع: أن الاعتكاف لا يتحقق بغير الصيام، فإذا نذر بالاعتكاف فقد نذر بالصيام المقصودة، ولكن شرف رمضان الحاضر منع من الصيام المقصودة؛ لأن العبادة في رمضان أفضل من العبادة في غيره، فلأجل هذا الشرف انتقلنا من الصوم الأصلي المقصود، وهو النقل إلى صوم رمضان، فلما فات شرف رمضان وانفصل الاعتكاف عن صوم الوقت، فيتعدّى إلى المنذورات المتعينة من الصلاة والصيام والاعتكاف وفيما إذا نذر أن يعتكف شهر رمضان فصام ولم يعتكف إنما وجب القضاء بصوم مقصود؛ لأنه لما انفصل الاعتكاف عن صوم الوقت عاد شرطه إلى الكمال الأصلي لا لأن القضاء وجب بسبب آخر.
Book of Allah Almighty. Statement of the attribute of the ruling of command. The disagreement about the obligation of qada'. The mashayikh differed: is qada' obligated by an intended text (nas qasd) or by the cause that obligates ada'? The majority of them said: it is obligated by that cause, which is the address (khitab); because the survival of the original obligation for capability — i.e., qada' is a near act of piety from him and the falling away of the virtue of time, not to a like and guarantee for inability is a rational matter upon a like from that which is textually stipulated, namely qada' of fast and prayer. He guarded by his saying "from him" against performing the zuhr of today as a qada' for the zuhr of yesterday; because it is not from him rather it is a right of another because it is fard, and qada' is only by expending nafil which is the right of the obligated person towards what was obligatory upon him. His saying "and it is his right" is an emphasis and clarification that the intended meaning of "from him" is that it is his right, not mere presence. And ada' is sometimes used in the place of qada' metaphorically, as it is said: I intended to perform the zuhr of yesterday, i.e., to make it up; because performing the zuhr of yesterday after its passing is impossible, and likewise qada' is sometimes used in the place of ada' metaphorically as in the Almighty's saying: {When you have completed your rites} (al-Baqarah: 200), i.e., performed, and His saying: {When you have completed the prayer} (al-Nisa': 103), i.e., performed; because Friday has no qada'. And if this is established, then performance with the intention of qada' is permissible, and vice versa. "Or by the cause which" etc.: it is the command; so by cause is meant the command, not the well-known cause, namely time; because ada' is attributed to the command not to time, for the obligation itself is attributed to time. The net result: qada' is obligated by the cause by which ada' is obligated according to the majority of the verifiers among most Hanafis, some of the followers of al-Shafi'i, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and most of the People of Hadith; and this was the view of al-Qadi al-Imam Abu Zayd, Shams al-A'imma, Fakhr al-Islam, and the author. This is the meaning of his statement. It is the address, i.e., the command that obligates ada'. According to the Iraqis among our mashayikh, most followers of al-Shafi'i, and most Mu'tazila: qada' necessarily requires a new cause besides the cause of ada'. According to the first group, the text that obligates ada' — namely His saying: {Establish prayer} (al-An'am: 72), and His saying: {Fasting is prescribed for you} (al-Baqarah: 183) — itself indicates the obligation of qada', with no need for a new text obligating qada', such as his saying: "If any of you forgets a prayer or sleeps through it, let him pray it when he remembers it." Reported by al-Tirmidhi [no. 177], and some added: "For that is its time." And the Almighty's saying: {And whoever among you is ill or on a journey, then a number of other days} (al-Baqarah: 184). Rather, these two texts were revealed to indicate that the obligation on your responsibility by the two previous texts did not fall away by being missed. The original obligation, i.e., the prayer and fast themselves without their description, which is the virtue of time. The survival of the prayer and fast themselves on the responsibility of the obligated person, because he is capable of performing the virtue of time, and there is no like for it nor guarantee until he performs them, unlike the prayer and fast themselves; because performing their like is a near act of piety from one who is capable. And the survival of the prayer and fast themselves and the falling away of the virtue of time is a rational matter in the textually stipulated case, indicating that the obligation did not fall away by the exit of time, and that this text demands the discharge of the responsibility from that obligation by a like, and for this it is called qada'. Had it been obligated from the beginning, it would not be correctly called qada' in reality. So it is established in the textually stipulated case that the obligator of qada' is what obligates ada', as you have learned earlier, and that is a rational matter as you have also learned. This ruling — i.e., the survival of the obligation with capability for a like — extends to the specified vowed acts, etc., concerning which no text has come. So we say: just as in the textually stipulated case there is survival of the obligation itself on the responsibility by the first text, similarly where no new text has come — namely the vowed prayer, fast, and i'tikaf — the obligation itself survives by the first text. According to the second group, a new text is necessary for qada', either explicit as in prayer and fast — i.e., his saying: "If any of you forgets..." [al-Tirmidhi no. 177], and the Almighty's saying: {And whoever among you is ill} (the verse) — or by indication, i.e., causing the loss (tafwit) or the missing (fawat). So in what has no explicit text for qada', like the vowed prayer, fast, and i'tikaf, the obligated person's causing the loss is like a new text for its qada', and similarly its missing according to some. So the fruit of the disagreement between the two groups does not appear except in the derivation (takhrij). According to the first group, qada' is obligatory in all by the previous text; according to the second group, qada' is obligatory by the new text or by causing the loss. "And he did not perform i'tikaf due to a preventing excuse" etc.: this is a rebuttal of a question raised against the first group: namely, if someone vows to observe i'tikaf in the month of Ramadan, then he fasts but does not observe i'tikaf due to an excuse preventing i'tikaf, he does not make up his i'tikaf within another Ramadan, but rather makes it up with a specified fast — that is, supererogatory fasting. So it is known that the cause for qada' is not the cause that obligated ada', but another cause; because if the cause for qada' were the cause for ada' — namely the Almighty's saying: {And let them fulfill their vows} (al-Hajj: 29) — it would be necessary that qada' would be valid within a second Ramadan, just as ada' was valid within the first Ramadan, as is the madhhab of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, or qada' would fall away entirely due to inability to fulfill its condition, which is the fasting of the past Ramadan. So it is known that the cause for qada' is another cause, i.e., causing the loss, and it is unrestricted with respect to time, so it turns to the complete, i.e., the specified fast. The explanation of the rebuttal: i'tikaf is not realized without fasting. So if one vows i'tikaf, he has vowed specified fasting, but the honor of the present Ramadan prevented specified fasting, because worship in Ramadan is better than worship in other times. For this honor we transferred from the original specified fast to the fast of Ramadan. So when the honor of Ramadan passed and i'tikaf became separate from the time's fast, it extends to the specified vowed acts of prayer, fasting, and i'tikaf; and in the case where one vows to observe i'tikaf in the month of Ramadan then fasts but does not observe i'tikaf, qada' is only obligated by a specified fast because when i'tikaf became separate from the time's fast, its condition returned to its original perfection, not because qada' is obligated due to another cause.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute about the basis of qada' (make-up) in Hanafi usul. The key question: does the same command that obligates ada' (performance) also obligate qada', or does qada' require a new cause? The author presents two positions. The majority of Hanafis, along with some Shafi'is, Ahmad ibn Hanbal, and the People of Hadith, hold that the original command (e.g., 'Establish prayer') itself obligates both ada' and qada'. This is because the obligation for prayer does not fall away with the passage of time; rather, the command requires fulfillment even after the time, and qada' is merely performing the same obligation later. The second view, held by Iraqi Hanafis, most Shafi'is, and Mu'tazila, is that qada' needs a new text or cause, such as the hadith 'If one forgets...' or causing the loss. The passage then discusses the application to vows (nadhr): if someone vows to do i'tikaf in Ramadan but misses it, he makes up with a specified fast, not within another Ramadan. The author defends the first view by explaining that the cause for qada' in such cases is the loss (tafwit) itself, which functions like a new text. The dispute is based on whether the original command remains effective after the time or whether a new address is required. The passage is from a sharh on a text about the attributes of the command.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada'): The performance of an obligatory act after its prescribed time has passed, as a make-up for the missed original.
الأداء (al-ada'): The performance of an obligatory act within its prescribed time.
الخطاب (al-khitab): The divine address (command) that obligates the act.
تفويت (tafwit): The act of causing the loss of an opportunity to perform an act within its time, which in some views serves as a cause for qada'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what is the cause that obligates qada'?
A) A new text specifically mentioning qada'
B) The same command that obligates ada'
C) The passing of time
D) The intention of the worshipper

Key Takeaway
The same divine command that obligates an act within its time also obligates its make-up when missed; the obligation persists.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
15 min
Three Divisions of the Prohibited in Hanafi Usul
Explains three categories of prohibited acts and their rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three categories of prohibited acts in Hanafi usul and explain the practical differences from the Shafi'i school regarding validity.

Core Idea
Not every prohibition invalidates the act; the ruling depends on the type of ugliness (qubh).

The prohibition (nahy) does not always render an act invalid. Hanafi usul divides prohibited acts into three categories based on the nature of their ugliness: inherent (li 'aynihi), incidental-separable (li ghayrihi qabil al-infikak), and incidental-inseparable (li ghayrihi ghayr qabil al-infikak). Each category carries a distinct legal effect: batil, sahih/makruh, or fasid. This classification has direct practical consequences for acts like sale during the Friday call, fasting on Eid, and intercourse during menstruation. The Shafi'i school, however, holds that absolute prohibition always implies inherent ugliness and thus invalidates the act, leading to different rulings in many cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the passage, extract the three divisions of al-munhi 'anhu: (1) qabih li 'aynihi (ugly in essence), (2) qabih li ghayrihi with a separable quality, (3) qabih li ghayrihi with an inseparable quality.
Examples given: zina (1), sale during call (2), fasting on Eid (3).
2
Determine the legal effect for each category
For each category, note the ruling: (1) batil, (2) sahih (valid) or makruh, (3) fasid. Understand why: inseparable ugliness makes the act defective but not void.
The principle: al-hukm yathbutu biqadri dalilihi.
3
Apply the categories to the examples
Work through the examples: sale at call time (valid), fasting on Eid (fasid), intercourse during menstruation (sahih but makruh). Check how each example fits the category.
The passage emphasizes that acts like sale during call remain valid and transfer ownership.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
State the Shafi'i view: absolute prohibition always implies qabih li 'aynihi, so all prohibited acts are batil unless evidence shows otherwise. Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical differences).
E.g., Shafi'i considers sale during call batil, fasting on Eid batil.
5
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
Understand the Hanafi response: the Shafi'i argument conflates two types of ugliness; the prohibition of sensory acts defaults to qabih li 'aynihi, but for legislative acts, it defaults to qabih li ghayrihi with inseparable ugliness (fasid).
The key is the nature of the act (hissi vs shar'i) and the quality of the ugliness.

Chapter on the prohibited: Its ruling is that it becomes valid (sahih) and legitimate (mashru') after the prohibition. Hence we said: intercourse during menstruation renders her permissible for the first husband, and establishes ihsan for the one who commits it. And what is connected to it as a description (wasf) like invalid sale (al-bay' al-fasid) and fasting on the day of Nahr. And similarly, intercourse during menstruation is legitimate in that she is a wife, but it is ugly due to harm, which is an incidental matter that can be separated from intercourse—harm may exist without intercourse and intercourse without harm. Its ruling (i.e., the ruling of this division) is that it is valid etc., without dispute; even a sale during the call to prayer is valid and entails ownership without delay in taking possession, and the obligatory prayer is fulfilled in usurped land, and intercourse during menstruation causes ihsan like intercourse in purity. Therefore, if a man divorces his wife three times, she is not lawful for him until she marries another husband and he enters her; if he enters her during menstruation, she becomes lawful for the first husband by his marriage to her. Similarly, ihsan is established; one condition of ihsan is entering a woman legitimately; if he enters her during menstruation, he becomes muhsan, so if he later commits zina, his punishment is stoning. And [the third] is that the other [quality] for which the prohibited becomes ugly is an inseparable description (wasf lazim) that cannot be separated, like invalid sale and fasting on the day of Nahr. The invalid sale (e.g., selling wine) is not ugly in itself because the essential elements of sale exist, but it is connected to what necessitates its ugliness as an inseparable description, i.e., wine not being valuable property, and the price in sale is like an inseparable description because sale cannot be separated from the price; thus it becomes fasid, not batil. Similarly, fasting on the day of Nahr is not ugly in itself, but it becomes ugly due to the time, which is an inseparable description. The ruling of this division is that the prohibited becomes fasid due to the connection of ugliness, unlike the previous division where it is makruh. After completing the division, the author intends to clarify that the word 'ayy' (any) applies to the first division (ugly in itself), and 'ayy' applies to the second division (ugly for other). Prohibition of sensory acts (af'al hissiyya) falls on the first division, and prohibition of legislative acts (af'al shar'iyya) falls on the last division. Al-Shafi'i said: In both categories, the absolute prohibition is directed to the first division unless evidence indicates otherwise, because prohibition necessitates ugliness (qubh) in reality just as command necessitates goodness (husn). So the absolute [prohibition] is directed to the first division. He argues that the prohibition on sensory and legislative acts is for ugliness in itself unless evidence shows otherwise, thus the prohibition of zina, wine, and fasting on Nahr are all batil and do not yield reward or ownership.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul work explains the classification of prohibited acts (al-munhi 'anhu) into three divisions and their legal effects. The first division is the prohibition of an act that is ugly in itself (qabih li 'aynihi), such as zina, murder, and drinking wine. Such prohibitions make the act invalid (batil) and sinful. The second division is the prohibition of an act that is ugly due to an incidental, separable quality (qabih li ghayrihi with a quality that can be separated). Examples include intercourse during menstruation, which is prohibited due to harm, but the act itself (intercourse) is legitimate; or selling during the Friday call to prayer, which is prohibited but the sale remains valid (sahih). The third division is the prohibition of an act that is ugly due to an inseparable quality (qabih li ghayrihi with a lazim wasf), such as an invalid sale (e.g., selling wine) because the defect (wine not being property) is inseparable from the act, or fasting on the day of Eid because the time is integral to the definition of fasting. In this third division, the act is fasid (defective) but not batil in the sense that it lacks all effect; for example, an invalid sale still transfers ownership after possession (in the Hanafi view). The passage contrasts the Hanafi view with that of Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that any prohibition (whether on sensory or legislative acts) renders the act invalid (batil) unless evidence shows it is prohibited for an external reason. The Hanafis argue that the nature of the act and the type of prohibition determine the ruling, following the principle that rulings are established according to their evidence.

Key Terms
القبيح لعينه (al-qabih li 'aynihi): An act that is ugly and prohibited due to its own essence, e.g., zina, murder.
القبيح لغيره (al-qabih li ghayrihi): An act that is ugly due to an external quality, not its essence. It may be separable (e.g., harm in intercourse) or inseparable (e.g., time in fasting on Eid).
الأفعال الحسية (al-af'al al-hissiyya): Sensory acts that are known by sense and do not depend on revelation for their reality, e.g., killing, drinking wine.
الأفعال الشرعية (al-af'al al-shar'iyya): Legislative acts that depend on revelation for their reality and definition, e.g., prayer, fasting, sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, which of the following is an example of a prohibited act that is considered sahih (valid) after the prohibition?
A) Drinking wine.
B) Fasting on the day of Eid al-Adha.
C) Selling during the Friday call to prayer.
D) Committing zina.

Key Takeaway
The effect of prohibition on an act depends on the type of ugliness: inherent (batil), incidental separable (sahih/makruh), or incidental inseparable (fasid).

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
12 min
The Effect of Prohibition on Worship: When Does Nahy Render an Act Fasid vs Naqis?
Explains the impact of nahy on two distinct worship acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle that a prohibition (nahy) attaching to the description (wasf) renders an act fasid only when the act's essence depends on the time, and naqis when time is merely a container.

Core Idea
Prohibition on the description, not the essence: fasting on Eid is fasid, prayer at sunrise is naqis—because fasting subsists by time, prayer does not.

This passage from a classic Hanafi usul text tackles a critical question: does every prohibition (nahy) in worship render the act invalid? The answer is no—it depends on whether the prohibition attaches to the act's essence (asl) or its description (wasf), and crucially, on the relationship between the act and the prohibited time. By examining two cases—a vow to fast on the day of Nahr and prayer at sunrise or sunset—the author extracts a powerful usul principle that governs how prohibitions interact with different acts of worship. We will walk through the reasoning step by step, then see how the same principle applies to other scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases and their apparent contradiction
The passage juxtaposes fasting on the day of Nahr (invalid/fasid) and prayer at prohibited times (deficient/naqis). Why different outcomes?
Look for the different rulings despite both being prohibited.
2
Locate the Hanafi position on the vow
The Hanafi school holds the vow valid because the prohibition is on the description (being Eid), not the essence of fasting. The vower only uttered the word 'fast,' not an act of disobedience.
Understand the distinction between essence and description.
3
Analyze the objection and response
Al-Shafi'i and Zufar argue the vow is invalid because the act itself is sin. The Hanafi response: the sin is in the performance, not the promise. Hence vow valid but must break and make up.
Focus on the Hanafi rebuttal using the wasf/asl distinction.
4
Contrast the relationship of time to prayer vs fasting
For prayer, time is merely a container (zarf) and cause (sabab), not a defining element. Thus prohibition renders it deficient (naqis). For fasting, time is its measure (mi'yar) and enters into its essence; thus prohibition renders it invalid (fasid).
The key usul insight: time is intrinsic to fasting but extrinsic to prayer.
5
Extract the usul principle
When nahy attaches to a description that is inseparable from the act's essence (like time in fasting), the act becomes fasid. When the description is external (like time as mere container), the act becomes naqis.
This principle can be applied to other acts where a prohibition targets a description.

فصل في النهي
والنهي يتعلّق بوصفه، وهو أنه يوم عيد، فصار فاسداً، ولهذا يصح النذر به عندنا؛ أي بوصف الوقت صوم يوم النحر لا بإطلاق لأنه نذر بالطاعة، وإنما وصف المعصية متصل بذاته فعلاً لا باسمه ذكراً. أي هذا النذر حكم وقت طلوع الشمس و وقت طلوع الشمس ودلوكها صحيح بأصله، فاسد بوصفه، وهو أنه أي الوقت منسوب إلى الشيطان كما جاءت به السنة، يوم عيد: أي يوم ضيافة الله والمتصل بالوقت مثل المتصل بالصوم؛ لأنه يقوم به. ولهذا: أي لكونه مشروعاً باعتبار أصله عندنا استحساناً خلافا لزفر والشافعي له، وهو رواية ابن المبارك عن أبي حنيفة له. نذر بالطاعة من حيث ذات الصوم. وإنما وصف المعصية إلخ: يعنى إنما صح النذر به؛ لأن وصف المعصية وهو الإعراض عن الضيافة إنما يوجد بفعل الصوم حتى لو شرع فيه يصير عاصيا، وهو لم يوجد من الناذر، وإنما وجد ذكر اسم الصوم منه إذا قال: الله عليّ أن أصوم يوم النحر، أو أصوم غداً ، والغد يوم النحر، والذكر ليس بمعصية؛ لأنه ليس بإعراض، فلا يمنع صحة النذر حتى لو صام في ذلك اليوم لخرج عن العهدة؛ لأنه أداء كما التزمه، ولكن عليه أن يفطر في هذا اليوم ويقضي في يوم آخر ليتخلّص عن المعصية، كما هو في ظاهر الرواية. وهذا جواب عن قول الشافعي وزفر وهو أن الصوم في هذه الأيام معصية، فلا يصح النذر، فقال: إن المعصية إنما توجد بالصوم لا بذكره والناذر لم يوجد منه إلا الذكر، فكيف لا يصح. طلوع الشمس ودلوكها أي زوالها أو غروبها، يقال: دلكت الشمس إذا زالت أو غابت. بأصله: لأنه يساوي سائر الأوقات في كونها ظرفاً صالحا للعبادة. كما جاءت به السنة: لأن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم نهى الصلاة عند طلوع الشمس ودلوكها. قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: "لا يتحرى أحدكم فيصلّي عند طلوع الشمس ولا عند غروبها". [البخاري رقم: ٥٨٥] وفي رواية قال: "إذا طلع حاجب الشمس فدعوا الصلاة حتى يبرز، وإذا غاب حاجب الشمس فدعوا الصلاة حتى تغيب ولا تحينوا بصلاتكم طلوع الشمس ولا غروبها، فإنها تطلع بين قرني الشيطان. متفق عليه البخاري، رقم: ۳۲۷۳، ۳۲۷۲] وعن عبد الله الصنابحي قال: قال رسول الله : "إن الشمس تطلع ومعها قرن الشيطان فإذا ارتفعت فارقها، ثم إذا استوت قارنها، فإذا زالت فارقها، فإذا دنت للغروب قارنها، فإذا غربت فارقها، ونهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عن الصلاة في تلك الساعات." رواه مالك وأحمد والنسائي [٥٦٠] وقرنا الشيطان ناحيتا رأسه والكلام يمكن أن يحمل على الحقيقة بأنه يقابل الشمس وقت طلوعها، فيثبت حتى يكون = صحیح عن
فصل في النهي
كتاب الله تعالى إلا أن أصل الصلاة لا يوجد بالوقت لأنه ظرفها لا معيارها، وهو سببها، فصارت الصلاة فيه ناقصةً لا فاسدة. فقيل: لا يتأدّى بها الكامل، ويضمن بالشروع، والصوم يقوم بالوقت ويعرف به، فازداد الأثر فصار فاسداً، فلم يضمن بالشروع، الصوم في يوم النحر طلوعها بين قرنيه فينقلب سجود الكفار للشمس عبادة له، ويمكن أن يحمل على التمثيل لتسلّطه على عبدة الشمس وتحريكه إياهم على عبادتها في هذا الوقت، ولما كان يرد أنه لما ثبت أن هذه الأوقات في حق الصلاة كيوم النحر في حق الصوم وجب أن يفسد الصلاة في هذه الأوقات كالصوم والحال أن الصلاة في هذه الأوقات ناقصة وليست بفاسدة دفعه بالفرق بينهما بقوله: إلا أن أصل إلخ.
إلا أن أصل إلخ: يعني بين الصوم في يوم النحر والصلاة في الأوقات المكروهة فرق، وهو أن الصلاة لا توجد بالوقت؛ لأنه ظرفها، والظرف لا تأثير له في إيجاد المظروف بل الصلاة توجد بأفعال معلومة، والوقت مجاور لها، فلا يكون فساد الوقت مؤثرا في فسادها بخلاف الصوم فإنه يوجد بالوقت؛ لأنه معيار له على ما مر، فيكون متصلا بالصوم، فيؤثر فساده في الصوم. وقوله: "هو" أي الوقت سببها أي الصلاة إشارة إلى دفع مقدر يرد أن فساد الظرف لما لم يؤثر في فساد المظروف كما قلتم ينبغي أن لا يؤثر فساد الوقت في نقصان المظروف أيضاً حتى يتأدّى الصلاة في هذا الوقت كاملا، والحال أنكم تقولون : إنها تؤدى ناقصاً. تقرير الجواب: أن الوقت وإن كان ظرفاً للصلاة لكنه سبب لها، ففساد السبب يؤثر في فساد المسبب ضرورة، إلا أنه لما كان مجاوراً و لم يكن وصفاً يؤثر في النقصان لا في الفساد، فإذا ثبت أن الوقت سبب، ونقصانه يؤثر في نقصان المسبب وهو الصلاة فقيل: لا يتأدى بها إلخ.
عليه، وهو لا يتأدّى بها أي في هذه الأوقات المكروهة الواجب الكامل وهو الصلاة الكامل بالشروع حتى لو شرع النفل في هذه الأوقات ثم قطعه وجب عليه القضاء، وينبغي أن يقضي في وقت كامل، فإن قضى في هذه الأوقات أجزأه وقد أساء والتفريع الأول باعتبار تحقق النقصان، والثاني باعتبار الصيانة عن النقصان، وهذا بخلاف الصوم؛ لأن الصوم يقوم بالوقت ويعرف تخفيفا وتشديدا به أي بالوقت، فيقال: هو الإمساك المفطرات الثلاث نهاراً مع النية، حتى يعرف مقدار الصوم بالوقت فيزيد بازدياده وينقص بانتقاصه، عن ويكون الوقت داخلا في ماهية الصوم. فازداد الأثر أي ازداد أثر فساد الوقت في الصوم لشدة الاتصال بينهما. فلم يضمن بالشروع في يوم النحر، ولما كان يرد على الأصل المذكور (وهو أن النهي عن يعرف هذا بناء على تخفيف يعرف.
Chapter on Prohibition. And the prohibition is attached to its description, and it is that it is the day of Eid, so it became invalid (fasid), and therefore the vow (nidhr) of fasting on the day of Nahr is valid according to us; i.e., by the description of time, not absolutely, because it is a vow of obedience, and the description of disobedience is connected to its essence as an act, not by its name as a mention. This means the ruling of the time of sunrise and its setting: it is valid in its essence, invalid in its description, and it is that the time is attributed to Satan as reported in the Sunnah. The day of Eid is the day of God's hospitality, and what is connected to time is like what is connected to fasting because it stands by it. And therefore, i.e., because it is legislated in terms of its essence according to us as istihsan, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, and it is a transmission of Ibn al-Mubarak from Abu Hanifa. A vow of obedience from the perspective of the essence of fasting. And 'the description of disobedience etc.' means that the vow is valid only because the description of disobedience—which is turning away from hospitality—only occurs by the act of fasting, so if he begins it he becomes disobedient, and that has not occurred from the vower; only the mention of the name of fasting occurred from him when he said: 'By Allah, I will fast on the day of Nahr,' or 'I will fast tomorrow,' and tomorrow is the day of Nahr, and the mention is not disobedience because it is not turning away, so it does not prevent the validity of the vow, even if he fasts on that day he would discharge his obligation because it is the performance of what he committed himself to, but he must break his fast on this day and make it up on another day to escape disobedience, as in the apparent narration. And this is a response to the statement of al-Shafi'i and Zufar that fasting on these days is disobedience, so the vow is not valid. He said: disobedience only exists by fasting, not by its mention, and the vower only made a mention, so how can it not be valid? Sunrise and its setting: i.e., its zenith or its setting; it is said: the sun set when it declines or sets. 'In its essence': because it equals other times in being a container suitable for worship. 'As reported in the Sunnah': because the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade prayer at sunrise and its setting. The Prophet said: 'Let none of you deliberately pray at sunrise nor at sunset.' [Bukhari no. 585] And in another narration he said: 'When the disk of the sun rises, leave prayer until it has fully risen, and when the disk of the sun sets, leave prayer until it has set, and do not time your prayers at sunrise or sunset, for it rises between the two horns of Satan.' Agreed upon by Bukhari, no. 3273, 3272. And from Abd Allah al-Sunabihi: The Messenger of Allah said: 'The sun rises and with it is a horn of Satan; when it rises high he separates from it, then when it is at its zenith he accompanies it, then when it declines he separates from it, then when it approaches setting he accompanies it, then when it sets he separates from it. And the Messenger of Allah forbade prayer at those times.' Narrated by Malik, Ahmad, and al-Nasa'i [560]. And 'the two horns of Satan' are the two sides of his head; the statement can be interpreted literally, that he confronts the sun at the time of its rising, so it is established until = correct from... Chapter on Prohibition. The Book of Allah the Exalted, except that the essence of prayer does not exist by time because time is its container, not its measure, and it is its cause, so the prayer in it becomes deficient (naqis), not invalid (fasid). It is said: the complete (obligation) is not performed by it, and one is responsible by starting, whereas fasting subsists by time and is known by it, so the effect increased and it became invalid, so one is not responsible by starting (i.e., no guarantee). Fasting on the day of Nahr: its rising between his two horns, so the prostration of disbelievers to the sun becomes worship of him, and it can be interpreted metaphorically for his dominance over sun-worshippers and his incitement of them to worship it at this time. And since it might be objected that since it is established that these times with respect to prayer are like the day of Nahr with respect to fasting, it would be necessary that prayer becomes invalid in these times like fasting, but the case is that prayer in these times is deficient and not invalid, he rebuts it by the difference between them through his statement: 'except that the essence etc.' 'Except that the essence etc.' means: there is a difference between fasting on the day of Nahr and prayer in the disliked times, namely that prayer does not exist by time because time is its container, and the container has no effect in bringing about the contained; rather prayer exists by known actions, and time is adjacent to it, so the invalidity of the time does not affect its invalidity. Unlike fasting, for it exists by time because it is its measure as previously mentioned, so it is connected to fasting, so its invalidity affects fasting. And His statement: 'it' i.e., time is its cause, i.e., of prayer, indicates a rebuttal of an implicit objection that if the invalidity of the container does not affect the invalidity of the contained as you say, then the invalidity of the time should also not affect the deficiency of the contained, so that prayer would be performed completely at this time, whereas you say it is performed deficiently. The articulation of the answer: that time, although it is a container for prayer, is also its cause, so the invalidity of the cause necessarily affects the invalidity of the effect, except that since it is adjacent and not a description, it affects deficiency, not invalidity. So when it is established that time is a cause, its deficiency affects the deficiency of the effect, which is prayer, so it is said: the complete is not performed by it etc. Upon it, and it is: the complete obligation is not performed in these disliked times, meaning the complete prayer by initiation, such that if one begins a supererogatory prayer in these times and then cuts it off, he must make it up, and he should make it up in a complete time; if he makes it up in these times, it suffices him but he does wrong. The first furu' (branch) is based on the verification of deficiency, and the second is based on safeguarding against deficiency. This is unlike fasting, because fasting subsists by time and is known—with lightening and intensifying—by it, i.e., by time. So it is said: it is refraining from the three nullifiers during the day with intention, such that the extent of fasting is known by time, increasing with its increase and decreasing with its decrease. Thus time enters into the essence of fasting. So the effect increased, meaning the effect of the invalidity of time on fasting increased due to the strong connection between them. Hence, one is not responsible by starting (i.e., no guarantee) on the day of Nahr. And since it might be objected to the aforementioned principle (that the prohibition is about...) this is known based on a lightening of 'apparent' or an intensification of 'known'.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal discussion on the effect of prohibition (nahy) when it attaches to the description (wasf) of an act rather than its essence (asl). The author opens by stating that the prohibition on fasting on the day of Nahr (10th Dhul Hijjah) relates to its description (it being an 'Eid day), not its essence (the act of fasting itself). Hence, the vow to fast on that day is valid according to the Hanafi school, contrary to al-Shafi'i and Zufar who deemed it invalid because the act itself is a disobedience. The Hanafi response is that the disobedience lies in the actual fasting on that day, not in the mere verbal vow; thus the vow is valid, but the person must break the fast and make it up later. The author then compares this to prayer at prohibited times (sunrise, sunset). These times are valid in essence (asl) for prayer (since time is generally a suitable container), but invalid in description because they are attributed to Satan. However, prayer at these times is not invalid (fasid) but merely deficient (naqis). The reason, as explained, is that prayer does not depend on time for its existence—time is merely its container (zarf) and cause (sabab). The deficiency of the cause (time) only renders the prayer deficient, not invalid. Fasting, by contrast, is defined by time; time is its measure (mi'yar) and part of its essence. Therefore, the invalidity of the time directly makes the fasting invalid. This difference explains why the prohibition leads to fasid in fasting but only naqis in prayer. The passage also includes a rational defense against an objection: if time is a cause, why doesn't its invalidity cause total invalidity? The answer is that because time is merely adjacent (mujawir), not an intrinsic description, its effect is limited to deficiency. The practical consequence: for prayer, if one begins a supererogatory prayer at a prohibited time and cuts it, it must be made up (since the prayer is deficient, not void). For fasting on the day of Nahr, no make-up is required for starting it? Actually, the text says the person must break and make up, but in the comparison it says 'there is no guarantee by starting' meaning if a person starts fasting on the day of Nahr, he is not liable for any guarantee (damān) because the act is invalid from the start. This aligns with the principle that a fasid act does not entail guarantee for supererogatory acts. The commentary here is dense, covering the core Hanafi principle that the effect of prohibition depends on the nature of the act and its relationship to time.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A prohibition that attaches to the description (wasf) of an act, not its essence (asl).
الوصف (al-wasf): The description or attribute that makes the act prohibited, such as being a day of Eid or a time associated with Satan.
الأصل (al-asl): The essence or core nature of the act, which is valid (sahih) in itself.
فاسد (fasid): Invalid; an act that is not legally sound due to a defect in its description, requiring neither completion nor guarantee.
ناقص (naqis): Deficient; a worship act that is legally valid but incomplete in reward or perfection due to a temporal prohibition.
النذر (al-nidhr): A vow to perform an act of worship or charity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is fasting on the day of Nahr considered fasid, while prayer at sunrise is only naqis?
A) Because the prohibition on fasting is stronger than on prayer.
B) Because time is part of the essence of fasting but only a container for prayer.
C) Because prayer can be made up later, but fasting cannot.
D) Because the hadith about prayer is weak, while the one about fasting is strong.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul principle: a prohibition on the description (wasf) renders an act fasid only when the description is intrinsic to the act's essence (e.g., time in fasting); otherwise, the act becomes naqis (deficient) but remains valid.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Legal Effect of Prohibition in Usurpation and Fornication
Explains why prohibition does not nullify legal effects like ownership and marital prohibitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a prohibited act can still produce valid legal effects, using the examples of usurpation and fornication.

Core Idea
A prohibited act can establish valid legal consequences if those consequences are incidental and necessary for justice.

This passage from the Hanafi usul text addresses a key question: does a prohibited act completely negate any legal effect? The author argues that prohibition does not nullify incidental effects that are necessary for compensation or establishing marital prohibitions. We will explore how usurpation transfers ownership for the sake of compensation, and how fornication can cause marital prohibitions due to the potential for a child.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage discusses two distinct cases: usurpation (ghasb) and fornication (zina). In both, the act is prohibited, but the author argues that certain legal effects follow.
Usurpation involves wrongful taking of property; fornication involves unlawful sexual relations.
2
Understand the objection
One might object: if an act is prohibited, how can it produce any valid legal effect? This is the surface issue the author addresses.
The objection is based on the assumption that prohibition nullifies all consequences.
3
Analyze the Hanafi reasoning for usurpation
Ownership is not the direct aim of usurpation; rather, it is established as a condition (shart) for the ruling of compensation (daman). Compensation is a good ruling, so the condition becomes good by its goodness.
The reasoning uses the principle that the end justifies the means when the end is legislated as good.
4
Analyze the Hanafi reasoning for fornication
Fornication does not directly cause marital prohibitions; it causes the fluid (water), which causes the child. The child is the true origin of prohibitions and is sinless. Thus, fornication stands in place of lawful intercourse for this effect.
The reasoning is analogical: the substitute takes the ruling of the original in terms of effects.
5
Compare with the opposing view
The Shafi'i school holds that fornication cannot cause marital prohibitions because it is sinful and cannot be a cause for a blessing. The Hanafi response is that the prohibition does not negate the effect; the cause operates by its inherent nature, not by the moral status of the act.
This difference has practical implications in family law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في النهي ولا يقال في الغصب بأنه يثبت الملك مقصودا به بل يثبت شرطاً لحكم شرعي، وهو الضمان؛ لأنه شرع جبراً، فيعتمد الفوات، وشرط الحكم تابع له، فصار حسناً بحسنه. وكذلك الزنا لا يوجب حرمة المصاهرة أصلاً بنفسه، بل إنما هو سبب للماء، والماء سبب للولد والولد هو الأصل في استحقاق الحرمات، ولا عصيان ولا عدوان فيه ثم تتعدّى منه إلى أطرافه وتتعدّى إلى أسبابه، هذه السببية من الزنا فالزنا وأسبابه أقيم مقام الولد الذي هو الأصل في هذه الحرمة.
Section on prohibition: And it is not said regarding usurpation that it establishes ownership intentionally, rather it establishes it as a condition for a legal ruling, which is compensation; because it is legislated as a remedy, so it depends on loss, and the condition of the ruling follows it, so it becomes good by its goodness. Similarly, fornication does not cause marital prohibition by itself at all, but rather it is a cause for the fluid, and the fluid is a cause for the child, and the child is the origin in entitlement to prohibitions, and there is no disobedience nor transgression in it, then it extends from it to its ends and to its causes. This causality from fornication, so fornication and its causes are made to stand in the place of the child, which is the origin in this prohibition.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and reasoning section. The author addresses a common objection: how can a prohibited act like usurpation (ghasb) confer ownership? The answer is that ownership is not the direct goal but a necessary condition for the legal ruling of compensation (daman), which is good. Similarly, fornication (zina), though sinful, does not directly cause marital prohibitions (hurmah al-musaharah); rather, it is a cause for the fluid, which leads to the child, and the child is the true basis for prohibitions. Since zina can result in a child, it is treated like lawful intercourse in establishing prohibitions. The text emphasizes that the child itself is free of sin, so the prohibition derived from it applies regardless of the sinfulness of the intercourse.

Key Terms
النهي (al-nahy): A prohibition that does not necessarily nullify the legal effects of an act if those effects are unintended but necessary for a good ruling.
الغصب (al-ghasb): Usurpation: taking another's property wrongfully, which nevertheless can establish ownership as a condition for compensation.
حرمة المصاهرة (hurmah al-musaharah): Marital prohibitions that arise from sexual relations, including through fornication, based on the potential for a child.
الضمان (al-daman): Compensation, the legal ruling that justifies ownership transfer in usurpation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position explained in this passage, why does usurpation (ghasb) still establish ownership for the usurper?
A) Because the act of usurpation becomes permissible after payment.
B) Because ownership is established as a condition for compensation, which is a good legal ruling.
C) Because the usurper intends to own the property from the start.
D) Because the owner forfeits his right by being negligent.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: prohibition of an act does not nullify its incidental legal effects that are necessary for justice.

Distinguishing Ta'āruḍ from Tanāquḍ in Hanafi Usul
Defining contradiction and distinguishing it from logical inconsistency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ta'āruḍ and tanāquḍ, state the Hanafi position on their relationship, and evaluate the opposing argument.

Core Idea
Contradiction between proofs does not invalidate them; each retains its force until a preponderator resolves it.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, not every conflict between proofs leads to invalidity. This passage clarifies the crucial distinction between ta'āruḍ (contradiction) and tanāquḍ (inconsistency), and explains why the Hanafi school treats them differently. Understanding this distinction is essential for handling apparent conflicts in Islamic legal sources.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two concepts
Extract the definitions of ta'āruḍ and tanāquḍ from the text. Note the key conditions: equal proofs, inability to reconcile for ta'āruḍ; failure of proof to produce its ruling for tanāquḍ.
Ta'āruḍ involves two proofs; tanāquḍ involves one proof and its ruling.
2
Locate the dispute
Find where the commentator al-Muhaqqiq claims that ta'āruḍ and tanāquḍ entail each other in texts. Identify the two reasons he gives.
He argues that failure of a ruling must be due to a preventer (a contradictory proof), and contradiction necessarily leads to failure.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
Understand the author's two-part rebuttal: first, for those who allow tarkhīṣ al-‘illa (specification of the cause), failure is not due to a preventer; second, in ta'āruḍ, each proof still establishes its own ruling, so there is no failure.
This rebuttal preserves the validity of both proofs in a contradiction.
4
Determine the practical consequence
For the Hanafi school, when two proofs contradict, we do not discard them but seek a preponderating factor (tarjīḥ). In tanāquḍ, the proof is invalid.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if ta'āruḍ were tanāquḍ, both proofs would be invalid; Hanafis seek preponderance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المعارضة
وحكم المعارضة بين الآيتين المصير إلى السنة،
=
أن
العلة،
ف: التعارض تقابل الحجتين المتساويتين على وجه لا يمكن الجمع بينهما بوجه، وهو يمنع ثبوت الحكم من غير يتعرض بالدليل، والتناقض تخلّف الدليل عن المدلول بأن يوجد الدليل في بعض المواضع ولايوجد المدلول، سواء كان ذلك التخلّف لمانع، وذلك عند من لا يجوز تخصيص العلة، أو بغير مانع، وذلك عند من يجوز تخصيص وهو يوجب بطلان الدليل في نفسه. قال الشارح * المحقق: إن كل واحد منهما يستلزم الآخر في النصوص، فإن تخلف المدلول عن الدليل فيها لا يكون إلا لمانع، فيكون ذلك المانع معارضاً للدليل، وكذا إذا تعارض النصان يكون الحكم متخلفا عن كل واحد لا محالة، فيتحقق التناقض، فلذلك جمع الشيخ بينهما، كذا قيل. أقول: - وبالله التوفيق فيه نظر، أما أولاً فلأنه لا يصح على مذهب من جوز تخصيص العلة؛ لأن عنده لا يكون التخلف لمانع بل بغير مانع كما عرفت فكيف الاستلزام؟ وأما ثانياً فلأن في صورة التعارض لا يثبت التناقض؛ لأن كل واحد من الدليلين لا يتخلّف عن المدلول، وهو الحكم؛ إذ لو كان ذلك لما يتحقق التعارض، بل كل واحد منهما مثبت للحكم غاية ما في الباب أن حكم كل واحد منهما يخالف حكم الآخر فلا تأخذ بأحدهما بغير مرجّح، فتأمل.
Section on Contradiction. The ruling on contradiction between two verses is recourse to the Sunnah. Contradiction is the confrontation of two equal proofs in a way that cannot be reconciled by any means, and it prevents the establishment of the ruling without exposing the proof. Inconsistency is the failure of the proof to produce the indicated ruling, in that the proof exists in some instances but the indicated ruling does not exist, whether that failure is due to a preventer, according to those who do not allow specification of the cause, or without a preventer, according to those who allow specification, and it necessitates the invalidity of the proof itself. The commentator al-Muhaqqiq said: Each of them entails the other in texts, for the failure of the indicated ruling to follow the proof in texts only occurs due to a preventer, so that preventer is in contradiction with the proof; likewise, if two texts contradict, the ruling inevitably fails to follow each one, so inconsistency is realized. That is why the Sheikh combined them, as it is said. I say: — and with Allah is success — there is consideration in this. First, because it is not valid according to the school of those who allow specification of the cause, because for them the failure is not due to a preventer but without a preventer, as you know, so how can there be entailment? Second, because in the case of contradiction, inconsistency does not hold, because each of the two proofs does not fail to produce the indicated ruling, i.e., the ruling; if that were the case, contradiction would not be realized; rather each one establishes the ruling, the only issue being that the ruling of each contradicts the ruling of the other, so we do not adopt either without a preponderating factor. So ponder.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defines two key usul concepts: ta'āruḍ (contradiction) and tanāquḍ (inconsistency). The author begins by stating the ruling on contradiction between Quranic verses leading to recourse to Sunnah. Then he defines ta'āruḍ as two equal proofs that cannot be reconciled, preventing the ruling from being established without the proof itself being questioned. Tanāquḍ is defined as a proof failing to produce its ruling in some instances, either due to a preventer (for those who do not allow specifying the cause, i.e., the 'illa) or without a preventer (for those who allow specification), and it invalidates the proof. The commentator al-Muhaqqiq argued that ta'āruḍ and tanāquḍ entail each other in texts. The author rebuts this: first, for those who allow specification of the cause, the failure is not due to a preventer, so entailment fails; second, in true ta'āruḍ, each proof still establishes its own ruling, so there is no failure—it is only that rulings conflict, requiring a preponderating factor. This distinction is critical in Hanafi usul because it affects how contradictory proofs are handled: they are not automatically invalid; rather, we seek a preponderator.

Key Terms
التعارض (al-ta'āruḍ): Confrontation of two equal proofs in a way that cannot be reconciled
التناقض (al-tanāquḍ): Failure of a proof to produce its indicated ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of tanāquḍ compared to ta'āruḍ?
A) Both invalidate the proofs involved.
B) Tanāquḍ invalidates the proof, while ta'āruḍ does not.
C) Both require seeking a preponderator.
D) Neither affects the validity of the proof.

Key Takeaway
Ta'āruḍ (contradiction) between equal proofs does not invalidate them; it requires seeking a preponderator, whereas tanāquḍ (inconsistency) invalidates the proof.

Conflict Between Negative and Positive Reports (Khabar al-Nafy vs Khabar al-Ithbat)
Dispute on whether a negating report conflicts with an affirming report

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an affirmative report (khabar al-ithbat) takes precedence over a negative report (khabar al-nafy) when they conflict, and explain why the case of Barira illustrates this principle.

Core Idea
An affirmative report is stronger than a negative one because negation only confirms the original status.

This passage addresses a fundamental usul dispute among Hanafis: when two equally authentic reports conflict—one affirming a new attribute, the other negating it—which prevails? The majority, led by al-Karkhi, holds that the affirmative report is given precedence. The dispute is illustrated by the well-known story of Barira, where narrations differed on whether her husband was slave or free. In this lesson, you will learn the Hanafi position, the reasoning behind it, and the practical consequence of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting reports
Determine which report affirms an additional attribute (khabar al-ithbat) and which negates it (khabar al-nafy). In the Barira case, the affirmative report states the husband was free; the negative report states he was a slave.
The affirmative report introduces a new element (freedom), while the negative maintains the original state (slavery).
2
Define the terms muthbit and nafi
Understand that the muthbit affirms an extra ruling not previously established, while the nafi keeps the ruling on the original state.
These terms are key to the Hanafi analysis.
3
State the majority Hanafi rule
Al-Karkhi and the Shafi'is hold that affirmation is given precedence over negation; thus no real conflict exists—the affirmative report is acted upon.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
4
Contrast with the minority view
Isa ibn Aban holds that the two reports truly conflict and the mujtahid must choose without presumption. Note the practical difference in the Barira case.
The thamarah al-khilaf: under the majority, Barira's husband is free; under Isa, the mujtahid may rule differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف مشايخنا في أن خبر النفي هل يعارض خبر الإثبات أم لا ؟ واختلف عمل أصحابنا المتقدمين في ذلك، فقد روي أن بريرة أعتقت وزوجها عبد، وروي أنها أعتقت وزوجها حرّ، مع اتفاقهم على أنه كان عبداً، فأصحابنا أخذوا بالمثبت. الرواة زوج بريرة الثلاثة وهو خبر الحرية. واختلف مشايخنا: فذهب الشيخ أبو الحسن الكرخي وكذا أصحاب الشافعي له إلى أن الإثبات مقدّم على النفي، فلا تعارض بينهما حقيقةً، فيعمل بالإثبات، وإنما يقع التعارض بينهما صورةً وذهب الإمام عيسى بن أبان إلى أنهما يتعارضان حقيقة، والمراد بالمثبت ما يثبت أمراً زائداً لم يكن ثابتاً فيما مضى، وبالنافي ما ينفي الأمر الزائد ويبقيه على الأصل. في ذلك: حيث أخذوا بالمثبت في بعض المواضع، وفي بعضها بالنافي. ثم أثبت ذلك بقوله: فقد روي إلخ. عبد: كما في الصحيحين عن أم المؤمنين عائشة لما قالت إنه الا الله خيرها وكان زوجها عبداً. [البخاري، رقم: ٥٢٨٤، مسلم رقم ٣٧٨٥] أنها أي بريرة أعتقت وزوجها حرّ كما في الكتب الستة، كذا في التيسير. عبداً في الحقيقة، وإنما وقع الاختلاف في الحرّية العارضة، فقال بعضهم: إنه كان عبداً على حاله حين خير وهو مختار الشافعي يالله حيث لا يثبت الخيار للمعتقة عنده إلا إذا كان زوجها عبداً، فهو عمل النبي بريرة، بريرة: اعلم أن بريرة كانت مكاتبة لعائشة، وكانت في نكاح عبد اسمه مغيث فلما أدت بدل الكتابة خيرها النبي فما اختارت زوجها، فكان يبكي في حبها ويمشي في سكك المدينة متحيراً.
And our scholars differed regarding whether a report of negation conflicts with a report of affirmation or not. And the practice of our earlier companions differed in that: it was narrated that Barira was freed and her husband was a slave, and it was narrated that she was freed and her husband was free, while they agreed that he was a slave. So our companions took the affirmative. The three narrators of Barira's husband [reported] the report of freedom. And our scholars differed: Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi and likewise the companions of al-Shafi'i [held] that affirmation is given precedence over negation, so there is no real conflict between them; they act by affirmation, and the conflict occurs only in form. Imam Isa ibn Aban held that they truly conflict. The term 'muthbit' means what affirms an additional matter that was not previously established, and 'nafi' means what negates the additional matter and keeps it upon the original. In that: they took the affirmative in some places and the negative in others. Then he proved that by his saying: 'it was narrated...' Slave: as in the two Sahihs from the Mother of the Believers Aisha when she said that the Prophet gave her choice and her husband was a slave. [Bukhari no. 5284, Muslim no. 3785] That Barira was freed and her husband was free as in the six books, likewise in al-Taysir. In reality he was a slave, and the difference occurred in the accidental freedom. Some said he remained a slave at the time of choice, which is the view of al-Shafi'i, because according to him the choice for a freedwoman is only established if her husband is a slave. So he acted according to the Prophet's action regarding Barira. Barira: know that Barira was a mukatibah of Aisha, and she was in marriage to a slave named Mughith. When she paid the kitaba, the Prophet gave her choice, and she did not choose her husband, so he cried from his love for her and walked in the streets of Medina bewildered.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage addresses a key usul dispute among Hanafi scholars: whether a hadith that negates an additional attribute (like freedom) conflicts with a hadith that affirms it. The context is the case of Barira, where some narrations state her husband remained a slave (negating freedom), while others state he was freed (affirming freedom). The majority of Hanafi scholars (following Karkhi) and Imam al-Shafi'i hold that an affirmative report is given precedence over a negative one, because the negative merely maintains the original status (al-bara'a al-asliyya), while the affirmative brings a new ruling. Thus, there is no real conflict—the affirmative report is acted upon. Imam Isa ibn Aban, however, holds that they truly conflict and the mujtahid must choose. The text illustrates with the well-known story of Barira, where the Prophet gave her the choice to stay with her husband or leave when she became free. The dispute over her husband's status at that time (slave vs free) led to different narrations, and the Hanafis adopted the report that affirmed freedom (khabar al-ithbat), aligning with the majority view that affirmation prevails over negation.

Key Terms
خبر النفي (khabar al-nafy): A report that negates an additional attribute, asserting the original state (e.g., the husband was a slave, i.e., no new freedom).
خبر الإثبات (khabar al-ithbat): A report that affirms an additional attribute not previously established (e.g., the husband was free).
المثبت (al-muthbit): The one who affirms an additional ruling or attribute (e.g., freedom of Barira's husband).
النافي (al-nafi): The one who negates the additional attribute, maintaining the original state (e.g., slavery of Barira's husband).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (like Karkhi), when a report of negation conflicts with a report of affirmation, which is given precedence?
A) The negative report
B) The affirmative report
C) Both are considered equal
D) The stronger in chain

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule that an affirmative report takes precedence over a negative report resolves apparent conflicts between hadiths without needing abrogation, as illustrated by the case of Barira.

Resolving Conflict Between Negative and Positive Reports
Rule for preferring negative reports when based on evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for resolving conflict between negative and positive reports by identifying whether the negative report is based on evidence or default.

Core Idea
A negative report only conflicts with a positive if it is based on evidence – otherwise the positive prevails.

In Islamic legal theory, reports (riwayat) often conflict. The Hanafi school developed a nuanced rule: a negative report (nafi) does not automatically conflict with a positive one (ithbat). The key is whether the negative report is grounded in evidence (dalil) or merely in default presumption. This lesson unpacks that rule using two famous hadith examples: the marriage of Maymunah and the case of Barirah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting reports
Determine which report is negative (nafi) and which is positive (ithbat). The negative negates an attribute; the positive affirms it.
2
Assess the basis of the negative report
Check if the negative report is 'of the type known by its evidence' (like ihram being observed) or based on default (like slavery being assumed). If based on evidence, it is like a positive report.
3
Determine if conflict occurs
Only if both reports are based on evidence do they conflict equally. If the negative is based on default, it does not oppose the positive.
4
Apply tarjih if conflict
If conflict occurs, prefer by narrator conditions (e.g., reliability). The Hanafi default is to prefer the negative if based on evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب السنة فصل في المعارضة. فجعل أصحابنا العمل بالنافي أولى، وقالوا في الجرح والتعديل: إن الجرح أولى، وهو المثبت. والأصل في أن النفي متى كان من جنس ما يعرف بدليله أو كان مما يشتبه حاله لكن عُرف أن الراوي اعتمد على دليل المعرفة كان مثل الإثبات وإلا فلا. فالنفي في حديث بريرة مما لا يعرف إلا بظاهر الحال، فلم يعارض الإثبات. وفي حديث ميمونة مما يعرف بدليله، وهو هيئة المحرم، فوقعت المعارضة. فإذا كان النفي مثل الإثبات في كونهما مبنيين على دليل، وقعت المعارضة بين الخبرين على السواء، فاحتيج إلى ترجيح أحدهما بحال الراوي. وطهارة الماء وحل الطعام والشراب من جنس ما يعرف بدليله؛ لأنه ليس لنفي الحرية الطارية عنده دليل سوى أنه بناه على ظاهر الحال وهو أن عبديته كانت معلومة متقرّرة من قبل.
Chapter of Sunnah, Section on Objection. Our companions made the negative report preferable, and they said in jarh and ta'dil: jarh is preferable, and it is the positive. The principle is that when the negative is of the type that is known by its evidence, or it is of ambiguous state but it is known that the narrator relied on evidence of knowledge, it is like the positive and otherwise not. So the negative in the hadith of Barirah is not known except by apparent state, so it did not oppose the positive. In the hadith of Maymunah, it is known by its evidence, which is the state of ihram, so opposition occurred. So if the negative is like the positive in both being based on evidence, opposition occurs between the two reports equally, so one must be preferred by the condition of the narrator. Purity of water and permissibility of food and drink are of the type known by their evidence; because to negate the accidental freedom he has no evidence except that he based it on apparent state, which is that his slavery was known and established previously.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi usul rule for resolving apparent conflict (ta'arud) between a negative report (nafi) and a positive report (ithbat). The context is the case of the Prophet's marriage to Maymunah: one report (from Ibn Abbas) says he married her while in ihram (negative of halal), another (from Yazid ibn al-Asamm) says he was halal (positive of halal). Hanafi scholars ruled that the negative report is to be given precedence, but only if it is based on evidence (dalil) or if despite being ambiguous, it is known that the narrator relied on evidence. If the negative is simply based on default (istishab) or apparent state (zahir al-hal), it does not equal the positive, which is always based on evidence. The rule is applied to the hadith of Barirah (where a narrator said her husband was a slave, negating freedom, but that was based on default, not evidence) and the hadith of Maymunah (where the negative of halal was based on observed signs of ihram, hence evidence). The passage also gives the example of water purity and food permissibility: a report that water is impure (negative of purity) must be based on evidence, not on default. This principle resolves the methodological dispute between Hanafi scholars al-Karkhi and Ibn Aban regarding when negation can oppose affirmation.

Key Terms
النفي (al-nafi): A report that negates an additional or accidental attribute (like freedom, halal status)
الإثبات (al-ithbat): A report that affirms an additional attribute (like freedom, halal status) beyond the default
ظاهر الحال (zahir al-hal): Default or apparent state, not based on specific evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always, because negative reports are inherently stronger.
B) Only if the negative report is based on evidence (dalil) or known to rely on evidence.
C) Only if the negative report comes from a more reliable narrator.
D) Never, because positive reports always take precedence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule resolves hadith conflict by conditioning the weight of a negative report on its evidentiary basis, separating evidence-based negation from default presumption.

The Invalidity of Tarjih by Number, Gender, and Freedom in Conflicting Reports
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tarjih criteria

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on tarjih by number and personal attributes, explain the opposing view, and evaluate the Hanafi refutation using consensus and rational critique.

Core Idea
Tarjih by number or gender/freedom is abandoned by Salaf consensus; only precision and trustworthiness matter.

When two hadith reports conflict, scholars seek to prefer one over the other. In Hanafi usul, some argued that preference could be given by the number of narrators or their attributes like masculinity and freedom—similar to testimonial evidence. However, the majority of Hanafi imams, including Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, rejected this, relying on the consensus of the Salaf and a critical distinction between testimony and narration. This lesson dissects that dispute and clarifies the correct Hanafi methodology for weighing conflicting reports.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting views on tarjih
Recognize that some Hanafis (e.g., Abdullah al-Jurjani, Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi in one narration) held that preference can be given by greater number, masculinity, and freedom. The majority view opposes this.
The majority view is the mufta bihi position.
2
Analyze the opposing dalil: analogy with water/food issues
Understand that the proponents analogized from rulings in water and food (testimony-like situations) where Imam Muhammad gave weight to number, freedom, and gender. They claimed these factors also apply to hadith reports.
This analogy is central to the dispute.
3
Examine the Hanafi response: distinction between testimony and narration
The majority refutes the analogy by highlighting that testimony (shahada) is based on direct observation, whereas hadith narration is based on transmission. The rules differ because testimony requires a different threshold for proof.
The passage states: 'this is analogy with a difference.'
4
Recognize the use of Ijma as evidence
The Hanafis cite the consensus of the Salaf—that they did not practice tarjih by number or personal attributes—as a decisive proof. This constitutes an independent dalil against the opposing view.
Ijma of the Salaf is a valid source of law in Hanafi usul.
5
Apply the principle: valid criteria for tarjih
The correct criteria are increased precision (dabt), accuracy (itqan), and trustworthiness (thiqa). These are internal qualities of the narrator, not external factors like number or gender.
This aligns with the practice of the Salaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

القلب إليه أميل، وبالذكورة والحرية في العدد دون الأفراد؛ فإذا تعارض الخبران فيه بأن يقول أحد إنه نجس أو حرام، ويقول الآخر: طاهر أو حلال، فخبر الأول مثبت للحرمة والنجاسة العارضتين ولا شك أنه مبني على دليل وهو أنه رأى سبب الحرمة والنجاسة، وخبر الثاني ناف للحرمة والنجاسة المذكورتين فخبر النفي يشتبه حاله لم يدر أن الراوي أخبر بالأصل أو بدليل، فلا بد من أن يتفحص حاله، فإن كان خبره بمجرد أن الأصل فيهما
