Ruling Attached to Outward Cause: Mistaken Utterance
Usul rule on mistaken utterance with Shafi'i dispute and qiyas extension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi usul principle that the ruling attaches to the external cause (al-sabab al-zahir) and apply it to mistaken utterances in divorce and sale, while also explaining the role of qiyas in extending the ruling.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, legal effect is based on the outward cause, not hidden intent, to avoid hardship.

This passage presents a key Hanafi usul principle: rulings are tied to the external, observable cause rather than the inner intention of the actor. This is demonstrated through the case of a mistaken utterance in divorce and sale, where even if the speaker intended something else, the legal effect still applies. The passage also shows how qiyas extends this principle to analogous cases, such as the sale of a coerced person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Act and Ruling
First, note the specific acts: divorce (talāq) and sale (bayʿ) issued by mistake. According to the Hanafi school, both are valid (divorce takes effect, sale is concluded but invalid).
The text distinguishes between validity (divorce) and conclusion (sale) due to lack of consent.
2
Locate the Hanafi Dalil (Proof)
The Hanafi proof is that intent (qaṣd) is an internal matter (bāṭin) that cannot be accessed. Therefore, the ruling (ḥukm) is attached to the external cause (al-sabab al-ẓāhir) – the spoken words – to avoid hardship (ḥaraj).
This is a textual indication from the principle of rafʿ al-ḥaraj.
3
Understand the Opposing View (Shafi'i)
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that divorce occurs only by speech uttered with intent. A mistaken person lacks intent, so the utterance is void, akin to the speech of an insane person or sleeper.
This disagreement roots in different weight given to internal vs. external causes.
4
Analyze the Qiyas (Analogy) used by the Author
The author extends the same principle to a mistaken sale, analogizing to the coerced person (mukrah). Since there is no transmitted (riwāyah) ruling on mistaken sale, the ruling is established by qiyas.
The qiyas uses the common 'illah (cause): the cause (spoken offer and acceptance) emanated from a qualified person (ahlīyyah), but consent is missing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكفارة، وصح طلاقه عندنا، ويجب أن ينعقد بيعه كبيع المكره.‏ باب القياس وهو أي الجزاء القاصر [السفر] عوارض مكتسبة وأما السفر فهو من أسباب التخفيف يؤثر في قصر ذوات الأربع وفي تأخير الصوم، الكفارة: لأنها من وجه عبادة حيث لايلزم على الكفار، ومن وجه عقوبة حيث يجب جزاء لفعله، والجزاء القاصر يناسب لتقصير يسير بخلاف القصاص والحد؛ إذ هما جزاءان كاملان وعقوبتان تامتان فلايجبان على الخاطئ المعذور. وصح طلاقه حتى لو أراد أن يقول لامرأته: اسقي، فجرى على لسانه أنتِ طالق وقع عليها الطلاق؛ لأن القصد أمر باطن لا يمكن الاطلاع عليه، فيتعلق الحكم بالسبب الظاهر الدال عليه، وأهلية القصد الثابتة بالعقل والبلوغ نفياً للحرج كما في السفر مع المشقة، وهذا السبب متحقق فيمن يدعي الخطاء، وعند الشافعي لا يقع؛ إذ الطلاق يقع بالكلام، وهو إنما يصح إذا صدر عن قصد والمخطئ غير قاصد فلايعتد به مثل كلام المجنون والنائم. كبيع المكره: حتى إذا جرى على لسانه البيع خطاً بأن أراد أن يقول: الحمد الله، فجرى على لسانه: بعث هذا بكذا، وقال الآخر: قبلتُ، ينعقد البيع فاسداً كبيع المكره، أما انعقاده فلأن السبب صدر عن أهله، وأما فساده فلفوات الرضاء، ولما لم يكن في هذه المسألة رواية عن أصحابنا قال المصنف: يجب ليدل أنه أثبتها قياساً على المكره.
The expiation, and his divorce is valid according to us, and it is necessary that his sale be concluded like the sale of the coerced one. Chapter of Qiyas. It is: i.e., the deficient retribution. [Travel] Acquired accidental qualities. As for travel, it is a cause of concession, affecting the shortening of four-rak'ah prayers and the postponement of fasting. The expiation: because it is from one aspect worship, since it is not obligatory upon disbelievers, and from another aspect punishment, since a retribution is required for his action, and the deficient retribution is appropriate for minor deficiency, unlike retaliation and fixed punishments; for they are complete retributions and complete punishments, so they are not obligatory upon the mistaken excused one. And his divorce is valid, even if he intended to say to his wife: 'Give water,' but his tongue slipped and he said: 'You are divorced,' divorce occurs upon her. Because intention is an internal matter that cannot be accessed, so the ruling attaches to the external cause that indicates it, and the capacity for intention established by reason and puberty negates hardship, as in travel with difficulty, and this cause exists in the one who claims error. According to al-Shafi'i, it does not occur; for divorce occurs by speech, and it is only valid if issued with intent, and the mistaken one is not intending, so it is not considered like the speech of the insane or sleeping. Like the sale of the coerced one: even if the sale slips from his tongue by mistake, such that he intended to say 'Praise be to Allah,' but his tongue slipped and he said: 'I sold this for such-and-such,' and the other said: 'I accept,' the sale is concluded as invalid, like the sale of the coerced one. As for its conclusion, because the cause came from its qualified person; and as for its invalidity, because of the loss of consent. And since there is no transmission from our companions on this issue, the author said: 'It is necessary' to indicate that he established it by analogy to the coerced one.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling with commentary. It addresses the effect of a mistaken utterance in divorce and sale. The Hanafi position is that if a person intends to say one thing but inadvertently says a divorce or a contract of sale, the divorce or sale takes effect. The reasoning is that intention is internal and inaccessible, so the ruling is based on the external cause (the spoken words), provided the person is legally competent (adult and sane). This avoids hardship. The text then contrasts with al-Shafi'i, who requires intent for such utterances, so the mistaken utterance would be null like the speech of the insane or sleeping. The analogy is drawn to sale under coercion (mukrah): the sale is concluded but invalid (fasid) because the cause (spoken offer and acceptance) is present, but consent is lacking. Since there is no transmitted ruling from the Hanafi founding imams on this specific scenario, the author establishes it by qiyas (analogy) to the coerced sale. This shows the usul principle of applying the outward cause over inner intent.

Key Terms
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): Expiation; a religious penalty that is both an act of worship (not required of disbelievers) and a punishment (required for one's action).
الجزاء القاصر (al-jazā' al-qāṣir): Deficient retribution; a penalty that is less than complete (like kaffarah) and thus suitable for minor shortcomings.
المكره (al-mukrah): The coerced person; one who is forced to act under threat of harm, whose actions are concluded but may be invalid due to lack of consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a mistaken divorce valid even though the speaker intended something else?
A) Because intention is irrelevant in all acts.
B) Because the ruling attaches to the external cause (spoken words) to avoid hardship.
C) Because the speaker's capacity is presumed from puberty alone.
D) Because the mistake is considered a form of coercion.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, legal effect is determined by the outward, observable cause (al-sabab al-ẓāhir), not the hidden intent, to ensure certainty and avoid hardship.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

Types of Ikrāh: Ibaḥa vs Rukḥṣa
Classifying actions under complete duress into ibāḥa and rukḥṣa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ibāḥa (permissibility) and rukḥṣa (concession) under complete duress, and apply the Quranic dalīl to identify which category an action falls under.

Core Idea
Under complete duress, some acts become permissible, others remain prohibited but are excused as concessions.

This passage teaches us that actions under duress are not all treated the same. Some become absolutely permissible (ibāḥa), others remain prohibited but are excused as a concession (rukḥṣa). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying rulings on coerced actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ikrāh
Recognise that the text distinguishes between complete duress (ikrāh kāmil) and partial duress (ikrāh qāṣir).
Complete duress is coercive to the point of overwhelming compulsion; partial duress is less intensive (e.g., shackling or beating).
2
List the three categories of actions under complete duress
The text mentions: (a) actions that remain absolutely prohibited despite duress (e.g., killing, zinā for men), (b) actions that become permissible (ibāḥa: carrion, wine, pork), (c) actions that become concessions (rukḥṣa: uttering kufr, spoiling prayer and fasting, destroying property, violating ihrām, woman enabling zinā).
3
Distinguish ibāḥa from rukḥṣa
In ibāḥa, the original prohibition is lifted entirely; in rukḥṣa, the prohibition remains but the act is excused to avoid greater harm.
The text highlights that for rukḥṣa, if the person endures and dies, he is a martyr; for ibāḥa, refraining would be sinful because the prohibition is gone.
4
Apply the Quranic dalīl for ibāḥa
The Quran (6:119) exempts from prohibition ‘mā uḍṭurirtum ilayhi’ (what you are compelled to). This forms the basis for complete duress lifting the prohibition entirely for carrion, wine, and pork.
The text states that these become mubāḥ, not just rukhṣa.
5
Note the practical consequence
If someone under complete duress refuses to perform a rukhṣa act (e.g., utter kufr) and is killed, he is a martyr. If he refuses a mubāḥ act (e.g., eat carrion) and dies, he is sinful because the prohibition was lifted.

لا يصلح لإبطال شيء من الأقوال والأفعال جملةً إلا بدليل غيره على مثال فعل الطائع. كالطلاق والعتاق كالقتل وإتلاف المال
والجناية إلخ : أي رخص له الجناية على إحرامه بقتل الصيد ولبس المخيط بسبب الإكراه الكامل. وتمكين المرأة: أي رخص للمرأة أن تمكن الرجل بالزنا في الإكراه الكامل متعلق بالكل، هذا مثال الرخصة، وإنما صارت تلك الأشياء مرخصة فيها عند الإكراه الكامل لا مباحاً؛ لأن حرمة تلك الأشياء باقية على حالها، وإنما رخص للمكره الكامل دفعاً للحرج، ولهذا لو صبر المكره حتى قتل كان شهيداً ومأجوراً إن شاء الله تعالى، بخلاف المباح حيث لا يبقى الحرمة فيها ولا يؤجر المكره في امتناعه عنه بل يأثم كما مرّ، ولما كان يتوهم أن المرأة
كما ترخص في الإكراه الكامل يجب أن يرخص الرجل أيضا في الإكراه الكامل دفعه بقوله: وإنما فارق إلخ. في الرخصة حيث رخص لها التمكين من الزنا، ولا يرخص للرجل الزنا بالإكراه؛ لأن نسبة الولد لا تنقطع عنها، فلم يكن تمكين المرأة رجلا بالزنا معنى القتل أي قتل الولد الذي هو المانع من الترخص في جانب الرجل. بخلاف الرجل حيث ينقطع عنه نسبة الولد كما مرّ. ولهذا أي لأجل أن الإكراه الكامل أوجب الرخصة في جانبها دون جانبه أوجب الإكراه القاصر: وهو الإكراه بالقيد أو بالضرب شبهة في درء الحد إلخ والحاصل أن الإكراه الكامل لما أوجب الرخصة في جانب المرأة أوجب الإكراه القاصر شبهة في دفع الحد عنها، حتى إذا أكرهت على الزنا بالقيد فمكنت الرجل بالزنا لا يجب عليها الحد، والرجل لما لم يوجب الإكراه الكامل في حقه الرخصة لم يوجب الإكراه الناقص شبهة في درء الحد عنه حتى إذا أكره على الزنا بالضرب أو القيد فزنى يجب عليه الحد، وأما الإكراه الكامل فيوجب شبهة في درء الحد عنه استحساناً كما هو قول أبي حنيفة بالله آخراً، وهو قولهما، فلا يجب عليه الحد، وكان القياس أن لا يسقط عنه الحد كما هو قول أبي حنيفة ماله أولاً، وهو قول زفر به بهذه الجملة: أي بما قلنا من أن الإكراه لا ينافي الأهلية ولا يسقط الخطاب حتى تثبت الأحكام المذكورة فعل الطائع وهو ضدّ المكره ، والحاصل كما أن فعل الطائع وقوله "لا يبطل بل يعتبر إلا إذا لحقه" دليل مغيّر فحينئذ لا يعتبر كما إذا قال لامرأته: أنتِ طالق، يقع الطلاق بعد التكلم إلا إذا لحقه مغيّر من أو تعليق، فحينئذ لا يقع، وكذا إذا شرب الخمر، أو زنى يعتبر ذلك، ويقع عليه الحد إلا إذا لحقه مانع ومغير كتحقق تلك الأفعال في دار الحرب أو تمكين الشبهة فيها فحينئذ لا يعتبر، فكذلك جميع أفعال المكره وأقواله تعتبر وتصح لصدورها عن عقل وأهلية خطاب إلا عند وجود المغيّر، فحينئذ لاتصح ولاتعتبر، وكما كان يرد أن الإكراه لما لم يصح لإبطال شيء من الأقوال والأفعال ففي أي موضع يظهر أثره؟ دفعه بقوله التالي: وإنما إلخ.
استثناء
Chapter of Qiyas. Types of coercion. And zinā is not excused by duress originally, and there is no prohibition of the complete [duress] regarding carrion, wine, and pig. The ruling of concession in uttering the word of kufr. And he permitted uttering the word of kufr, and spoiling prayer and fasting, and destroying the property of others. And the offense against Ihrām, and a woman enabling a man for zinā under complete duress. And her act differs from his act in the concession; because the lineage of the child is not severed from her, so it is not similar to killing, unlike the man. And for this, the man's act [is different]. Enabling a woman a man for zinā. The partial duress created a doubt in averting the punishment from her but not from the man. So it is established by this statement that duress is not suitable to nullify any of the statements and actions absolutely except by another evidence on the example of the obedient person's act. Like divorce and manumission, like killing and destroying property. And the offense etc.: meaning he permitted the offense against his Ihrām by killing game and wearing stitched clothing due to complete duress. And enabling the woman: meaning he permitted the woman to enable the man for zinā under complete duress, related to all. This is an example of concession. And those things become permissible by concession under complete duress, not permissible; because the prohibition of those things remains as it is, but a concession was granted to the one under complete duress to remove hardship. For this, if the coerced person endured until he was killed, he would be a martyr and rewarded, God willing, unlike the permissible where the prohibition does not remain, and the coerced person is not rewarded for refraining from it, but rather sins, as passed. And since it could be understood that the woman, just as she is given concession under complete duress, should also be given concession to the man under complete duress, he refuted it by his saying: 'And indeed it differed...' regarding the concession where she is permitted to enable zinā, and the man is not permitted zinā under duress; because the lineage of the child is not severed from her, so enabling a woman a man for zinā is not in the sense of killing, i.e., killing the child which is the preventer of concession on the man's side. Unlike the man where the lineage is severed from him, as passed. And for this, i.e., because complete duress required concession on her side but not on his side, the partial duress, which is duress by shackling or beating, created a doubt in averting punishment [from her]. And the conclusion: when complete duress required concession on the woman's side, partial duress created a doubt in repelling punishment from her, so that if she is coerced to zinā by shackling and enables a man for zinā, the punishment is not wajib on her. And the man, since complete duress did not require concession in his right, partial duress did not create doubt in repelling punishment from him, so that if he is coerced to zinā by beating or shackling and commits zinā, the punishment is wajib on him. As for complete duress, it creates doubt in repelling punishment from him by istihsān, as is the later opinion of Abū Ḥanīfa (raḍiyallāhu 'anhu) and it is the opinion of the two [Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad], so punishment is not wajib on him. And the analogical reasoning was that punishment should not be dropped from him, as is the earlier opinion of Abū Ḥanīfa and the opinion of Zufar. By this statement: i.e., by what we said that duress does not conflict with legal capacity nor does it remove legal address so that the mentioned rulings are established. The action of the obedient person, and he is the opposite of the coerced. And the conclusion: just as the action and statement of the obedient person are not invalidated but are considered unless a modifying evidence intervenes, then they are not considered — as when he says to his wife: 'You are divorced', the divorce occurs after the utterance unless a modifier or condition intervenes, then it does not occur. And similarly, if he drinks wine or commits zinā, that is considered, and punishment occurs on him unless a preventer and modifier like those acts occurring in dār al-ḥarb or the presence of a doubt, then it is not considered. So too all actions and statements of the coerced are considered and valid because they issue from intellect and capacity for address, except when a modifier exists, then they are not valid and are not considered. And as it could be objected that since duress is not valid to nullify any of the statements and actions, then in which place does its effect appear? He repelled it by his following saying: 'And indeed...' Exception.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a systematic discussion of the types of ikrāh (duress) and their legal effects in Hanafi fiqh. The author explains that actions are categorized under duress into three categories: (1) actions where the prohibition remains despite duress (such as killing an innocent person and zinā for men); (2) actions that become permissible (ibāḥa) under complete duress, like eating carrion, drinking wine, or eating pork, based on Quran 6:119; (3) actions that remain prohibited but are granted as a concession (rukḥṣa) under complete duress, such as uttering words of kufr while keeping faith in the heart, spoiling prayer and fasting, destroying another's property, committing violations of ihrām, and a woman enabling zinā. The key distinction between ibāḥa and rukḥṣa: in ibāḥa the prohibition is lifted entirely, so one is sinful for not doing it if life depends; in rukḥṣa the prohibition remains but the person is excused to avoid greater harm, and if they endure and die, they are martyrs. The passage also differentiates between men and women regarding zinā: lineage is not severed from the woman, so she is granted concession; for men, lineage is lost, so no concession even under complete duress. Under partial duress (e.g., shackling or beating), women get a doubt (shubha) averting the ḥadd punishment, but men do not — except that under complete duress, by istiḥsān, the ḥadd is also averted for men according to the later opinion of Abū Ḥanīfa and the two companions. The underlying principle: ikrāh does not nullify statements or actions per se because the coerced person retains intellect and legal capacity; rather, a modifying evidence (dalīl mughayyir) may change the ruling, similar to how a condition or exception can modify an otherwise effective statement like divorce.

Key Terms
إكراه كامل (ikrāh kāmil): Duress that is overwhelming and compelling, such that the person cannot resist without fear of death or severe harm.
إكراه قاصر (ikrāh qāṣir): Incomplete or partial duress, such as being shackled or beaten, but not to the point of overwhelming compulsion.
رخصة (rukḥṣa): A ruling that permits an act due to necessity while the original prohibition remains in place.
إباحة (ibāḥa): A ruling that makes an act permissible with no remaining prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between ibāḥa and rukḥṣa under complete duress?
A) In ibāḥa, the prohibition is removed; in rukḥṣa, it remains but the act is excused.
B) In ibāḥa, the act is forbidden; in rukḥṣa, it is obligatory.
C) In ibāḥa, the person is punished if they refuse; in rukḥṣa, they are rewarded.
D) There is no difference; they are synonyms.

Key Takeaway
Complete duress either lifts the prohibition (ibāḥa) or leaves it intact but excuses the act (rukḥṣa), depending on the type of act.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
advanced
10 min
Gender, Shubha, and Istihsān in Duress
Comparing legal consequences for men and women under duress regarding zinā

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why women and men are treated differently regarding zinā under duress, and evaluate the role of istiḥsān in dropping the ḥadd for men.

Core Idea
Lineage preservation creates a shubha for women that averts punishment under duress, while men require complete duress to benefit from istiḥsān.

This passage delves into the specific case of zinā under duress, highlighting a critical gender distinction. For women, both complete and partial duress create a shubha (doubt) that averts the ḥadd punishment, because the child's lineage is not severed. For men, only complete duress—by way of istiḥsān—can create such a shubha; under partial duress, the ḥadd is imposed. The later Hanafi position (muftā bihī) adopts istiḥsān for men under complete duress as well.

Guided Path
1
Identify the gender difference in concessions
The text states that a woman is granted a concession (rukḥṣa) to enable zinā under complete duress, but a man is not given such a concession for committing zinā.
The reason is that the child's lineage is not severed from the woman, so the act is not akin to killing the lineage; for the man, the lineage is lost, making it like killing.
2
Understand the shubha under partial duress
For a woman, even partial duress (shackling or beating) creates a shubha that averts the ḥadd penalty. For a man, partial duress does not create such a shubha, and the ḥadd is imposed.
The passage: 'أوجب الإكراه القاصر شبهة في درء الحد عنها دون الرجل' (partial duress created a doubt in averting punishment from her but not from the man).
3
Recognise the role of istiḥsān for men under complete duress
Under complete duress, the man does not get a concession to commit zinā, but by istiḥsān (the later opinion of Abū Ḥanīfa and the Ṣāḥibayn), the ḥadd is dropped because the duress creates a shubha.
Qiyās would require the ḥadd (early opinion of Abū Ḥanīfa and Zufar), but istiḥsān overrides it to avoid hardship.
4
Analyse the underlying principle: duress does not nullify per se
The text emphasises that duress itself does not invalidate statements or actions because the coerced person retains intellect and capacity. A modifying evidence (dalīl mughayyir) may change the ruling, such as a shubha or necessity.
This principle explains why the ruling for men changes only with complete duress, not partial.
5
Determine the operative ruling (muftā bihī)
The relied-upon view is the later Hanafi position: under complete duress, the man is also excused from ḥadd by istiḥsān. This is the view used for fatwā.
The text prefers this view over the earlier qiyās-based opinion.

Like divorce and manumission, like killing and destroying property. And the offense etc.: meaning he permitted the offense against his Ihrām by killing game and wearing stitched clothing due to complete duress. And enabling the woman: meaning he permitted the woman to enable the man for zinā under complete duress, related to all. This is an example of concession. And those things become permissible by concession under complete duress, not permissible; because the prohibition of those things remains as it is, but a concession was granted to the one under complete duress to remove hardship. Unlike the man where the lineage is severed from him, as passed. And for this, i.e., because complete duress required concession on her side but not on his side, the partial duress, which is duress by shackling or beating, created a doubt in averting punishment [from her]. And the conclusion: when complete duress required concession on the woman's side, partial duress created a doubt in repelling punishment from her, so that if she is coerced to zinā by shackling and enables a man for zinā, the punishment is not wajib on her. And the man, since complete duress did not require concession in his right, partial duress did not create doubt in repelling punishment from him, so that if he is coerced to zinā by beating or shackling and commits zinā, the punishment is wajib on him. As for complete duress, it creates doubt in repelling punishment from him by istihsān, as is the later opinion of Abū Ḥanīfa (raḍiyallāhu 'anhu) and it is the opinion of the two [Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad], so punishment is not wajib on him. And the analogical reasoning was that punishment should not be dropped from him, as is the earlier opinion of Abū Ḥanīfa and the opinion of Zufar. By this statement: i.e., by what we said that duress does not conflict with legal capacity nor does it remove legal address so that the mentioned rulings are established. The action of the obedient person, and he is the opposite of the coerced. And the conclusion: just as the action and statement of the obedient person are not invalidated but are considered unless a modifying evidence intervenes, then they are not considered — as when he says to his wife: 'You are divorced', the divorce occurs after the utterance unless a modifier or condition intervenes, then it does not occur. And similarly, if he drinks wine or commits zinā, that is considered, and punishment occurs on him unless a preventer and modifier like those acts occurring in dār al-ḥarb or the presence of a doubt, then it is not considered. So too all actions and statements of the coerced are considered and valid because they issue from intellect and capacity for address, except when a modifier exists, then they are not valid and are not considered. And as it could be objected that since duress is not valid to nullify any of the statements and actions, then in which place does its effect appear? Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a systematic discussion of the types of ikrāh (duress) and their legal effects in Hanafi fiqh. The author explains that actions are categorized under duress into three categories: (1) actions where the prohibition remains despite duress (such as killing an innocent person and zinā for men); (2) actions that become permissible (ibāḥa) under complete duress, like eating carrion, drinking wine, or eating pork, based on Quran 6:119; (3) actions that remain prohibited but are granted as a concession (rukḥṣa) under complete duress, such as uttering words of kufr while keeping faith in the heart, spoiling prayer and fasting, destroying another's property, committing violations of ihrām, and a woman enabling zinā. The key distinction between ibāḥa and rukḥṣa: in ibāḥa the prohibition is lifted entirely, so one is sinful for not doing it if life depends; in rukḥṣa the prohibition remains but the person is excused to avoid greater harm, and if they endure and die, they are martyrs. The passage also differentiates between men and women regarding zinā: lineage is not severed from the woman, so she is granted concession; for men, lineage is lost, so no concession even under complete duress. Under partial duress (e.g., shackling or beating), women get a doubt (shubha) averting the ḥadd punishment, but men do not — except that under complete duress, by istiḥsān, the ḥadd is also averted for men according to the later opinion of Abū Ḥanīfa and the two companions. The underlying principle: ikrāh does not nullify statements or actions per se because the coerced person retains intellect and legal capacity; rather, a modifying evidence (dalīl mughayyir) may change the ruling, similar to how a condition or exception can modify an otherwise effective statement like divorce.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity that can prevent the application of a ḥadd punishment.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that allows departing from an analogical ruling (qiyās) in favour of a more suitable ruling based on a stronger evidence or public interest.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning that extends a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause (ʿillah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a woman under partial duress avoid the ḥadd for zinā, but a man under the same duress does not?
A) Because the man's act is more sinful.
B) Because the child's lineage is not severed from the woman, creating a shubha.
C) Because the woman is physically weaker.
D) Because the man has no choice under partial duress.

Key Takeaway
Under the muftā bihī view, a man under complete duress avoids the ḥadd for zinā by istiḥsān, while a woman avoids it under both complete and partial duress due to the shubha of lineage.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
The Usul of Coercion: Attribution, Consent, and Khul'
Analyzing the effects of coercion on contracts and khul'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi classification of coercion (complete/incomplete) to determine the validity of contracts, divorce, and financial obligations in khul', and distinguish coercion from jest.

Core Idea
Complete coercion shifts attribution of the act to the coercer; incomplete coercion only spoils consent — this determines whether financial obligations stand.

Coercion (ikrah) is a central concept in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, as it affects the validity of contracts and other legal acts. This passage from a classical text explains the dual nature of coercion: when it is complete, it changes who the act is attributed to; when incomplete, it only vitiates consent. Understanding this distinction is key to applying rulings correctly in cases of duress, especially in transactions like khul'.

Guided Path
1
Classify the type of coercion
Determine if coercion is complete (mulji') — involving threat of death or severe harm — or incomplete (ghayr mulji') — lesser threats. Only complete coercion changes attribution; both types spoil consent.
Incomplete coercion retains the coerced person's choice (ikhtiyar) but corrupts consent (rida).
2
Identify the two legal effects
Coercion has two effects: (a) changing the attribution of the act from the coerced to the coercer (only for complete coercion), and (b) spoiling the coerced person's consent (for both types). Note that choice remains in incomplete coercion.
The passage states: 'In spoiling consent: not in spoiling choice.'
3
Apply to different legal acts
Apply the effects to each type of act: (i) contracts dependent on consent (sale, lease) become fasid (corrupt) but can be ratified; (ii) acts not dependent on consent (divorce, manumission) are valid; (iii) acknowledgments (iqrar) are invalid; (iv) khul': divorce occurs, but compensation is not obligatory because consent to both cause and ruling is absent.
The analogy to a minor's khul' reinforces this: divorce occurs but compensation not binding.
4
Distinguish from jest and condition of option
Compare coercion with jest (hazl) and condition of option (shart al-khiyar). Jest and option only affect consent to the ruling, not the cause. In jest khul', compensation may be binding upon later consent (Abu Hanifa) or immediately (Sahibayn). Coercion kills consent to both cause and ruling, so compensation never binds.
The passage explains: 'Jest prevents consent to the ruling without the cause; coercion prevents consent to both.'
5
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on jest khul'
Abu Hanifa holds that in jest khul', compensation depends on the woman's consent to the ruling; the Sahibayn hold it is obligatory upon acceptance of the cause. Trace the rationale: for Abu Hanifa, the cause alone cannot obligate compensation without consent to the ruling; for the Sahibayn, the cause (offer) is like a price in a sale, making compensation binding once accepted.
This ikhtilaf shows how different readings of the role of consent lead to different fiqh outcomes.

باب القياس
أنواع الإكراه
[متى يظهر أثر الإكراه]
وإنما يظهر أثر الإكراه إذا تكامل في تبديل النسبة، وأثره إذا قصر في تفويت في الأمرين يظهر بأن لم يكن ملجئاً الرضاء فيفسد بالإكراه ما يحتمل الفسخ، ويتوقف على الرضاء مثل البيع والإجارة ولا يصح الأقارير كلها؛ لأن صحتها تعتمد على قيام المخبر به وقد قامت دلالة عدمه، وإذا اتصل الإكراه بقبول المال في الخلع فإن الطلاق يقع، والمال لايجب؛ لأن الإكراه يعدم الرضاء بالسبب والحكم جميعاً، والمال ينعدم عند عدم الرضاء، فكان المال لم يوجد، فوقع الطلاق بغير مال كطلاق الصغيرة على مال، إذا تكامل الإكراه بأن يكون ملجئاً يظهر أثره في تبديل النسبة: حتى يكون الفعل منسوباً إلى المكره- بالكسر- بعد ما كان منسوباً إلى المكره بالفتح بشرط عدم المانع عن التبديل، وكون ذلك الفعل صالحاً للتبديل، هذا أول الأمرين. في تفويت الرضاء: لا في تفويت الاختيار، حتى أن من أكره بالقيد أو بالضرب يبقى اختياره ولكن يفسد رضائه، وهذا آخر الأمرين الذين يظهر فيهما أثر الإكراه، ثم تفرّع على تفويت الرضاء فقال: فيفسد إلخ. بالإكراه: سواء كان كاملاً أو قاصراً. مثل البيع والإجارة: فينعقد تلك التصرفات فاسداً، أما الانعقاد فلصدورها من أهلها في محلها، وأما الفساد فلفوات الرضاء الذي هو شرط النفاذ، حتى لو أجاز المكره بعد زوال الإكراه يصح لزوال المفسد. وأما التصرفات التي لا تتوقف على الرضاء كالطلاق والعتاق فتنفذ من المكره كما تنفذ من الطائع، وإنما قلنا سواء كان الإكراه كاملا أو قاصراً؛ لأن في تفويت الرضاء كلاهما سواء. الأقارير كلها : أي سواء كانت بالإكراه الملجئ أو بالقاصر، وسواء كانت بما يحتمل الفسخ أو بما لا يحتمله. دلالة عدمه أي عدم المخبر به، وهي إنه إنما تكلّم به لدفع الضرر عن نفسه لا لوجود المخبر به فلم يترجح جانب صدقه بل يترجح كذبه، فلم يثبت حكمه.
الإكراه سواء كان كاملاً أو قاصرا عطف على قوله: "ولا يصلح". في الخلع بأن أكرهت المرأة على قبول المال في الخلع، فتقبلت ذلك وقد دخل بها الزوج والحكم جميعاً : وهو وجوب المال، ففي هذه الصورة يقع الطلاق؛ لأنه لم يتوقف إلا على القبول وقد وجد، وعدم الرضاء بالعقد لا يخل في وقوعه. نعم يخل بوجوب المال، فلذا لايجب عليها المال، وإليه أشار بقوله : والمال إلخ. عدم الرضاء لأن الرضاء شرط له وفوات الشرط يوجب فوات المشروط. كطلاق الصغيرة على مال: فإنه إذا طلق الصغيرة زوجها على مال يتوقف طلاقها على قبولها، فإذا قبلت يقع الطلاق لوجود القبول، ولا يلزم عليها المال لبطلان التزامها، فكذا هنا. ولما كان يرد أن الإكراه إذا كان ملحقا بالهزل= 

باب القياس
أنواع الإكراه بخلاف الهزل؛ لأنه يمنع الرضاء بالحكم دون السبب، فكان كشرط الخيار على ما مرّ، وإذا اتصل الإكراه الكامل بما يصلح أن يكون الفاعل فيه آلة لغيره مثل إتلاف النفس =
أي بأفعال في الطلاق وجب أن لا ينفصل المال عن الطلاق فيما أكرهت على الخلع كما لا ينفصل في الهزل بالخلع بالاتفاق؛ لأن عند أبي حنيفة له إذا لم تلتزم المرأة المال في الهزل بالخلع ولا تقبله لا يقع الطلاق ولا يلزم المال، وعندهما يقع الطلاق ويلزم المال من غير توقف على الرضاء، والحال أن في الخلع بالإكراه انفصل المال عن الطلاق حيث وقع الطلاق بغير مال، أشار إلى دفعه بالفرق بين الخلع بالهزل والخلع بالإكراه حيث قال بخلاف الهزل إلخ: لأن الهازل إنما يتكلم باختياره بغير إكراه عليه، فالسبب مرضي عنده.
وحاصل الفرق أن الهزل إنما يمنع اختيار الحكم والرضاء به، وأما السبب فالرضاء والاختيار ثابت فيه، والإكراه يمنع الرضاء بالسبب والحكم جميعاً. وإذا تقرّر هذا فاعلم أن أبا حنيفة لله قال: إنه قد تحقق في الهزل الرضاء بالسبب فقط يصح التزام المال موقوفاً على تمام الرضاء لوجود السبب، لكن توقف ثبوته على رضاءها بحكم العقد؛ إذ المال لا يثبت بدون الرضاء بالحكم، وتوقف وقوع الطلاق على ثبوت المال؛ لأن الطلاق في الخلع يقابل المال، فإذا التزمت المال ورضت بحكم العقد يقع الطلاق ويلزم المال، وإلا فلايقع الطلاق ولا يلزم. كشرط الخيار من جانبها بأن اختلعت على أن يكون لها الخيار إلى ثلاثة أيام فإن هذا شرط الخيار إنما يمنع رضائها بالحكم دون السبب، حتى إذا التزمت المال يقع الطلاق ويلزم المال وإلا فلا.
على ما مر بيانه في بحث الهزل، وهذا بخلاف الإكراه حيث ينعدم السبب والحكم جميعاً، فإذا لم ترض بالسبب في الإكراه لم يصح التزام المال بعقد الخلع فيقع الطلاق لقبولها عقد الخلع ولا يلزم المال لعدم الرضاء به، ولا يتوقف أحدهما على الآخر فافترقا، فهذا الفرق على قول أبي حنيفة لله، وأما على قولهما فهو أن ما يدخل على الحكم دون السبب أي ما لا يبطل السبب ولكن يعدم الحكم كالهزل وشرط الخيار لايؤثر في بدل الخلع بالمنع، أي لايمنع وجوب المال أصلاً؛ لأنه لما لم يؤثر في أحد الحكمين وهو الطلاق بالمنع لعدم توقفه على الرضاء لم يؤثر في الآخر أيضاً، وهو لزوم المال؛ لأنه تابع للطلاق، فيلزم المال بلزومه، فيقع الطلاق ويلزم المال غير توقف على الرضاء، وأما ما يدخل على السبب كالإكراه ويؤثر بالمنع في المال دون الطلاق؛ لأن المال في الخلع لا يجب إلا بالذكر فيه كالثمن في البيع، فلا بد لصحته من الإيجاب كثبوت الثمن، والداخل على السبب يمنع الإيجاب، فلم يثبت به المال فيصير كأن المال لم يكن في العقد، فيقع الطلاق بغير مال في الإكراه فافترقا، وظهر بما قررنا أن قوله فكان كشرط الخيار إشارة لطيفة إلى الفرق بين الهزل والإكراه في الخلع على المذهبين. ولما فرغ من بيان تأثير الإكراه القاصر في تفويت الرضاء الواقع في الأقوال شرع في تأثير الإكراه الكامل في تبديل النسبة الواقع في الأفعال غالباً فيه: أي في تلك الأفعال، والضمير يرجع إلى "ما".
Chapter of Qiyas. Types of coercion. [When does the effect of coercion appear?] The effect of coercion appears when it is complete in changing attribution, and its effect when it falls short in spoiling consent. It appears in two matters: if it is not compelling, it spoils consent, so whatever can be rescinded and depends on consent is corrupted by coercion, such as sale and lease. And all acknowledgments are not valid; because their validity is based on the existence of the reported matter, and there is evidence of its non-existence. And if coercion is connected to the acceptance of compensation in khul', then the divorce occurs, but the compensation is not obligatory; because coercion nullifies consent in both the cause and the ruling, and compensation is nullified when consent is absent, so it is as if the compensation did not exist, so the divorce occurs without compensation, like the divorce of a minor girl upon compensation. When coercion is complete by being compelling, its effect appears in changing attribution: so that the act is attributed to the coercer (with kasr) after it was attributed to the coerced (with fath), provided there is no impediment to the change, and that act is suitable for change. This is the first of the two matters. In spoiling consent: not in spoiling choice, so that someone coerced by shackles or beating retains his choice but his consent is corrupted. This is the last of the two matters in which the effect of coercion appears. Then he branched from spoiling consent and said: 'so it is corrupted' etc. By coercion: whether complete or incomplete. Such as sale and lease: those transactions occur as corrupt (fasid). As for occurring, it is because they emanate from their people in their place; as for corruption, it is due to the loss of consent, which is a condition for enforceability. So if the coerced person ratifies after coercion ends, it becomes valid due to the removal of the corrupting factor. As for transactions that do not depend on consent, such as divorce and manumission, they are executed from the coerced person just as they are executed from the willing person. We said 'whether coercion is complete or incomplete' because in spoiling consent both are equal. All acknowledgments: whether by compelling coercion or incomplete, and whether they concern what can be rescinded or not. 'Evidence of its non-existence' i.e. non-existence of the reported matter, meaning that he only spoke to repel harm from himself, not due to the existence of the reported matter, so his truthfulness is not preponderant; rather his falsehood is preponderant, so its ruling is not established. 'Coercion whether complete or incomplete' is an apposition to his statement 'does not become valid'. In khul': that the woman is coerced to accept the compensation in khul', so she accepts it and the husband has consummated the marriage, and the ruling (obligation of compensation) is [considered] together. So in this case, the divorce occurs because it does not depend except on acceptance and it has occurred, and lack of consent to the contract does not impair its occurrence. Yes, it impairs the obligation of compensation, so the compensation is not obligatory on her. This is what he indicated by his statement 'and the compensation' etc. Lack of consent because consent is a condition for it, and loss of the condition necessitates loss of the conditioned. Like the divorce of a minor girl upon compensation: if her husband divorces her upon compensation, her divorce depends on her acceptance; so if she accepts, the divorce occurs due to the existence of acceptance, but the compensation is not binding on her due to the nullity of her undertaking. So likewise here. And since it could be objected that coercion when it is akin to jest... Chapter of Qiyas. Types of coercion: contrary to jest; because jest prevents consent to the ruling without the cause, so it is like the condition of option as discussed. And when complete coercion is connected to something in which the doer can be a tool for another, such as destroying one's own life by acts in divorce, then the compensation must not be separated from divorce in what the woman is coerced to khul', just as it is not separated in jest in khul' by agreement. Because according to Abu Hanifa, if the woman does not undertake the compensation in jest khul' and does not accept it, then neither the divorce occurs nor is the compensation obligatory. According to them (the two companions), the divorce occurs and the compensation is obligatory without depending on consent. Yet in khul' with coercion, the compensation is separated from divorce, as the divorce occurs without compensation. He indicated the rebuttal by the difference between khul' in jest and khul' with coercion, saying: 'contrary to jest' etc. Because the jester speaks with his choice without coercion, so the cause is consented to by him. The gist of the difference: jest prevents choice of the ruling and consent to it, but as for the cause, consent and choice are established in it; whereas coercion prevents consent to both the cause and the ruling. When this is settled, know that according to Abu Hanifa, indeed in jest the consent to the cause alone is realized, so the undertaking of compensation is valid subject to complete consent due to the existence of the cause, but its establishment depends on her consent to the ruling of the contract; because compensation is not established without consent to the ruling, and the occurrence of the divorce depends on the establishment of the compensation; because the divorce in khul' corresponds to the compensation. So if she undertakes the compensation and consents to the ruling of the contract, the divorce occurs and the compensation is obligatory; otherwise, neither the divorce occurs nor the compensation. Like the condition of option from her side: if she makes khul' on condition that she has the option for three days, then this condition of option prevents her consent to the ruling without the cause, so if she undertakes the compensation, the divorce occurs and the compensation is obligatory; otherwise, not. As discussed in the section on jest. And this is different from coercion, where both the cause and the ruling are nullified. So if she does not consent to the cause in coercion, the undertaking of compensation in the khul' contract is not valid, so the divorce occurs due to her acceptance of the khul' contract, but the compensation is not obligatory due to lack of consent to it. And neither depends on the other. So they differ. This is the difference according to Abu Hanifa. As for according to them (the two companions), that which enters upon the ruling without the cause (i.e., it does not nullify the cause but nullifies the ruling), like jest and the condition of option, does not affect the substitute in khul' by prevention, i.e., it does not prevent the obligation of compensation at all; because since it does not affect one of the two rulings (divorce) by prevention (since it does not depend on consent), it also does not affect the other, which is the obligation of compensation; because it is subsidiary to divorce, so the compensation becomes obligatory by its obligation; so the divorce occurs and compensation is obligatory without dependence on consent. As for that which enters upon the cause (like coercion) and affects by preventing compensation without preventing divorce, because compensation in khul' is only obligatory by mention in it, like the price in sale, so its validity requires offer like the establishment of price; and that which enters upon the cause prevents the offer, so compensation is not established by it, so it becomes as if compensation was not in the contract, so the divorce occurs without compensation in coercion. So they differ. And it is clear from what we have stated that his statement 'so it is like the condition of option' is a subtle indication of the difference between jest and coercion in khul' according to both schools. When he finished explaining the effect of incomplete coercion in spoiling consent occurring in statements, he began on the effect of complete coercion in changing attribution occurring in actions mostly. 'In it' i.e., in those actions; the pronoun refers to 'what'.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the effects of coercion (ikrah) on various legal acts, particularly contracts and divorce. The author explains that coercion has two main effects: (1) changing the attribution of the act (from the coerced to the coercer) when coercion is complete (mulji'), and (2) spoiling consent (tafwit al-rida) when coercion is incomplete (ghayr mulji'). The passage then applies these principles to different transactions. For contracts that depend on consent and are susceptible to rescission (like sale and lease), coercion renders them corrupt (fasid) but not void, because the act emanates from a capable person but lacks the condition of consent. However, ratification after coercion ends can validate the contract. For acts that do not require consent (like divorce and manumission), coercion does not prevent their effectiveness; they are executed as if from a willing person. Acknowledgments (iqrar) are not valid because the basis for their validity (truthfulness) is absent; the coerced person speaks only to avoid harm, so his statement is considered false. The passage then discusses a specific scenario: khul' (divorce by redemption) where the woman is coerced to accept compensation. Here, the divorce occurs because acceptance is present, but the compensation is not obligatory because consent is lacking in both the cause (the khul' contract) and the ruling (the obligation to pay). This is compared to the case of a minor girl who accepts compensation in khul' from her guardian: she lacks legal capacity, so the divorce occurs but the compensation is not binding. The author then distinguishes coercion from jest (hazl) and the condition of option (shart al-khiyar). In jest, the jester consents to the cause but not the ruling, so the compensation is binding subject to later consent. In coercion, both cause and ruling lack consent, so the compensation is not binding but the divorce still occurs because it does not require consent. The passage presents two positions: Abu Hanifa's view that compensation in jest khul' depends on the woman's consent to the ruling, and the view of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that compensation is obligatory without such consent. The discussion shows how the usul principle of 'coercion affects the cause' leads to different fiqh rulings. Finally, the author notes that the previous section dealt with incomplete coercion in statements, and now he turns to complete coercion in actions.

Key Terms
الإكراه الملجئ (al-ikrāh al-mulji'): Compelling coercion that removes choice and leaves no alternative but to comply, typically involving threat of death or severe harm.
الخلع (al-khul‘): Divorce by redemption where the wife gives compensation to the husband to obtain release from the marriage.
تبديل النسبة (tabdīl al-nisba): Changing the attribution of an act from the coerced person to the coercer, so that the coerced person is considered a mere tool.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a woman is coerced into accepting compensation in khul', what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Both the divorce and the compensation are effective and binding.
B) The divorce occurs but the compensation is not obligatory because consent is lacking in both cause and ruling.
C) The divorce does not occur because coercion invalidates the entire contract.
D) The compensation is obligatory but the divorce is suspended until the coercion ends.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, coercion must be classified as complete or incomplete; only complete coercion shifts attribution, but both types vitiate consent, affecting the validity of financial obligations in contracts like khul'.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
6 min
Hazl vs Ikrah in Khul': Cause and Ruling
Analysing the distinction between hazl and ikrah in khul' regarding property and divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hazl and ikrah in khul' regarding their effects on the cause and ruling, and apply this distinction to determine the obligation of mal and occurrence of talaq.

Core Idea
Hazl affects only the ruling, not the cause; ikrah affects both — this determines whether the khul' divorce obligates property.

This passage explores a subtle but critical distinction in Hanafi usul: how jest (hazl) and coercion (ikrah) differently affect the validity of a khul' divorce. The dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn hinges on whether the cause (the utterance) or the ruling (the legal effect) is impaired. Understanding this difference is key to applying the principle that what affects only the ruling does not invalidate the entire transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two states
First, recognize that hazl (jest) and ikrah (coercion) are two different circumstances affecting a khul' divorce. In hazl, the person speaks voluntarily but without serious intent. In ikrah, the person is compelled to speak under threat.
The passage clarifies that hazl is like a condition of option (khiyar) in that it prevents consent to the ruling but not the cause.
2
Distinguish effect on cause vs. ruling
In Hanafi usul, every valid act has a cause (sabab) and a ruling (hukm). Hazl prevents consent to the ruling only, leaving the cause intact. Ikrah prevents consent to both cause and ruling.
The author says: 'الهزل إنما يمنع اختيار الحكم والرضاء به، وأما السبب فالرضاء والاختيار ثابت فيه' (Jest only prevents choice of the ruling and consent to it, but the cause is willingly accepted).
3
Apply to khul': obligation of mal and occurrence of talaq
Based on the above, in hazl, the cause (the speech of khul') is valid, so the wife's undertaking of mal is valid but suspended on her consent to the ruling. If she consents, talaq occurs and mal is binding. In ikrah, the cause is invalid, so her undertaking of mal is void; talaq occurs (because she accepted the contract) but mal is not binding.
The passage contrasts this with the Sahibayn's view that in hazl, talaq and mal are immediate without waiting for consent.
4
Note the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn
The Sahibayn argue that what affects only the ruling (like hazl) does not prevent the obligation of mal, because mal is subsidiary to talaq and talaq occurs regardless of consent. Ikrah, which affects the cause, prevents the declaration of mal, similar to a sale where the price is not mentioned.
The practical difference: According to Abu Hanifa, a coerced wife gets a divorce without paying; according to Sahibayn, she must pay.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القياس
أنواع الإكراه بخلاف الهزل؛ لأنه يمنع الرضاء بالحكم دون السبب، فكان كشرط الخيار على ما مرّ.
المال
وإذا اتصل الإكراه الكامل بما يصلح أن يكون الفاعل فيه آلة لغيره مثل إتلاف النفس في الطلاق وجب أن لا ينفصل المال عن الطلاق فيما أكرهت على الخلع كما لا ينفصل في الهزل بالخلع بالاتفاق؛ لأن عند أبي حنيفة له إذا لم تلتزم المرأة المال في الهزل بالخلع ولا تقبله لا يقع الطلاق ولا يلزم المال، وعندهما يقع الطلاق ويلزم المال من غير توقف على الرضاء، والحال أن في الخلع بالإكراه انفصل المال عن الطلاق حيث وقع الطلاق بغير مال، أشار إلى دفعه بالفرق بين الخلع بالهزل والخلع بالإكراه حيث قال بخلاف الهزل إلخ: لأن الهازل إنما يتكلم باختياره بغير إكراه عليه، فالسبب مرضي عنده.
وحاصل الفرق أن الهزل إنما يمنع اختيار الحكم والرضاء به، وأما السبب فالرضاء والاختيار ثابت فيه، والإكراه يمنع الرضاء بالسبب والحكم جميعاً. وإذا تقرّر هذا فاعلم أن أبا حنيفة لله قال: إنه قد تحقق في الهزل الرضاء بالسبب فقط يصح التزام المال موقوفاً على تمام الرضاء لوجود السبب، لكن توقف ثبوته على رضاءها بحكم العقد؛ إذ المال لا يثبت بدون الرضاء بالحكم، وتوقف وقوع الطلاق على ثبوت المال؛ لأن الطلاق في الخلع يقابل المال، فإذا التزمت المال ورضيت بحكم العقد يقع الطلاق ويلزم المال، وإلا فلا يقع الطلاق ولا يلزم. كشرط الخيار من جانبها بأن اختلعت على أن يكون لها الخيار إلى ثلاثة أيام فإن هذا شرط الخيار إنما يمنع رضائها بالحكم دون السبب، حتى إذا التزمت المال يقع الطلاق ويلزم المال وإلا فلا.
على ما مر بيانه في بحث الهزل، وهذا بخلاف الإكراه حيث ينعدم السبب والحكم جميعاً، فإذا لم ترض بالسبب في الإكراه لم يصح التزام المال بعقد الخلع فيقع الطلاق لقبولها عقد الخلع ولا يلزم المال لعدم الرضاء به، ولا يتوقف أحدهما على الآخر فافترقا، فهذا الفرق على قول أبي حنيفة لله، وأما على قولهما فهو أن ما يدخل على الحكم دون السبب أي ما لا يبطل السبب ولكن يعدم الحكم كالهزل وشرط الخيار لا يؤثر في بدل الخلع بالمنع، أي لا يمنع وجوب المال أصلاً؛ لأنه لما لم يؤثر في أحد الحكمين وهو الطلاق بالمنع لعدم توقفه على الرضاء لم يؤثر في الآخر أيضاً، وهو لزوم المال؛ لأنه تابع للطلاق، فيلزم المال بلزومه، فيقع الطلاق ويلزم المال غير توقف على الرضاء، وأما ما يدخل على السبب كالإكراه ويؤثر بالمنع في المال دون الطلاق؛ لأن المال في الخلع لا يجب إلا بالذكر فيه كالثمن في البيع، فلا بد لصحته من الإيجاب كثبوت الثمن، والداخل على السبب يمنع الإيجاب، فلم يثبت به المال فيصير كأن المال لم يكن في العقد، فيقع الطلاق بغير مال في الإكراه فافترقا، وظهر مما قررنا أن قوله فكان كشرط الخيار إشارة لطيفة إلى الفرق بين الهزل والإكراه في الخلع على المذهبين. ولما فرغ من بيان تأثير الإكراه القاصر في تفويت الرضاء الواقع في الأقوال شرع في تأثير الإكراه الكامل في تبديل النسبة الواقع في الأفعال غالباً فيه: أي في تلك الأفعال، والضمير يرجع إلى "ما".
Chapter of Qiyas. Types of coercion: contrary to jest because jest prevents consent to the ruling without the cause, so it is like a condition of option as previously. Property: And when complete coercion is connected to something in which the doer can be a tool for another, like destroying life in divorce, it is necessary that property not be separated from divorce in the case where she is coerced into khul', just as it is not separated in jest by khul' by agreement. Because according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, if the woman does not undertake the property in jest by khul' and does not accept it, divorce does not occur and the property is not binding. And according to them, divorce occurs and the property is binding without waiting for consent, while in khul' by coercion, the property is separated from divorce as divorce occurred without property. He indicated its rebuttal by the difference between khul' by jest and khul' by coercion, when he said 'contrary to jest etc.': because the jester speaks by his choice without coercion, so the cause is willingly accepted. The gist of the difference: jest only prevents choice of the ruling and consent to it, but as for the cause, consent and choice are established in it; coercion prevents consent to both cause and ruling. When this is established, know that Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said: That in jest, the consent to the cause alone is realized, so undertaking property is valid but suspended on complete consent because of the existence of the cause, but its establishment depends on her consent to the ruling of the contract, since property is not established without consent to the ruling; and the occurrence of divorce depends on the establishment of the property because divorce in khul' is in exchange for property. So if she undertakes the property and consents to the ruling of the contract, divorce occurs and property is binding; otherwise, divorce does not occur and property is not binding. Like a condition of option on her part, e.g., she enters khul' on condition that she has option for three days: that condition of option only prevents her consent to the ruling, not the cause, so if she undertakes the property, divorce occurs and property is binding; otherwise not. According to what was explained previously in the discussion of jest. This is contrary to coercion where both cause and ruling are non-existent. So if she does not consent to the cause in coercion, her undertaking property by the khul' contract is not valid; divorce occurs because she accepted the khul' contract, but property is not binding due to lack of consent, and neither depends on the other; thus they differ. This difference is according to Abu Hanifa's saying. As for their saying: That which enters upon the ruling without the cause — i.e., that which does not nullify the cause but eliminates the ruling, like jest and condition of option — does not affect the consideration in khul' by prevention, i.e., it does not prevent the obligation of property at all; because since it did not affect one of the two rulings, i.e., divorce, by prevention due to non-dependence on consent, it also did not affect the other, i.e., bindingness of property, because it is subsidiary to divorce; so property becomes binding by its bindingness, so divorce occurs and property becomes binding without waiting for consent. As for that which enters upon the cause, like coercion, and affects by prevention in the property without divorce — because property in khul' is only obligatory by its mention, like the price in sale, so its validity requires a declaration like the establishment of price, and that which enters upon the cause prevents the declaration, so property is not established by it; thus it becomes as if property never existed in the contract, so divorce occurs without property in coercion; thus they differ. And it became apparent from what we have established that his saying 'so it is like a condition of option' is a subtle indication of the difference between jest and coercion in khul' according to both schools. And when he finished explaining the effect of incomplete coercion in losing consent occurring in statements, he began explaining the effect of complete coercion in changing attribution occurring in actions mostly in them, i.e., in those actions; the pronoun refers to 'what'.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is from a commentary on a matn about qiyas, specifically discussing types of coercion (ikrah) and their distinction from jest (hazl) in the context of khul' (divorce initiated by the wife with compensation). The author first states a general rule: contrary to jest, which prevents consent to the ruling but not to the cause, making it like a condition of option. The text then focuses on the issue of property (mal) in khul' under coercion. It presents the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). According to Abu Hanifa, in jest, if the wife does not accept the property, no divorce occurs and no property is owed; but according to the Sahibayn, divorce occurs and property is obligatory regardless. In coercion, the situation differs: Abu Hanifa holds that since both cause and ruling are absent, if she does not consent to the cause, her undertaking of property is invalid, so divorce occurs but property is not binding. The Sahibayn argue that anything that affects only the ruling (like jest) does not prevent property obligation; but coercion affects the cause, so it prevents the obligation of property because property is like the price in a sale and requires a valid declaration. The commentary elaborates the rationale for each position, emphasizing that in jest the cause (the speech) is valid but the ruling is suspended, while in coercion the cause itself is invalidated. The passage concludes by noting that the distinction is subtle and that the commentator now moves from incomplete coercion (which affects consent in statements) to complete coercion (which changes attribution in actions). The legal dispute shows the importance of distinguishing between the cause and the ruling in determining the effects of intention and consent in Islamic legal transactions.

Key Terms
هزل (hazl): Jest or joking speech that lacks serious intent; it affects the ruling but not the cause.
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion that eliminates consent in both cause and ruling; it nullifies the validity of the act depending on its type.
خلع (khul'): Divorce initiated by the wife in exchange for compensation (usually property).
مال (mal): Property or compensation given by the wife in khul' in exchange for divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between hazl and ikrah in terms of their effect on khul'?
A) Hazl makes the khul' invalid; ikrah makes it valid.
B) Hazl prevents consent to both cause and ruling; ikrah prevents only the ruling.
C) Hazl prevents consent to the ruling only; ikrah prevents consent to both cause and ruling.
D) Both have the same effect: they prevent the cause but not the ruling.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between hazl and ikrah in Hanafi usul is that hazl affects only the ruling, while ikrah affects both the cause and the ruling, leading to different rulings on the obligation of property in khul'.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
10 min
Complete Coercion and Attribution of Actions
Explaining the effect of complete coercion on choice and attribution of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that complete coercion vitiates choice and distinguish actions that can be attributed to the coercer from those that cannot.

Core Idea
Complete coercion makes the coerced a tool; but some acts (eating, speech) remain attributed to the coerced.

This passage from a Hanafi usul work explores the legal effect of complete coercion (ikrah kamil). The central principle is that coercion corrupts choice, so the coerced is treated as an instrument of the coercer. However, this rule has limits: certain inherently personal acts cannot be transferred. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly attributing liability in coerced actions.

Guided Path
1
Define complete coercion and its effect on choice
Complete coercion (ikrah kamil) nullifies free choice. The coerced (mukrah) becomes like a tool (ala) for the coercer (mukrih). The act is thus attributed to the coercer, and the coercer bears the legal consequences.
This is the baseline rule.
2
Explain the tool analogy and its scope
The coerced is likened to a sword or staff—a mere instrument. This applies only to acts where the coerced can physically serve as a tool, such as killing or destroying property, where the coercer manipulates the coerced's body.
The tool analogy fails for acts that require the coerced's personal involvement.
3
Identify acts that do not admit tool status
Acts like eating, sexual intercourse, and all forms of speech (e.g., divorce, emancipation) are inherently personal. One cannot eat with another's mouth or speak with another's tongue. Hence, the act remains attributed to the coerced, not the coercer.
These are called 'ma la yahtamil'—what does not admit being a tool.
4
Apply the rule to examples
If coerced to eat during fasting, the fast is broken for the eater. If coerced to eat another's property, the eater is liable for guarantee (detailed rules based on benefit). For coercion into fornication, no hadd on either, but the coerced must pay the dower (uqr). Similarly, coerced divorce or emancipation takes effect on the coerced.
These examples illustrate the personal nature of the act overriding the tool analogy.
5
Analyze the third category and istihsan
Some acts (e.g., a muhrim coerced to kill game) are ambiguous: the act itself (killing) could be performed through a tool, but the locus (ihram) is personal. Analogy (qiyas) would absolve the coerced, but istihsan (juristic preference) holds the muhrim liable because the offense is against his own ihram and cannot be transferred to the coercer.
This shows how istihsan overrides qiyas when the tool analogy leads to an impossibility.

للمحمول، ولو أكره على أكل طعام غيره يجب الضمان على الحامل سواء كان جائعاً أو لا. وكذا لو أكره على الزنا لا يجب به الحدّ على واحد منهما، ويجب به العقر على المحمول، ولا يرجع به على الحامل؛ لأن منفعة الوطي حصلت له هذا في الأفعال وكذا الحكم في الأقوال التي لاتنفسخ ولا تتوقف على الرضاء كالطلاق والعتاق والنكاح والتدبير والعفو عن دم العمد والنذر واليمين، فلو أكره بها أحد وتكلّم بها تنفذ على المكره إلى نفسه يصلح أن يكون الفاعل فيه آلة لغيره ولكن بالنظر إلى المحل لا يصلح آلة فيه بمعنى أنه لو جعل آلة فيه يتبدل المحلّ المكره عليه؛ إذ في صورة كون المكره آلة محل الإكراه هو إحرام المكره بالكسر كما سيأتي تقريره، وفي صورة نسبة الفعل إلى المكره بالفتح محله إحرامه لا إحرام المكره بالكسر ، وبينهما مغائرة صورةً، وهذا معنى قوله "إلا أن المحل إلخ". وكان في هذه الجملة بيان للتغائر المذكور بقوله "إلا أن المحل إلخ". آلة للمكره بالكسر يعني لو جعل المكره آلة للمكره يلزم تبدّل المحل حيث يصير محل الإكراه إحرام المكره بالكسر، وحينئذ يلزم بطلان الإكراه وعود الفعل إلى المباشر، فلا يقصر الفعل على المباشر ابتداء. إكراه المحرم إلخ: أي إذا أكره إنسان محرماً على قتل صيد فقتله، فالقياس أن لايجب شيء لا على الأمر إذا كان حلالاً؛ لأنه قتله بنفسه لا يلزمه شيء، فكذا لو أكره عليه غيره، ولا على المأمور؛ لأنه بالإلجاء التام صلح أن يكون آلة للأمر فيه فانعدم الفعل منه، ولكن الاستحسان أن ذلك إلخ. يقتصر إلخ: لا أن يجعل آلة له؛ لأنه لو جعل آلة له لبطل الإكراه واللازم باطل؛ إذ الفرض أنه مكره، أمــــا الملازمة فهي أن في جعله آلة يلزم نقل محل الإكراه أو الجناية إلى المكره بالكسر في ذلك: أي في إيقاع الجناية على إحرامه. آلة: لغيره في ذلك كما قلتم لانتقل فعله، وهو جناية على إحرام نفسه إلى غيره وهو المكــره بالكسر؛ لأن الآلة لاتصلح أن ينسب إليها الفعل، فيكون غيره جانيا على إحرامه، وذلك غير ممكن؛ لأن الإنسان
Chapter of Qiyas. Types of Coercion. Complete coercion corrupts choice... and the property? attributes the action to the coercer, and its ruling is incumbent upon him because complete coercion corrupts choice. And the corrupt [choice] in opposition to the sound is like non-existent, so the coerced becomes like one devoid of choice, a tool for the coercer in what admits that. As for what does not admit it, its attribution to the coercer is not sound, so it does not occur... opposition in deserving the ruling, so it remains attributed to the corrupt choice. That is like eating, sexual intercourse, and all sayings, for it is inconceivable that a human eats with another's mouth or speaks... and its ruling is incumbent upon him, and the coerced is removed from the picture and attached to the tool due to the corruption of his choice by complete coercion; for he is compelled in this act, and man is disposed to love life. So when he is threatened with death by the coercer saying: kill so-and-so and destroy his property or else I will kill you, and he seeks for himself an escape from destruction by committing murder or destroying property even if it is forbidden, his choice becomes corrupted in this respect, and to that the author referred by saying 'corrupts choice' as we said. And because it might be thought that the coerced has choice, albeit corrupt, so the act could be attributed to him and not attach to the tool, he refuted it by saying: 'And the corrupt' i.e., the corrupt choice obtained by the coerced is like non-existent, not regarded, a tool for the coercer like the sword and staff, and the tool is not fit for the act to be attributed to it and its ruling to be incumbent upon it, so inevitably the act is attributed to the coercer and his ruling of blood-money and retaliation is incumbent by consensus. And this is only possible in what does not admit: i.e., that the doer be a tool for another, which is some acts and all sayings... to the coercer, rather it is attributed to the coerced in ruling because they do not conflict here. The corrupt choice is the choice of the coerced because it is the fit one then, no other, and that is what does not admit that the doer be a tool for another. And all sayings like divorce, emancipation, and marriage. That a human eats etc.: evidence for the first which is eating. As for evidence for intercourse, it is that intercourse with another's tool is impossible, and likewise it is not conceivable. And that a human speaks with another's tongue: this is evidence for all sayings. So in this section, if a man is coerced into direct commission, its ruling is confined to the direct doer and the act is attributed to him; so if a person is coerced to eat during fasting, the fasting of the eater specifically is broken; likewise if coerced to eat another's property, the eater specifically sins, but there is disagreement about the obligation of guarantee. It is mentioned in Sharh al-Tahawi and al-Khulasa: if he is coerced to eat another's property, the guarantee is on the coerced, not the coercer, because the benefit of eating accrued to the eater, so the guarantee is on him. And in al-Muhit there is detail: if he is coerced to eat his own food, then if he is hungry he does not have recourse against the coercer because the benefit accrued to the coerced; if he is full, he has recourse because the benefit did not accrue. Likewise if the act itself is such that the doer can be considered a tool for another, except that with regard to the essence of the act, the locus is other than what the destruction meets in form, and that would change by making him a tool, such as coercion of the one in ihram to kill game: that is confined to the doer because the coercer only compelled him to commit aggression against his own ihram, and in that he is not fit as a tool for another... for the coerced, and if coerced to eat another's property, the guarantee is on the coercer whether hungry or not. Likewise if coerced to commit fornication, the hadd penalty is not obligatory on either, and the dower is obligatory on the coerced, and he does not have recourse against the coercer because the benefit of intercourse accrued to him. This applies to acts, and likewise the ruling for sayings that are not revocable and do not depend on consent, such as divorce, emancipation, marriage, tadbir, pardon for intentional killing, vows, and oaths. So if someone is coerced into any of these and utters them, they take effect on the coerced and are not nullified by coercion. And since there is a category of acts (every act in which the coerced is fit as a tool for the coercer in form but not in locus, i.e., he is fit as a tool with regard to the essence of the act but not fit as a tool with regard to its locus) which is ambiguous whether it belongs to what does not admit or what admits, the author clarified that by his saying: 'Likewise' i.e., like what does not admit that the doer be a tool for another, but is attributed to the direct doer. The locus: i.e., the locus of the coerced act. 'Destruction in form': i.e., the act in itself is fit for the doer to be a tool for another, but with regard to the locus it is not fit as a tool, meaning that if he were made a tool, the locus of the coerced act would change; for in the form of the coerced being a tool, the locus of coercion is the ihram of the coercer as will be settled, and in the form of attribution of the act to the coerced, its locus is his own ihram, not the ihram of the coercer, and there is a difference in form between them. This is the meaning of his saying 'except that the locus etc.' And in this sentence there is an explanation of the aforementioned difference by his saying 'except that the locus etc.' A tool for the coercer means if the coerced is made a tool for the coercer, it necessitates a change of locus, where the locus of coercion becomes the ihram of the coercer, and then the corruption of coercion and return of the act to the direct doer follows, so the act is not confined to the direct doer initially. Coercion of the muhrim etc.: i.e., if a person coerces a muhrim to kill game and he kills it, the analogy is that nothing is obligatory either on the orderer if he is non-muhrim because if he killed it himself nothing would be obligatory, so too if he coerced another, nor on the ordered because by complete compulsion he is fit to be a tool for the orderer in it, so the act is negated from him. But istihsan is that... etc. It is confined etc.: i.e., not to make him a tool; for if made a tool, coercion would be invalidated, and the necessary is false; since it is assumed that he is coerced. As for the entailment, it is that in making him a tool, it necessitates transferring the locus of coercion or the offense to the coercer in that: i.e., in committing the offense against his ihram. A tool for another in that as you said, his act would not transfer, and it is an offense against his own ihram to another, i.e., the coercer; because the tool is not fit for the act to be attributed to it, so someone else would be committing aggression against his ihram, and that is impossible because a human...
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Qiyas discusses the types of coercion (ikrah) and their effect on the attribution of actions. The author explains that complete coercion (ikrah kamil) corrupts choice, making the coerced person like a tool (ala) for the coercer. However, this rule applies only to actions that can be attributed to the coercer, i.e., those where the coerced can physically serve as a mere instrument. For acts like eating, sexual intercourse, and speech, it is inconceivable for the coerced to be a tool because the act essentially requires the direct involvement of the individual. In such cases, the ruling falls on the coerced (the direct doer), not the coercer. The text then provides detailed examples: if coerced to eat during fasting, the fast of the eater breaks; if coerced to eat another's property, the eater sins, and there is a difference of opinion on guarantee. Citing sources like Sharh al-Tahawi, al-Khulasa, and al-Muhit, the author notes that guarantee depends on benefit received. For coercion into fornication, no hadd punishment is imposed on either party, but the coerced must pay the dower (uqr). For pronouncements like divorce, emancipation, or marriage, coercion does not nullify them because these acts do not depend on consent. Finally, a third category is discussed: acts where the coerced can be a tool in form but not in locus, such as a muhrim coerced to kill game. Here, analogy would suggest no liability, but istihsan (juristic preference) holds the muhrim responsible because the offense is against his own ihram, and making him a tool would transfer the locus to the coercer, which is impossible. The passage is a commentary expanding on a matn, explaining the legal reasoning behind these rules.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Compulsion or coercion that eliminates free choice.
مكره (بالفتح) (mukrah (bi al-fath)): The coerced person (the one upon whom coercion is applied).
مكره (بالكسر) (mukrih (bi al-kasr)): The coercer (the one who applies coercion).
آلة (ala): A tool or instrument; the coerced is analogized to a tool when choice is vitiated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of complete coercion on acts like eating and speech?
A) The act is attributed to the coercer, and the coercer bears all liability.
B) The act remains attributed to the coerced, who bears liability.
C) Both the coercer and coerced are equally liable.
D) No liability arises because coercion eliminates responsibility.

Key Takeaway
Complete coercion vitiates choice but does not transfer liability for inherently personal acts; the coerced remains responsible for eating, speech, and similar actions.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement

The Coerced as a Tool (Âlah): Hanafi Classification of Coerced Acts
Expansion on coercion categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify coerced acts into three categories under Hanafi usul and determine the attribution of worldly liability versus sin using the âlah rule.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a coerced person can be a tool for worldly consequences but never for sin.

The Hanafi jurists developed a sophisticated framework for analyzing coercion (ikrāh), distinguishing between acts where the coerced is a mere instrument (ālah) and those where he remains fully responsible. This passage from a sharh expands on this concept, categorizing actions and statements based on whether the coerced can be considered a tool for the coercer. The key insight is that while the coercer assumes liability for the harmful effects of the act, sin (ithm) always remains with the direct doer because intention (qaṣd) is personal and non-transferable. By mastering this framework, you will be able to predict legal outcomes in complex coercion scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of coerced acts
Read the passage and locate the three distinct categories mentioned: (1) acts where the coerced cannot be a tool at all (e.g., a muhrim coerced to kill game), (2) statements that are effective regardless of coercion (e.g., divorce, manumission), and (3) intermediate acts where the coerced can be a tool in form but not in locus (e.g., killing a person). For each, note the example given.
2
Understand the ālah (tool) concept and its conditions
Focus on the definition of ālah: the coerced is a tool when complete compulsion (iljā' tāmm) eliminates his free choice, and the act's effect can be isolated from his personal intention. Identify the conditions (e.g., the locus must not be personal to the coerced) and preventers (e.g., if the act requires personal intention).
The legal_definition_dissector block below summarizes this.
3
Analyze the intermediate case of killing: dual aspects
The passage explains that killing a Muslim has two aspects: destruction of the locus (izhāq al-rūh) which can be transferred, and sin (ithm) which cannot. Trace how the passage uses this dual aspect to justify attributing diyah, kaffarah, and qisās to the coercer, while sin remains on the coerced.
This is the key example of the intermediate category.
4
Apply the framework to statements and sale
Review the rulings on statements (divorce, etc.) which are effective on the coerced because they do not depend on consent. Also consider the sale example: coercion to sell and deliver results in valid ownership transfer for the buyer, as delivery is attributed to the coerced.
Statements are a distinct category where consent is not required for legal effect.
