وكذلك إذا كان نفس الفعل مما يتصور أن يكون الفاعل فيه آلة لغيره إلا أن يكون بالنظر إلى ذات الفعل المحل غير الذي يلاقيه الإتلاف صورةً، وكان ذلك يتبدل بأن يجعل آلة مثل إكراه المحرم على قتل الصيد إن ذلك يقتصر على الفاعل؛ لأن المكره إنما حمله على أن يجني على إحرام نفسه، وهو في ذلك لا يصلح آلة لغيره، = أي المكره بالفتح للمحمول، ولو أكره على أكل طعام غيره يجب الضمان على الحامل سواء كان جائعاً أو لا. وكذا لو أكره على الزنا لا يجب به الحدّ على واحد منهما، ويجب به العقر على المحمول، ولا يرجع به على الحامل؛ لأن منفعة الوطي حصلت له هذا في الأفعال وكذا الحكم في الأقوال التي لاتنفسخ ولا تتوقف على الرضاء كالطلاق والعتاق والنكاح والتدبير والعفو عن دم العمد والنذر واليمين، فلو أكره بها أحد وتكلّم بها تنفذ على المكره بالفتح، ولم تبطل بالكره. ولما كان قسم من الأفعال (وهو كل فعل صلح المحمول آلة فيه للحامل صورةً لا محلاً بأن يصلح آلة فيه بالنظر إلى ذات الفعل، لكن لا يصلح آلة فيه بالنظر إلى محله) متردّداً فيه هذا القسم مما لا يحتمله أو مما يحتمله، فبين المصنف ذلك بقوله: "وكذلك" أي مثل ما لا يحتمل أن يكون الفاعل فيه آلة لغيره بل ينسب إلى المباشر. المحل: أي محل الفعل المكره عليه. الإتلاف صورة: أي الفعل بالنظر إلى نفسه يصلح أن يكون الفاعل فيه آلة لغيره ولكن بالنظر إلى المحل لا يصلح آلة فيه بمعنى أنه لو جعل آلة فيه يتبدل المحلّ المكره عليه؛ إذ في صورة كون المكره آلة محل الإكراه هو إحرام المكره بالكسر كما سيأتي تقريره، وفي صورة نسبة الفعل إلى المكره بالفتح محله إحرامه لا إحرام المكره بالكسر، وبينهما مغائرة صورةً، وهذا معنى قوله "إلا أن المحل إلخ". وكان في هذه الجملة بيان للتغائر المذكور بقوله "إلا أن المحل إلخ". آلة للمكره بالكسر يعني لو جعل المكره آلة للمكره يلزم تبدّل المحل حيث يصير محل الإكراه إحرام المكره بالكسر، وحينئذ يلزم بطلان الإكراه وعود الفعل إلى المباشر، فلا يقصر الفعل على المباشر ابتداء. إكراه المحرم إلخ: أي إذا أكره إنسان محرماً على قتل صيد فقتله، فالقياس أن لايجب شيء لا على الأمر إذا كان حلالاً؛ لأنه قتله بنفسه لا يلزمه شيء، فكذا لو أكره عليه غيره، ولا على المأمور؛ لأنه بالإلجاء التام صلح أن يكون آلة للأمر فيه فانعدم الفعل منه، ولكن الاستحسان أن ذلك إلخ. يقتصر إلخ: لا أن يجعل آلة له؛ لأنه لو جعل آلة له لبطل الإكراه واللازم باطل؛ إذ الفرض أنه مكره، أمــــا الملازمة فهي أن في جعله آلة يلزم نقل محل الإكراه أو الجناية إلى المكره بالكسر في ذلك: أي في إيقاع الجناية على إحرامه. آلة: لغيره في ذلك كما قلتم لانتقل فعله، وهو جناية على إحرام نفسه إلى غيره وهو المكــره بالكسر؛ لأن الآلة لاتصلح أن ينسب إليها الفعل، فيكون غيره جانيا على إحرامه، وذلك غير ممكن؛ لأن الإنسان لا يجني على إحرام غيره فلا محالة. إحرام المكره بالكسر لو كان محرماً؛ إذ جناية كل واحد إنما تقع على إحرام نفسه لا على إحرام الغير المكره بالكسر ؛ لأنه أوقع الفعل في غير المحل الذي أمره بالإيقاع فيه. وبطلان الإكراه لوقوع الفعل على خلاف ما أمره به فكأنه أوقع بطريق الطواعية، فبطل الإكراه. المحل الأول: وهو إحرام المحمول؛ لأن سبب نقله الإكراه، فإذا بطل الإكراه بطل النقل، فثبت أن النقل إلى الحامل يجعل المكره المحمول آلةً له مستلزم لعوده فيه فلم يكن فيه فائدة، فيقتصر الفعل على الفاعل ابتداء قطعاً للمسافة، واحترازاً عن الاشتغال بما لا فائدة فيه. ولهذا: أي لأجل أنه إذا تبدل محلّ الجناية لجعله آلة له يقتصر الفعل على المباشر. يأثم إثم القتل؛ لأنه قصد الممنوع، وهو قتل المسلم، والقصد عمل القلب، وهو لايصلح آلة لغيره فيه؛ إذ الإنسان لايقصد بقلب غيره كما لا يتكلم بلسان غيره، فلذا بقي إثم القتل عليه وإن يصلح آلة له تفويت المحل، وهو إذهاق روح المقتول، فينتقل الفعل إلى الأمر من حيث الإتلاف، فلذا يجب عليــــه القصاص والدية والكفارة ويحرم عن الميراث، لا من حيث الإثم. لا يصلح في ذلك إلخ إذ لا يمكنه كسب الإثم على غيره فإنه لاتزر وازرة وزر أخرى. محل الجناية: إذ في جعله آلة يلزم أن ينسب ذلك الإثم إلى الآمر ؛ لأن الآلة لا تصلح أن تضاف إليها الفعل، فصار كأن المكره بالكسر كسب الإثم على المكره بالفتح، وذا لا يمكن فحينئذ يكون محل الجناية هو المكره بالكسر، وقد كان محلها المكره، وهو لم يأمره أن يكسب الإثم عليه فيبطل الإكراه، وبطلانه يستلزم عود الفعل إلى المباشرة، فلا فائدة في نقله فيقتصر عليه ابتداء والحاصل أن لقتل المسلم اعتبارين أحدهما أنه يوجب تفويت المحل، وبهذا الاعتبار يصلح أن يكون المباشر له آلة لغيره، وثانيهما أنه يوجب الإثم، وبهذا الاعتبار لا يصلح أن يكون المباشر آلة لغيره. وكذلك أي كما قلنا: إن القتل من حيث الإثم يقتصر على المباشر على البيع والتسليم بأن أكره على بيع شيء فباع، ثم أكره على تسليمه إلى المشتري فسلّم يقتصر عليه حتى يثبت الملك للمشتري
Chapter of Qiyas. Types of Coercion: And similarly, if the act itself can be conceived of the doer being a tool for another, except that with respect to the essence of the act, the locus is other than that which meets the destruction in form, and that changes by making it a tool, like coercing a muhrim to kill game — that is confined to the doer; because the coerced person only was made to commit an aggression against his own ihram, and in that he is not suitable as a tool for another. (Meaning the coerced [with fatha] for the one carrying the act). And if he is coerced to eat another's food, the guarantee is obligatory on the coercer whether he is hungry or not. Similarly, if he is coerced to commit zina, the hadd penalty is not obligatory on either of them, and the bridal gift (ʿaqr) is obligatory on the coerced, and he does not recourse to the coercer; because the benefit of intercourse accrued to him. This is regarding actions. And similarly the ruling applies to statements that are not revocable and do not depend on consent, like divorce, freeing slaves, marriage, tadbir, pardon for intentional killing, vows, and oaths. So if one is coerced into such and utters them, they are effective on the coerced (with fatha) and are not nullified by coercion. And since a division of actions (which is every action in which the coerced is suitable as a tool for the coercer in form but not in locus — meaning it is suitable as a tool with respect to the essence of the act, but not suitable as a tool with respect to its locus) is ambiguous as to whether it belongs to the category that does not allow being a tool or that does allow, the author clarified that by his saying: 'And similarly' meaning like that which does not allow that the doer be a tool for another but is attributed to the direct doer. The locus: meaning the locus of the coerced act. Destruction in form: meaning the act with respect to itself is suitable for the doer to be a tool for another, but with respect to the locus it is not suitable as a tool, meaning that if it were made a tool, the locus of coercion would change. For in the form of the coerced being a tool, the locus of coercion is the ihram of the coerced (with kasra) as will come, and in the form of attributing the act to the coerced (with fatha), its locus is his own ihram, not the ihram of the coerced (with kasra), and between them is a difference in form. This is the meaning of his saying 'except that the locus etc.' And in this sentence was an explanation of the mentioned difference by his saying 'except that the locus etc.' A tool for the coerced (with kasra) means if he makes the coerced a tool for the coercer, a change of locus is necessitated, whereby the locus of coercion becomes the ihram of the coerced (with kasra), and then nullification of the coercion and return of the act to the direct doer is necessitated, so the act is not confined to the direct doer initially. Coercion of the muhrim etc.: meaning if a person coerces a muhrim to kill game and he kills it, the analogical reasoning (qiyas) is that nothing is obligatory on the commander if he is non-muhrim, because if he killed it himself nothing is obligatory, so likewise if he coerces another. And nothing on the commanded, because by complete compulsion he is suitable as a tool for the commander in it, so the act is negated from him. But istihsan is that it etc. Is confined etc.: meaning not to be made a tool for him; because if it were made a tool for him, coercion would be nullified, and the necessary consequence is false; since the assumption is that he is coerced. As for the correlation, it is that making him a tool necessitates transferring the locus of coercion or the injury to the coercer (with kasra) in that: i.e., in committing the injury on his ihram. A tool: for another in that, as you said, his act would transfer — which is an aggression on his own ihram — to another, namely the coercer (with kasra); because a tool is not suitable to have the act attributed to it, so another would be aggressing on his ihram, and that is impossible because a person does not aggress against another's ihram, inevitably. The ihram of the coerced (with kasra) if he is a muhrim; since the aggression of each one only falls on his own ihram, not on the ihram of the other, the coercer (with kasra); because he performed the act in a locus other than that which he was ordered to perform it in. And nullification of coercion due to the act occurring contrary to what he ordered him, as if he performed it willingly, so coercion is nullified. The first locus: which is the ihram of the coerced; because the cause of its transfer is coercion, so when coercion is nullified, the transfer is nullified, so it is established that transferring to the coercer makes the coerced a tool for him, necessitating its return in it, so there is no benefit in it, so the act is confined to the doer initially to cut off the distance and to avoid occupying oneself with what has no benefit. And for this: i.e., because when the locus of the injury is changed by making him a tool, the act is confined to the direct doer. He incurs the sin of killing; because he intended the prohibited, which is killing a Muslim, and intention is an act of the heart, and he is not suitable as a tool for another in it; since a person does not intend with the heart of another, just as he does not speak with the tongue of another, therefore the sin of killing remains on him, although he is suitable as a tool for the destruction of the locus, which is extinguishing the soul of the killed. So the act transfers to the commander in terms of destruction, therefore retaliation, blood money, and expiation are obligatory on him, and he is prohibited from inheritance, not in terms of sin. He is not suitable in that etc., since he cannot acquire sin for another, for no bearer of burdens bears another's burden. The locus of the injury: for in making him a tool, it would be necessary to attribute that sin to the commander; because a tool is not suitable to have the act attributed to it, so it would be as if the coercer (with kasra) acquired sin upon the coerced (with fatha), and that is impossible, so then the locus of the injury becomes the coercer (with kasra), whereas its locus was the coerced, and he did not order him to acquire sin upon him, so coercion is nullified, and its nullification necessitates return of the act to the direct doer, so there is no benefit in transferring it, so it is confined initially. The conclusion is that killing a Muslim has two aspects: one is that it necessitates destruction of the locus, and by this aspect the direct doer is suitable as a tool for another; the second is that it necessitates sin, and by this aspect the direct doer is not suitable as a tool for another. And similarly, just as we said that killing in terms of sin is confined to the direct doer, so too with sale and delivery: if he is coerced to sell something and he sells, then coerced to deliver it to the buyer and he delivers, the delivery is confined to him so that ownership is established for the buyer.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn about the types of coercion (ikrah) and their legal effects, within a chapter on qiyas. The commentator expands on the principle that in some acts of coercion, the coerced person (mukrah) can be considered a mere tool (ālah) for the coercer (mukrih), such that the act is attributed to the coercer, while in others the coerced cannot be a tool, so the act remains attributed to him. The text classifies actions into three categories. The first category includes acts where the coerced cannot be a tool at all, e.g., a muhrim (one in ihram) being coerced to kill game — the sin and liability are on the muhrim because he cannot transfer the violation of his own ihram to another. Similarly, coercion to eat another's food makes the coercer liable for the guarantee (damān), but the act of eating is still attributed to the coerced. Coercion to zina does not impose the hadd penalty on either, but the 'aqr (bridal gift) is on the coerced because he benefits from the act. The second category includes statements that are effective regardless of coercion, like divorce and manumission — these are binding on the coerced because they are not revocable and do not require consent. The third, intermediate category consists of acts where the coerced can be a tool in form (regarding the act itself) but not in locus (the specific subject matter). The example is coercion to kill a person: the coerced can be a tool in causing death (the destructive effect), so the coercer bears the blood money, expiation, and is barred from inheritance; but the sin of intentional killing remains on the coerced because intention is an act of the heart and cannot be transferred. The commentator explains the reasoning behind this distinction, emphasizing that if making the coerced a tool would change the locus of the act (e.g., from the coerced's own ihram to the coercer's), then coercion would be nullified and the act reverts to the direct doer. The passage concludes with the example of coercion to sell and deliver: the delivery is confined to the coerced, so ownership transfers to the buyer. Throughout, the commentator uses the concepts of qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference) to derive rulings, showing how Hanafi usul applies these tools.

Key Terms
آلة (ālah): A tool or instrument; in the context of coercion, a person who is coerced so completely that he is like a tool in the hands of the coercer, so the act is attributed to the coercer.
إكراه (ikrāh): Coercion that compels a person to act against his will, typically with a threat of harm.
محل (maḥall): The locus or subject matter of the act; e.g., in killing, the locus is the victim or the ihram of the killer.
إثم (ithm): Sin; a personal moral culpability that cannot be transferred from the coerced to the coercer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a person is coerced into killing a Muslim, who bears the sin (ithm) of the killing?
A) The coercer alone
B) Neither the coercer nor the coerced
C) The coerced person alone
D) Both the coercer and the coerced equally

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, coercion does not automatically absolve the coerced of sin; the key is whether the coerced can be considered a tool for the coercer in terms of the act's physical effect, but sin always stays with the direct doer due to personal intention.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate
8 min
Dispute on Coercion: Does It Nullify Choice? Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Compares Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on coercion's effect on acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that coercion removes consent but not choice, and apply it to derive rulings for verbal and physical acts.

Core Idea
Coercion removes consent, not choice: the coerced still chooses the lesser harm.

The effect of coercion (ikrah) on legal acts is a major usul dispute. The Shafi'i school holds that unjust coercion eliminates intention and choice entirely, rendering verbal transactions void. The Hanafis counter that coercion does not eliminate choice but only corrupts it or removes consent. This distinction leads to different rulings for verbal acts like divorce and physical acts like destroying property. This passage presents the Hanafi position with its proofs and contrasts the Shafi'i view, showing the thamarah al-khilaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core Hanafi principle
Find the statement in the text that says 'الإكراه لا يعدم الاختيار' (coercion does not eliminate choice). Understand that this is the foundational difference from the Shafi'i view.
This phrase appears twice, emphasizing its importance.
2
Distinguish between consent and choice
The Hanafis say coercion removes consent (al-rida') or corrupts choice (yufsid al-ikhtiyar). Recognize that the coerced still makes a choice (the lesser harm), so his act is not entirely involuntary like that of a sleeping person or madman.
3
Examine the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i is reported to hold that verbal acts under unjust coercion are void because they require intention and choice. For physical acts, if coercion is complete, the ruling is removed from the direct doer and attributed to the coercer if possible.
The condition for complete coercion is that the act becomes permissible (e.g., eating carrion when starving).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to verbal acts
The Hanafis argue that the coerced still chooses to speak (to avoid harm), so the act is not void. However, because his choice is defective, the act may have limited effect. Examples: coerced divorce is effective; coerced conversion to Islam is valid.
Coercion with right (e.g., judge forcing debtor to sell) produces valid transactions because the Law commands it.
5
Apply to physical acts and thamarah
For property destruction, liability transfers to the coercer. For unlawful acts like murder, coercion is incomplete, so the doer is liable. For acts like breaking fast, the fast is invalidated but treated as forgetfulness (no sin). This shows the practical difference between the schools.
The Hanafi principle leads to nuanced rulings, not blanket nullification.

Chapter of Qiyas (Analogy). Types of Coercion. And the meaning of [financial] destruction is transferred from him to the one who coerced him; because it is separate from him in a general sense, [the coerced] is bearing [the responsibility] of any destruction of the property of this slave — i.e., the destruction — i.e., regarding emancipation — for transfer in its origin, and this is according to us [Hanafis]. And al-Shafi'i said: The verbal transactions of the coerced are void if the coercion is without right; because the validity of speech is [based] on intention and choice, so that it is an expression of what is in the heart. That is only when [intention and choice] exist. So it becomes invalid when they are absent. And coercion by imprisonment is like coercion by killing according to him. And when coercion occurs on an action, if the coercion is complete, the ruling of the action is removed from the doer. Its completeness is by making it an excuse that permits the action — i.e., for the one being coerced — i.e., [the action] is dropped — i.e., the coercion — the action from him to the coercer, making him an instrument for him; because the condition for transfer is that the action is something that can be understood [as originating from the coercer] and imprisonment is not [such]; because it is impossible for the emancipation to occur from the coerced by the tongue of the coercer; since a person cannot speak with the tongue of another. Even if that were granted, he cannot free another's slave; because the slave who is freed under coercion is not his slave but rather the slave of the coercer — from him — i.e., from the coercer — something that can occur from the coercer, but its occurrence from him is not sensed [directly], so [the destruction] is attributed to him due to the existence of the condition [that the coercer is the cause]. He coerced him: i.e., the coercer, so the destruction is attributed to him until he guarantees the value of the slave to the direct [coerced] whether [the direct actor] is wealthy or poor. And because it could be objected that since the emancipation is not transferred to the coercer, how can the destruction be transferred to him alone? Because [the destruction] is within it [the emancipation?], he answered this by saying: 'because it [the destruction] is implied in the emancipation?'. The phrase 'in a general sense': for in the case of killing the slave, the destruction exists but the emancipation does not. In its origin: i.e., the destruction can be transferred to the coercer initially, such as when he destroys the slave of the coercer by killing, then it is transferred to him after its occurrence from the coerced, making the coerced an instrument for him, so the destruction is transferred to him without the emancipation. This is what has been mentioned regarding the rulings of coercion. The verbal transactions of the coerced, such as divorce, emancipation, sale, and what pertains to speech, [are considered] when intention and choice are absent. So whenever [the act] occurs without choice, it does not express what is intended in the heart; like the speech of a sleeping person, a child, or a madman. And coercion indicates that the coerced spoke to repel harm from himself, not to express what is intended in his heart, so it is not considered because it lacks intention. This applies to [verbal] transactions [done] without right. But if he is coerced with right, his verbal transactions are valid, even sound: if a war captive is coerced to embrace Islam, his Islam is valid; similarly if a judge coerces a debtor to sell his property, it is valid; because when coercion is with right, the Law has commanded coercion for that transaction, so the Law demands the transaction, and what the Law demands is valid. According to him — i.e., according to al-Shafi'i — [؟] explained in nullifying the speech and action of the coerced. These are the rulings of verbal transactions. As for physical transactions, their ruling according to al-Shafi'i — may Allah [guide?] — is what he stated: 'And when coercion occurs on an action...' meaning on an action, whether it is possible to attribute it to the coercer or not. [It is removed] from the direct doer, so the doer is not held accountable at all, whether the coercer is held accountable or not. His statement 'so when it is complete' is the apodosis of 'when it occurs'. The action: legally, such as coercion by killing or by perpetual imprisonment to destroy another's property, drink wine, or break the fast of Ramadan. = [Page 333] Chapter of Qiyas. Types of Coercion. Coercion does not eliminate choice. So if it is possible to attribute [the action] to the coercer, it is attributed to him; otherwise, it is void entirely. And we have stated that coercion does not eliminate choice, but it removes consent or corrupts choice. Up to the end — i.e., the coercion — what we have established, and with this the book concludes. So you [are to understand?]. And its becoming. If it is compelling [coercion], it permits the action according to him. And the permissibility of the action is only a proof of complete coercion because it indicates the completeness of the excuse, compulsorily, as in the case of one compelled to eat carrion: permissibility indicates complete excuse. If coercion is not complete due to the lack of permissibility of the action, then the action is not removed from the doer; rather, he is held accountable, as when one is coerced to commit adultery or to kill a Muslim unjustly, for it is not permissible for him to commit them. This indicates that the coercion is incomplete, so [the doer] is punished with the hadd penalty and retaliation is required. After excluding the direct doer from the matter, we look at that action: [if it can be] attributed to [the coercer], he is made the one held accountable, and the doer is an instrument for him, like coercion to destroy property, for then the guarantee is upon [the coercer]. Otherwise — i.e., if it is not possible to attribute it to the coercer — then the action is entirely void and no one is held accountable, like coercion to break the fast of Ramadan: if he breaks it, his fast is invalidated and it is treated as if he ate forgetfully. And we have stated what is the principle in the chapter of coercion: that coercion does not eliminate choice — i.e., it does not nullify it entirely. It removes consent etc., whether the coercion is compelling or not. What we established at the beginning of the discussion: that the coerced does not lose his choice because he is faced with two options and chooses the easier one, so choice is established for him. But that is based on another's choice [the coercer's] by his coercion to perform the action, so he is not independent in the action, so his choice is defective. Thus it is established that he has a defective choice, or that his consent is lost by coercion, so there is no effect to invalidate his verbal and physical transactions entirely, as al-Shafi'i said. Rather, rulings are based on the corruption of choice and loss of consent, as has been explained.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the effect of coercion (ikrah) on verbal and physical acts. The author explains that according to the Hanafi school, when someone is coerced into destroying property (like killing a slave), the liability for that destruction is transferred from the coerced person (mukrah bi'l-fath) to the coercer (mukrah bi'l-kasr), because the coercer is the real cause. However, this transfer does not apply to verbal acts like divorce or emancipation because those require direct utterance by the coerced; the coercer cannot speak for him. Al-Shafi'i is reported to hold that all verbal transactions of a coerced person are void if the coercion is unjust, because validity depends on intention and choice, which are absent. For physical acts, al-Shafi'i holds that complete coercion (like threat of death or perpetual imprisonment) removes the ruling from the direct doer entirely, attributing it to the coercer if possible; otherwise the act is void. The Hanafi position, reiterated here, is that coercion does not eliminate choice (ikhtiyar) but only removes consent (rida) or corrupts choice. Thus, the coerced still has a defective choice, so his acts are not entirely void. For example, if someone is coerced to break his fast, his fast is invalidated due to his defective choice, but he is not held sinful as if he chose freely. If someone is coerced to kill a Muslim unjustly, the coercion is not considered complete because the act remains unlawful, so the doer is subject to retaliation. The passage also distinguishes between coercion with right (bihaqq) and without right; coercion with right (e.g., forcing a debtor to sell assets) produces valid transactions because the Law itself commands such coercion. The core principle is that the Hanafis do not equate coercion with loss of sanity or sleep; the coerced makes a choice (to avoid harm), so his actions have some legal effect, whereas the Shafi'is see it as annulling the act entirely when coercion is complete and unjust.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compelling someone to do something against his will by threat of harm, such as killing or imprisonment.
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyār): Free choice; the ability to voluntarily decide between options.
الرضاء (al-riḍā): Consent; satisfaction with the act.
المكره بالفتح (al-mukrah bi'l-fatḥ): The person being coerced (the direct doer of the action).
المكره بالكسر (al-mukrih bi'l-kasr): The person who does the coercing.
إكراه ملجئ (ikrāh mulji'): Compelling coercion that leaves no choice but to comply, like threat of death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the effect of coercion on the choice (ikhtiyar) of the coerced person?
A) It eliminates choice entirely.
B) It removes consent but does not eliminate choice; the coerced retains a defective choice.
C) It has no effect on choice.
D) It makes the coerced like a sleeping person.

Key Takeaway
Coercion in Hanafi usul removes consent (rida) but not choice (ikhtiyar); this distinction is the basis for the school's rulings on coerced acts.

Ikhtilaf — Scholarly Disagreement
intermediate

The Particle Thumma and the Hanafi Dispute on Delay
States the ruling of thumma and presents an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two Hanafi positions on the particle thumma and apply them to a conditional divorce scenario.

Core Idea
Thumma's delay is complete for Abu Hanifa, partial for the Sahibayn — changing the entire sentence structure.

This passage introduces the internal Hanafi debate over the meaning of thumma (then). The difference lies in whether the delay after thumma cuts the speech into separate statements or merely delays the application. The practical outcome appears in conditional divorce cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and its function
Recognize thumma as a conjunction of delay (tarakhi). The passage states it is for conjunction upon the basis of delay.
Compare with fa (immediate sequence) and waw (mere conjunction).
2
State the disagreement
Distinguish Abu Hanifa's view (complete delay in both ruling and utterance) from the Sahibayn's view (delay only in existence of the ruling, not in utterance).
The phrase 'كأنه مستأنف حكماً قولاً' captures Abu Hanifa's position.
3
Apply to the divorce example
Walk through the scenario: a man says before consummation 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced, if you enter the house.' Under Abu Hanifa, only the first divorce occurs; the rest are void. The Sahibayn would likely consider the utterances connected, so all three could be valid upon condition fulfillment.
The cut in speech means the subsequent divorces cannot occur because the place (the wife) no longer is available for further divorces before consummation.
4
Extend to conditional variants
Note the difference between postponed and advanced conditions. If the condition is advanced ('If you enter, you are divorced, then divorced...'), the first divorce is suspended, the second occurs immediately, and the third is void.
The benefit of the first being suspended: if she is remarried and the condition occurs later, the first divorce applies.

["ثم" وحكمها]
حروف العطف
وأما "ثم" فللعطف على سبيل التراخي، ثم إن عند أبي حنيفة بالله التراخي على وجه القطع كأنه مستأنف حكماً قولاً بكمال التراخي، وعند صاحبيه التراخي في الوجود دون التكلّم بيانه فيمن قال لامرأته قبل الدخول بها: أنتِ طالق ثم طالق ثم طالق إن دخلت الدار، قال أبو حنيفة له: يقع الأول، ويلغو ما بعده كأنه سكت على الأول،
للحال أي في الوقت؛ لأن الفاء هنا للتعليل؛ إذ العتق علة لأداء الألف، والعلة مقدّمة على المعلول، فكأنه أنجز العتق ثم أمره بأداء الألف، فيعتق بقوله: فإنك حر" في الحال، فالفاء داخلة على العلة الدائمة وهي العتق، أما كونه علة فظاهر. فأشبه المتراخي عن الحكم، فيتحقق البعدية للعتق لدوامه بالنسبة إلى ابتداء الأداء، فلما تحقق التعقيب صـــح دخـــول الفاء عليها بهذا الاعتبار فافهم على سبيل التراخي وهو أن يكون بين المعطوف والمعطوف عليه مهلة في الفعل المتعلّق بهما على وجه القطع بأن يظهر أثره في الحكم والتكلّم جميعاً، حتى كأنه أي الكلام مستأنف حكماً: كان القائل قطع الكلام الأول ثم استأنف.
،
بكمال التراخي وهو أن يكون في الحكم والتكلم جميعاً، وذلك لأن تلك الكلمة موضوعة لمطلق التراخي، فتدل على كماله؛ إذ المطلق ينصرف إلى الكامل دون التكلم فعندهما التراخي في الحكم مع الوصل في التكلم؛ إذ ظاهر اللفظ موصول مع الأول، فكيف يجعل في التكلم منفصلا أن العطف لا يصح مع الانفصال، ومرة الخلاف ما بينه بقوله: بيانه فيمن قال إلخ. ويلغو ما بعده لأن التراخي لما كان في التكلم. على الأول: ثم استأنف الكلام، فيقع الأول لعدم توقف أول الكلام على آخره وإن وجد المغير لفوات شرط التوقف، وهو الاتصال في الكلام، ولما وقع الأول لم يبق المحل لما بعده؛ إذ المرأة غير مدخولة، فيلغو ما بعده ضرورةً كما إذا وجد السكوت حقيقة. هذا إذا أخر الشرط، ولو قدّم الشرط بأن قال: إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ثم طالق ثم طالق، تعلّق الأول بالشرط ووقع الثاني في الحال لبقاء المحل؛ إذ الأوّل تعلق بالشرط والمعلق لا ينزل في المحل، ولغا الثالث لعدم المحل. وفائدة تعلق الأول بالشرط أنه إذا نكحها ثانياً ووجد الشرط يقع الطلاق بسبب التعليق السابق. فإن قيل: إذا قلتم بالتراخي في التكلم حكماً انقطع الكلام الأول عن الثاني كأنه سكت حقيقةً ثم استأنف، فعلى هذا صار قوله: "ثم طالق" =
"Thumma" and its ruling
Particles of conjunction
As for "thumma", it is for conjunction upon the basis of delay. Then, according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him), the delay is in a definitive manner, as if it is independent in ruling and utterance, with complete delay. According to his two companions, the delay is in existence but not in utterance. Its explanation is in one who said to his wife before consummation: 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced, if you enter the house.' Abu Hanifa said: The first divorce occurs, and what follows is void, as if he was silent after the first.
For the immediate, i.e., at the time; because fa here is for causation; since manumission is a cause for giving the thousand dirhams, and the cause precedes the effect, so it is as if he executed the manumission then commanded him to give the thousand, so he is freed by his saying 'for you are free' immediately. So fa enters on a perpetual cause, which is manumission. As for it being a cause, it is apparent. So it resembles the delayed from the ruling, so the posteriority of the manumission is realized due to its perpetuity relative to the beginning of the giving. So when the immediate sequence is realized, the entry of fa upon it is correct by this consideration. So understand: upon the basis of delay, which is that there is a period between the coordinate and the coordinate in the action related to them, in a definitive manner that its effect appears in both the ruling and the utterance, so that it is as if the speech is independent in ruling: the speaker cut the first speech and then started anew.
With complete delay, which is that it is in both the ruling and the utterance. That is because that word is placed for absolute delay, so it indicates its completeness; since the absolute applies to the complete. Without the utterance: according to them, the delay is in the ruling while the utterance is connected; because the apparent meaning of the word is connected with the first, so how can it be made separate in utterance? Because conjunction is not valid with separation. And the summary of the disagreement is what he clarified by saying: its explanation is in who said etc. And what follows is void because the delay was in the utterance. On the first: then he started the speech anew, so the first occurs because the first part of speech does not depend on the last, even if there is a modifier due to loss of the condition of dependency, which is connection in speech. And when the first occurred, there remains no place for what follows; since the woman is not consummated, so what follows is void necessarily, just as if there was actual silence. This is if the condition is postponed. But if the condition is advanced, by saying: 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced, then divorced, then divorced,' the first is attached to the condition and the second occurs immediately due to remaining of the place; because the first was attached to the condition and the conditional does not descend into the place, and the third is void due to lack of place. And the benefit of the first being attached to the condition is that if he marries her again and the condition occurs, the divorce falls due to the previous suspension. If it is said: if you say delay in utterance as a ruling, the first speech is cut off from the second as if he actually was silent then started anew, then according to this his saying 'then divorced' becomes ...
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage provides a textual analysis of the particle thumma (ثم) in legal statements, focusing on the nature of the delay it implies. The author states that thumma is for conjunction upon delay (تراخي). He then presents the dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). According to Abu Hanifa, the delay is complete, meaning it affects both the ruling (الحكم) and the utterance (التكلم); the part after thumma is treated as if it is a new independent statement, with a cut in speech. According to the two companions, the delay is only in existence (الوجود) i.e., in the application of the ruling, but the utterance remains connected; the words are said in one continuous speech. The author illustrates with an example: if a man says to his wife before consummation 'You are divorced, then divorced, then divorced, if you enter the house,' Abu Hanifa rules that only the first divorce counts, and the rest are void, because after the first there is a complete cut (like a silent pause) and then a new start. The companions would rule differently: possibly all three are valid if the condition is fulfilled? The text doesn't state their position fully here, but implies they see the utterance as connected. The author then explains the ruling in detail: if the condition is advanced (i.e., mentioned before the divorces), the first divorce becomes suspended on the condition, the second occurs immediately, and the third is void. The benefit of the first being suspended is that if he remarries her later and the condition occurs, the first divorce takes effect. The passage ends with an objection regarding the implications of Abu Hanifa's view, suggesting that if the first speech is cut, then 'then divorced' would be a new beginning, affecting the count. The author notes that according to Abu Hanifa, the first occurs immediately (since there is no condition), and the second and third are void because the place (the wife) is already divorced once and cannot receive further divorces until after consummation? But the text is incomplete.

Key Terms
ثم (thumma): A particle of conjunction that indicates delay (تراخي) between the coordinate and the coordinate. The delay can be in both ruling and utterance (Abu Hanifa) or only in the ruling (Sahibayn).
التراخي (al-tarakhi): Delay or interval between two actions or events. In the context of thumma, it can refer to delay in the ruling and/or the utterance.
مطلق (mutlaq): An unqualified or absolute term. In this passage, 'absolute delay' (مطلق التراخي) is argued to indicate the complete form of delay.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what does the particle thumma (ثم) indicate in legal statements?
A) Delay only in the ruling, not in the utterance.
B) Complete delay in both the ruling and the utterance.
C) Immediate sequence without any delay.
D) Connection without any sequence.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of thumma in legal statements is contested: it can either cut the speech or merely delay the ruling, affecting the validity of subsequent clauses.

The Particle 'Bal' in Conditional Divorce: Pure Conjunction in Insha'
Analyzing 'bal' in divorce oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two semantic functions of 'bal' (tadaruk al-ghalat and atf mahd) and apply the Hanafi rule that in performative utterances (insha') 'bal' adds the second phrase without cancelling the first, deriving the correct number of divorces.

Core Idea
In performative language like divorce, 'bal' does not correct but adds—three divorces fall when used in a conditional oath.

The particle 'bal' (rather, but rather) has two distinct semantic roles: in informative statements (khabar) it corrects a mistake, but in performative utterances (insha') it acts as a pure conjunctive, simply adding the second item without negating the first. This lesson unpacks that distinction through the case of conditional divorce, where a husband says 'You are divorced once, no rather twice.' Understanding this rule is essential for correctly counting divorces and avoiding invalid oaths.

Guided Path
1
1. State the linguistic definition of 'bal'
Begin by recalling the basic meaning: 'bal' affirms what follows and turns away from what precedes. This is the foundation for both its functions.
From the source: 'بل' موضوع لإثبات ما بعده والإعراض عما قبله.
2
2. Distinguish the two semantic functions of 'bal' in khabar vs. insha'
In khabar (informative speech), 'bal' corrects a previous misstatement (e.g., 'Zaid came, rather 'Amr'). In insha' (performative utterances like divorce), it cannot correct because the utterance is an act itself; instead, it serves as pure conjunction (atf mahd).
The source explains that in insha', the first part cannot be retracted, so 'bal' merely joins the second.
3
3. Apply the rule to the given divorce scenario
Consider: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced once, no rather twice.' Since this is a performative oath, 'bal' adds 'twice' to 'once'. Both numbers are attached to the condition, resulting in three divorces upon fulfilment.
All three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree on this outcome.
4
4. Contrast with the particle 'waw' and note the intra-Hanafi dispute
If he says 'once and twice' using 'waw', a dispute arises: Abu Hanifa holds that only one divorce occurs because 'waw' implies sequence and the first divorce exhausts the wife's eligibility. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say all three occur because 'waw' is a pure conjunctive without sequence.
This contrast highlights the importance of particle semantics in legal rulings.
5
5. Derive the usul principle behind the Hanafi position
The key usul principle: In insha', the performative nature prevents retraction or correction, so any particle that ordinarily corrects (like 'bal') must be reinterpreted as a pure conjunctive. This principle is based on the nature of speech acts and the need to preserve the legal effect of the first utterance.
This is an example of ta'lil (reasoning) based on linguistic analysis.

['Bal' and its ruling] As for 'bal', it is placed to affirm what comes after it and to turn away from what came before. It is said: 'Zaid came to me' (the first part) 'rather 'Amr'. And they all said, concerning one who said to his wife before consummation: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced once, no rather twice' – meaning the three imams – '[she is] divorced once, no rather twice' = ... As for the consummated one, if the penalty is advanced, the first and second occur immediately... whereas for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), all occur because of their sharing in the condition...] ... after negation i.e. to remove a misunderstanding arising from the previous negated speech. 'Zaid did not come to me' might create the impression that 'Amr also did not come.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage deals with the particle 'bal' (rather, but rather) and its effect in conditional divorce utterances. The author first gives the linguistic definition: 'bal' is used to affirm the following and to turn away from the preceding. He then presents a case: a man says to his wife before consummation, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced once, no rather twice.' The Hanafi school (the three imams) agree that upon entry, three divorces occur. The reasoning is that in constructive utterances (insha'), 'bal' acts as a pure conjunctive (عطف محض) that does not negate the first but rather adds the second as an increment. This is because the first part is a performative utterance that cannot be retracted; therefore, 'bal' here simply joins the second to the first. The text contrasts this with the particle 'waw' (and) in a similar construction: if he says 'once and twice', according to Imam Abu Hanifa only one divorce falls, because 'waw' indicates sequential conjunction, so the first divorce occurs and removes the subject's eligibility. However, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, all three divorces fall because both phrases are equally linked to the condition. The passage also discusses the difference between the two meanings of 'bal': one that corrects a mistake (تدارك الغلط) in informative statements, and one that operates as pure conjunction in performatives. This analysis is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for understanding the effect of particles in legal utterances.

Key Terms
بل (bal): A particle used to affirm the following and turn away from the preceding; in insha' (performative speech) it acts as a pure conjunctive, adding the second phrase without cancelling the first.
عطف محض (atf mahd): Pure conjunction that simply joins two items without any additional meaning of correction or sequence.

When 'bal' appears in a performative utterance like a conditional divorce, it acts as a pure conjunctive; both numbers are added, and the total number of divorces is the sum of both.

The Meanings of Hatta in Conditional Oaths and Their Legal Effects
Analysis of hatta in oaths: ghaya, lām kay, and 'atf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether 'hatta' in a conditional oath indicates an endpoint (ghaya), a purpose (lām kay), or a conjunction (fa/waw), and apply the Hanafi ruling on when the oath is broken.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'hatta' determines oath-breaking: ghaya, purpose, or conjunction.

In conditional oaths, the particle 'hatta' (until) carries multiple possible meanings depending on the relationship between the actions before and after it. The Hanafi school distinguishes between when it indicates an endpoint (ghaya), a purpose (lām kay), or a conjunction (fa/waw). This passage from a classical usul text analyzes these distinctions through concrete examples and explains the legal consequences for oath-breaking. By mastering this analysis, you will be able to parse complex oath formulations and determine their validity.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish ghaya from lām kay
Identify whether the action after hatta is a natural endpoint of the action before it (ghaya) or a purpose of the first action (lām kay). In the first example, 'until you scream' is an endpoint of hitting; in the second, 'until you feed me' is a purpose of coming.
The test: if the second action can serve as an ending for the first, it is ghaya; if it motivates the first, it is lām kay.
2
Determine when the oath is broken for ghaya
If hatta is ghaya, the swearer breaks the oath if he stops before the endpoint. For 'I will not hit you until you scream', he breaks if he stops screaming before hitting or if he never hits at all. He does not break if he hits continuously until the scream occurs.
The endpoint itself (scream) is not an action of the swearer but a result.
3
Determine when the oath is broken for lām kay
If hatta is lām kay, the oath is conditional on the purpose being served. For 'I will not come to you until you feed me', if the swearer comes (even if not fed), he does not break his oath because his coming was for the purpose of being fed, and the feeding is the addressee's action.
The swearer's action is the means; the purpose is not his action.
4
Analyze when actions are from the same person
When both actions are from the same person (e.g., 'If I do not come until I eat breakfast'), hatta cannot be ghaya or lām kay because an action cannot be an endpoint or cause of itself. It is then interpreted as a conjunction ('atf).
The conjunction is metaphorically transferred to hatta.
5
Evaluate the dispute: fa vs waw
There is disagreement whether hatta in same-person cases should be interpreted as fa (implying immediacy but allowing delay) or waw (pure conjunction). The Hanafi tarjih prefers fa because it shares the notion of succession (ta'qib) with ghaya, and it allows for delay in fulfilling the condition.
Under fa, if the second action occurs with delay, the oath is broken. Under waw, it may not be if delay is not considered conjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حروف المعاني
٣٤٦
حروف العطف
حتى تصيح إنه يحنث إن أقلع قبل الغاية، واستعير للمجازاة بمعنى لام كي في قوله: أي امتنع عن الضرب إن لم آتك غداً حتى تغدينى، حتى إذا أتاه فلم يغدّه لم يحنث؛ لأن الإحسان لا يصلح
وهو تغدية المخاطب إياه منهيّاً للإتيان بل هو سبب له، فإن كان الفعلان من واحد كقوله: إن لم آتك حتى
أتغدى عندك تعلّق البرّ بهما ؛ لأن فعله لا يصلح جزاءً لفعله، فحمل على العطف بحرف الفاء؛ لأن الغاية تجانس التعقيب.
حتى تصيح أنت فهذا مثال للغاية؛ لأن صدر الكلام أي ما قبل "حتى" وهو ضرب المخاطب أمر ممتد والآخر أي ما بعد "حتى" وهو الصياح يصلح انتهاء له؛ لهيجان الرحمة أو لحدوث الخوف من أحد أنه مفعول. قبل الغاية وهي الصياح وكذا يحنث إن لم يضربه أصلاً . نعم لو ضربه إلى الصياح لوجود البر لا يحنث. حتى تغديني: فهنا "حتى" لا تصلح للغاية، فتكون بمعنى لام كي أي لم آتك لكي تغديني. لم يحنث لأنه أتاه للتغدية وهي فعل المخاطب لا اختيار للمتكلم فيه وأما قلنا لا يصلح للغاية؛ لأن صدر الكلام وهو الإتيان وإن صلح للامتداد بحدوث الأمثال، لكن آخره وهو التغدية لايصلح انتهاء له.
سبب
من
له: أي للإتيان وداع لزيادة الإتيان فإذا كان سبباً له صار "حتى" بمعنى لام كي مفيدة للسببية والمجازاة؛ لأن جزاء الشيء وسببه يكون مقصوداً منه بمنزلة الغاية من المغيا، والسببية والمجازاة إنما يتحقق إذا كان الفعلان فاعلين؛ لأن فعله لايصلح جزاء لفعله في الأغلب الفعلان: أحدهما قبل "حتى" والآخر بعدها من شخص. كقوله : إن لم آتك إلخ فعبدي حرّ، فالتغدية في هذا المثال كما كان لا يصلح انتهاء لصدر الكلام كذا لا يصلح السببية، فإن الإتيان على الغير ليس سببا لتغدّي الآتي عنده لعدم كون الإتيان مفضياً إليه، فتعين أن تجعل مستعارة للعطف، فكأنه قال: إن لم آتك فلم أتغد عندك فعبدي حرّ. بهما: أي بالإتيان والتغدية عنده غير متراخ، فإن لم يأت، أو أتاه و لم يتغدّ، أو أتاه وتغدّى متراخياً عن الإتيان، فيحنث في كل صورة. وإنما جعلنا "حتى" بمعنى الفاء؛ لأنها أقرب في الاستعارة، فإذا جعلت بمعنى الفاء يحتمل التراخي، وقال بعضهم: هـ بمعنى الواو؛ لأن المجوز للاستعارة الاتصال، وهو في الواو أكثر، ولكن الأول أوجه، وإليه ذهب المصنّف كما سيأتي. جزاء اء لفعله: دليل على كون "حتى" بمعنى العطف وقد مرّ تقريره آنفاً، وقد اعترض عليه بأن بعض أفعال الشخص قد يصلح سبباً للبعض ومفضياً إليه، كما في قولنا: نازعته كي أغلب وباحثه كي أفحمه. اللهم إلا أن يقال: هذا قليل جداً، والأوجه أن يقال: إن هذا في ما نحن فيه لا يردّ، وإنما الكلام في هذا لا في جميع الأفعال، فإذا لم يصلح للجزاء فحمل على العطف بحرف الفاء. الغاية تجانس التعقيب: هذا هو وجه جعل حتى بمعنى الفاء؛ لأن في الفاء معنى الغاية، فهما متجانسان حيث يكون ما بعد "حتى" غاية لما قبلها ويتأخر في الوجود عنه كذلك ما بعد الفاء متأخر عما هو قبلها بخلاف الواو. ف: قيل لا بدّ أن يكون أتغدى بإسقاط الألف ليكون مجزوما معطوفا على "آتك". وقيل: لا بأس به؛ لأن
الاستعارة إنما هي في المعنى لا في الإعراب فتأمل. ولما فرغ من الحروف العاطفة شرع في الجارة.
Chapter of Particles of Meanings. Conjunctions. 'Hatta tasih' (until you scream): He breaks his oath if he stops before the endpoint, and it is used metaphorically for recompense in the meaning of lām kay in his saying: that is, he refrains from hitting if I do not come to you tomorrow until you feed me; then if he came to him and he did not feed him, he does not break his oath because the favor does not qualify [as a cause]. And it is the feeding of the addressee by him, a prohibition against coming, rather it is a cause for it. If the two actions are from one person, as his saying: If I do not come to you until I eat breakfast at your place, the oath is attached to both because his own action cannot be a recompense for his action, so it is interpreted as conjunction with the letter fa because the endpoint is similar to succession. 'Hatta tasih anta' (until you scream yourself): this is an example of the endpoint because the first part of the statement, i.e., what is before 'hatta'—the hitting of the addressee—is an extended matter, and the other, i.e., what is after 'hatta'—the screaming—is suitable as an ending for it due to the arousal of mercy or the occurrence of fear from someone that it is an object. [He breaks] before the endpoint, which is the screaming, and likewise he breaks if he did not hit him at all. Yes, if he hit him to the point of screaming, due to the existence of piety, he does not break. 'Hatta tughaddini' (until you feed me): here 'hatta' is not suitable for the endpoint, so it is in the meaning of lām kay, i.e., I did not come to you so that you feed me. He does not break because he came to him for feeding, and it is the action of the addressee; the speaker has no choice in it. And as for our saying it is not suitable for the endpoint, because the first part of the statement—coming—although it is suitable for extension by the occurrence of similar instances, its end—feeding—is not suitable as an ending for it. Cause from? He: that is, for coming and a motivation for increasing coming; so if it is a cause for it, 'hatta' becomes in the meaning of lām kay indicating causality and recompense, because the recompense and cause of a thing are intended from it like the endpoint from what is ended. Causality and recompense only occur if the two actions are from different doers, because his action cannot be a recompense for his action in most cases. The two actions: one before 'hatta' and the other after it from one person, as his saying: If I do not come to you etc. then my slave is free. So feeding in this example, just as it was not suitable as an ending for the first part of the statement, likewise it is not suitable for causality, because coming to another is not a cause for the feeding of the one who comes at his place due to the lack of the coming being conducive to it. So it is determined to be used metaphorically for conjunction, as if he said: If I do not come to you and I do not eat breakfast at your place, then my slave is free. 'Bihima' (by both): that is, by coming and feeding at his place without delay; so if he did not come, or he came and did not eat, or he came and ate with delay from the coming, he breaks in every case. And we only made 'hatta' in the meaning of fa because it is closer in metaphor; if it is made in the meaning of fa it admits delay. And some said it is in the meaning of waw because the enabler for metaphor is connectivity, and it is more in waw, but the first is more appropriate, and the author adopted it as will come. 'Jaza' li fi'lihi' (recompense for his action): evidence that 'hatta' is in the meaning of conjunction, and its explanation has passed earlier. And it was objected that some actions of a person may be a cause and conducive to another, as in our saying: I debated him so that I overcome and I researched him so that I defeat him. Unless it is said: this is very rare, and the more appropriate is to say: this does not apply in our case, and the discussion is about this, not about all actions. So if it is not suitable as recompense, it is interpreted as conjunction with the letter fa. 'Al-ghaya tujanis al-ta'qib' (the endpoint is similar to succession): this is the reason for making hatta in the meaning of fa, because in fa there is the meaning of endpoint, and they are similar: what is after 'hatta' is an endpoint for what precedes it and occurs later in existence; likewise what is after fa is later than what precedes it, unlike waw. 'Fa': it is said that 'ataghadda' must have the alif dropped to be jussive and conjoined to 'atika'. And it is said: it is fine because the metaphor is in meaning not in inflection; contemplate. And when he finished with the conjunctive letters, he began on the prepositions.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of the particle 'hatta' in the context of conditional oaths and its legal effects. The author begins by stating that 'hatta' can signify an endpoint (ghaya) or be used metaphorically for recompense in the meaning of lām kay. The first example is 'until you scream' in a conditional oath about hitting; if the person stops before the screaming, he breaks his oath, but if the screaming is caused by his hitting (due to mercy or fear), he does not break. The second example 'until you feed me' shows that 'hatta' here cannot mean endpoint because the action after (feeding) is not an endpoint of the action before (coming); thus it takes the meaning of lām kay (purpose). The key distinction is whether the actions are from one person or two. If both actions are from the same person, 'hatta' cannot indicate recompense because an action cannot be a recompense for itself; it is then interpreted as conjunction with fa (and then) or waw (and). The author prefers fa because it shares the sense of endpoint and succession, though the fatwa position (as per the author) is that 'hatta' in such cases means fa, allowing for delay. The passage also discusses an objection that some actions can be causes for others, but the response is that this is rare and not applicable here. The phrase 'al-ghaya tujanis al-ta'qib' explains that both fa and ghaya imply temporal succession. Finally, there is a grammatical note about the form of 'ataghadda' (with or without alif) as a conjoined verb. This analysis is crucial for determining when an oath is broken in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
حتى (hatta): A particle that can indicate an endpoint (ghaya), purpose (lām kay), or conjunction ('atf) depending on context
الغاية (al-ghaya): The endpoint or limit of an action, after which the oath may be fulfilled or broken
لام كي (lām kay): A particle indicating purpose or cause, equivalent to 'in order to'
العطف (al-'atf): Grammatical conjunction; in this context, interpreting hatta as a conjunction like fa or waw
التعقيب (al-ta'qib): Succession or immediacy; the idea that what follows occurs shortly after what precedes

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example 'If I do not come to you until you feed me' (hatta tughaddini), why does the speaker not break his oath if he comes and the addressee feeds him?
A) Because 'hatta' here means the endpoint (ghaya), and the oath is fulfilled when the endpoint is reached.
B) Because the feeding is the action of the addressee, not the speaker, so the oath is conditional on a purpose, not an action of the speaker.
C) Because 'hatta' here is interpreted as conjunction (fa), so the oath is broken only if both actions occur.
D) Because the oath is invalid due to the ambiguity of 'hatta'.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'hatta' in conditional oaths is determined by whether the action after it is an endpoint, a purpose, or a conjunction, and this determines whether an oath is broken.

Conditional Particles 'In' and 'Idha' in Hanafi Usul
Discussing the primary conditional particle 'in' and the dual usage of 'idha' for time and condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditional particles 'in' and 'idha', explain their usage in Hanafi usul, and contrast the Hanafi view that 'idha' can be purely conditional with opposing views.

Core Idea
The conditional particle 'in' is primary; 'idha' can be stripped of temporal meaning and function like 'in'.

In this lesson, we examine two key conditional particles in Hanafi usul: 'in' and 'idha'. The passage establishes that 'in' is the fundamental conditional particle, applying only to future uncertain matters. It then presents the distinctive Hanafi view that 'idha', though originally temporal, can be used purely for condition without any time implication, a position rooted in Kufic grammar and advocated by Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of conditional particles
Understand that conditional particles apply only to future uncertain matters – neither to impossible nor to inevitable events.
This distinguishes conditions from other constructions.
2
Analyze the primary particle 'in'
Recognize 'in' as the original conditional particle, a letter (harf) that by itself signifies condition.
It is the benchmark for conditional meaning.
3
Distinguish 'idha' from 'in'
Note that 'idha' is shared between time and condition, but in conditional usage it can be stripped of temporal meaning entirely.
The text states it is 'stripped of time meaning altogether'.
4
Apply the Hanafi position
Understand the practical implication: conditions with 'idha' are not temporally bound, like those with 'in'. Evidence includes jussive verbs and 'fa' in the consequence.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa and Kufic grammarians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما فرغ من بحث الحروف الجارة شرع في كلمات الشرط، فقال ومن ذلك أي من باب حروف المعاني. حروف الشرط: أي كلمات الشرط، وتسميتها بالحروف باعتبار أن الأصل في هذا الباب "إن"، وهي حرف، أو تشبيهاً لها بالحروف في عدم إفاداتها معانيها بغير إلحاق اسم آخر إليها، كما أن الحروف لا تفيد بغير الانضمام. هذا الباب أي في باب الشرط؛ لأنه مختص بمعنى الشرط ليس له معنى آخر سواه بخلاف غيره، فإن له معان آخر أيضاً، فهو يربط إحدى الجملتين بالأخرى، ويسمّى الأولى شرطاً والثانية جزاء، ويدخل على أمر معدوم متردّد بين أن يكون وبين أن لا يكون فلا يدخل على ما لا يمكن وجوده ولا على ما هو كائن لا محالة، فلذا لايدخل على معنى الحظر (أي التردّد بين الوجود والعدم لا يتحقق في الأسماء، وقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنِ امْرُؤٌ هَلَكَ (النساء: ١٧٦)، وَإِنِ امْرَأَةٌ خَافَتْ (النساء: ۱۲۸)، من قبيل الإضمار على شريطة التفسير، ومن التقديم والتأخير. و"إذا" يصلح إلخ أي كلمة إذا مشتركة بين الوقت والشرط، وإذا تستعمل في الشرط لا يلاحظ فيها معنى عموم الأوقات والأحوال، بل تجرّد عن معنى الوقت أصلاً، ويكون استعمالها كاستعمال كلمة "إن" من جعل الأول سبباً والثاني مسبّباً، ومن جزم المضارع بعدها ودخول الفاء في جزائها، كما قال الشاعر : = الاسم؛ لأن
When he finished the discussion of prepositions, he proceeded to the words of condition, saying: 'And from that, i.e., from the chapter on particles of meanings, are conditional particles.' Conditional particles: i.e., words of condition, and they are called particles because the original in this chapter is 'in', which is a particle, or by analogy to particles in that they do not convey meanings without attaching another name to them, just as particles do not benefit without joining. This chapter, i.e., the chapter on condition, because it is specific to the meaning of condition, having no other meaning besides it, unlike others, which also have other meanings. So it connects one of the two sentences to the other, calling the first the condition and the second the consequence. It enters upon a nonexistent matter that is in doubt between existing and not existing. So it does not enter upon what cannot exist nor upon what is inevitably existent. Therefore, it does not enter upon the meaning of prohibition (i.e., the doubt between existence and non-existence is not realized in nouns). And Allah's statement: 'And if a man dies' (An-Nisa 4:176) and 'And if a woman fears' (An-Nisa 4:128) are of the type of implicit mention conditioned by interpretation, and of preposing and postponing. And 'idha' is fit, etc., i.e., the word 'idha' is shared between time and condition. When 'idha' is used in condition, the meaning of generality of times and states is not observed, rather it is stripped of time meaning entirely, and its usage is like the usage of the word 'in', making the first a cause and the second an effect, and by causing the present tense verb to be in the jussive case after it and the entry of 'fa' in its consequence, as the poet said: = the noun; because
Source pages p. 351

Commentary
This passage is a textual analysis of conditional particles from a Hanafi usul perspective. The author transitions from prepositions to conditional words, stating that these are called 'particles' because the fundamental particle in condition is 'in', which is a letter, or by analogy due to their dependency on other words for full meaning. The defining feature of conditional sentences is that they link two clauses, the first (condition) and the second (consequence), and they apply to a future, uncertain matter that may or may not occur. Thus, they cannot apply to something impossible or necessarily known to exist. The author cites Quranic examples (4:176, 4:128) as instances of ellipsis (idmar) conditioned by interpretation or syntactic rearrangement. Turning to 'idha', the author notes that it is shared between temporal and conditional meanings, but in conditional usage it can be stripped of temporal significance entirely, functioning like 'in' to indicate causality and taking the jussive verb with 'fa' in the result clause. This is the position of the Kufic grammarians and Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
إن (in): The original conditional particle, used to connect a condition and a consequence.
إذا (idha): A particle shared between time and condition, but in conditional use it can be stripped of temporal meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes 'idha' from 'in' in conditional usage?
A) 'Idha' always implies a time element
B) 'Idha' can be stripped of time meaning entirely
C) 'In' cannot be used with jussive verbs
D) 'Idha' is not a particle but a noun

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi view allows 'idha' to be used purely for condition, removing temporal restrictions from conditional statements.

The Hanafi View on 'Mata' and 'Idha': Temporal vs Conditional Meaning
Analyzing the temporal and conditional meanings of 'mata' and 'idha'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi and Basran views on the particles 'mata' and 'idha' in conditional constructions and apply the linguistic analysis to derive the practical fiqh consequence in a suspended divorce scenario.

Core Idea
Mata always retains temporal meaning; idha's conditional meaning is metaphorical and can drop — a linguistic nuance with a real divorce consequence.

This passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work tackles a subtle yet impactful linguistic dispute: how do the particles 'mata' and 'idha' function in conditional statements? The answer determines the timing of a suspended divorce. By examining the Basran and Kufi (Hanafi) positions, you will see how a seemingly grammatical point directly shapes fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two particles and their base meanings
Note that both 'mata' and 'idha' are primarily temporal particles. 'Mata' is always used for time; 'idha' can also denote time but is used conditionally.
The Basrans treat both as purely temporal; the Hanafis differentiate.
2
Understand the Hanafi vs Basran positions
The Basrans say both particles retain time in conditionals. The Kufis and Abu Hanifa hold that 'mata' always retains time, while 'idha' is used conditionally but its conditional meaning is metaphorical and can drop in contexts like interrogatives.
The key distinction: 'mata' requires the conditional meaning to be inherent (lazim), 'idha' does not.
3
Analyze the argument: why 'mata' retains time but 'idha' can lose it
The text argues: if 'mata' retains time even in conditional use, then 'idha' should too, but the metaphorical meaning with 'idha' is not necessary (ghayr lazimah). Thus in interrogatives like 'Mata tadhhab?' (When will you go?), 'mata' still denotes time, but 'idha' in such contexts loses any conditional nuance.
The Hanafi view uses the concept of 'majaz' (metaphor) to explain the conditional usage.
4
See the thamarah al-khilaf in the divorce example
The passage gives a concrete case: A man says to his wife 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced.' According to Abu Hanifa (Kufi view), the condition is purely conditional (time dropped), so divorce only occurs when divorce becomes impossible (i.e., after death). According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two disciples), 'idha' retains some temporality, so the condition is fulfilled immediately after the statement, causing immediate divorce.
This shows how the linguistic dispute has a real fiqh outcome.
5
Evaluate which view yields the practical ruling
The text presents Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position (mufta bihi). Understand the reasoning: because 'idha' is used conditionally, the time meaning drops, making the condition indefinite until a time when divorce cannot occur.
Contrast this with 'mata': if he had said 'Mata lam utalliquki...', the condition would be tied to a specific time, likely immediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب حروف المعاني ["متى" وحكمها]
حروف الشرط
وعند البصريين وهو قولهما هي للوقت، ويجازى بها من غير سقوط الوقت عنها
خاصة
معنى
بحال
مثل "متى"، فإنها للوقت لا يسقط عنها بحال والمجازات بها لازمة في غير موضوع
كلمة متى موضوعة
معنى الوقت للظرفية
الاستفهام، وبإذا غير لازمة بل هي في حين الجواز.
[
"من" و "ما" و"كل" و"كلما"]
...
(ثم يذكر الخلاف) هذا ما ذهب إليه الكوفيون وأبو حنيفة له وهو قولهما أي كلمة إذا موضوعة ويجازى بها: أي يستعمل استعمال كلمات المجازات، أي الشرط مجازاً.
غير موضع الاستفهام فإن في موضع الاستفهام يسقط عن "متى" معنى المجازات كما في قوله: متى تذهب، فإذا لم يسقط معنى الوقت عن متى مع لزوم المجازات لها فالأولى أن لا يسقط ذلك المعنى عن إذا؛ لأن المجازات بما غير لازمة، وإليه أشار بقوله في حين الجواز فإن قلت حينئذ لا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز؛ لأن معنى الوقت حقيقة لها، ومعنى الشرط مجاز، وكلاهما مرادان في الاستعمال كما قلتم قلت لا يلزم الجمع في المراد؛ لأنها لم تستعمل إلا في معنى الوقت الذي هو معنى حقيقي لها، ومعنى الشرط إنما لزم تضمناً من غير قصد وإرادة، كالمبتداء يتضمن معنى الشرط بلا قصد ويظهر ثمرة الخلاف في قول من قال لامرأته : إذا لم أطلقك فأنتِ طالق، حيث لا يقع الطلاق عنده ما لم يمت أحدهما ؛ لأن "إذا" هنا للشرط فسقط منها معنى الوقت، فهو علّق الطلاق ولا يثبت عدم الطلاق في حياتهما لإمكان الطلاق، فإذا مات أحدهما عدم الطلاق لعدم المحل أو لعدم من يوقع الطلاق، فإذا تحقق الشرط وقع الطلاق المطلق في حال صحة التعليق، وعندهما يقع الطلاق إذا فرغ من لأن "إذا" يسقط عنه معنى الوقت، فصار المعنى في زمان لم أطلقك فأنت طالق، فإذا فرغ من هذا الكلام وجد زمان لم يطلقها فيه، فيقع الطلاق في الحال لوجود الشرط كما في متى.
كلامه؛
في هذا الباب: أي في باب الشرط، فمن لذوات من يعقل كما في قوله تعالى: مَنْ عَمِلَ صَالِحان) (النحل: ۹۷)
Chapter on Particles of Meanings: 'Mata' and its ruling. Particles of condition. According to the Basrans, and it is their statement, they (mata and idha) are for time, and they are used with jazā (response) without the time meaning being dropped. Like 'mata', it is for time, it is not dropped in any case, and its metaphorical usage (majāz) is necessary in other than the interrogative context. The word 'mata' is placed for the meaning of time for adverbiality. The interrogative, and with 'idha' it is not necessary but rather it is at the time of permissibility. This is what the Kufis and Abu Hanifa held, and it is their statement: that the word 'idha' is placed and used conditionally, i.e., used as a particle of condition metaphorically. Other than the interrogative context, because in the interrogative context the metaphorical meaning drops from 'mata', as in his saying: 'when will you go?' So if the meaning of time is not dropped from 'mata' with the necessity of metaphor for it, it is more proper that that meaning should not drop from 'idha', because metaphorical usage with it is not necessary. And he pointed to this with his saying 'at the time of permissibility.' If you say then there is no necessary combination between reality and metaphor because the meaning of time is real for it, and the meaning of condition is metaphorical, and both are intended in usage as you claim. I say: there is no necessary combination in intention because it is not used except in the meaning of time which is its real meaning, and the meaning of condition only accompanies implicitly without intent and will, like the sentence that implies condition without intent. And the fruit of the dispute appears in the statement of one who said to his wife: 'If I do not divorce you, then you are divorced.' According to him (Abu Hanifa) divorce does not occur unless one of them dies, because 'idha' here is for condition, so the meaning of time drops from it. Thus he suspended divorce, and the absence of divorce is not established in their lifetimes due to the possibility of divorce. If one of them dies, divorce is absent due to absence of subject or absence of the one who would execute divorce. So if the condition is realized, the suspended divorce occurs in the state of validity of the comment. According to them (the two, i.e. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), divorce occurs when he finishes his speech, because 'idha' causes the meaning of time to drop, so the meaning becomes 'in a time when I did not divorce you, you are divorced.' So when he finishes this speech, a time is found in which he did not divorce her, so divorce occurs immediately due to existence of the condition, as in 'mata'.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh work discusses the particles of condition, specifically 'mata' and 'idha'. The author begins by noting that according to the Basran grammarians, both 'mata' and 'idha' primarily denote time, and they can be used in conditional constructions without losing their temporal meaning. However, the Kufis and Imam Abu Hanifa hold a different view: they consider 'mata' to always retain its temporal meaning even in conditional usage (metaphorically), whereas 'idha' is used conditionally but this conditional meaning is not inherent and can be dropped in certain contexts like interrogatives. The text then presents a refinement: even in conditional use, the temporal meaning of 'mata' remains, so the metaphorical conditional meaning is implied without being separately intended. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is illustrated with a divorce example: If a man says to his wife 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced,' according to Abu Hanifa (following the Kufi view) the divorce does not take effect immediately because 'idha' is purely conditional, stripping away time; thus, a time must pass where divorce is possible, so only after one party dies (making divorce impossible) does the condition realize. But according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two disciples), 'idha' retains some temporality, so the condition is fulfilled immediately after the statement (since at that moment he did not divorce her), and divorce occurs at once. This shows how a seemingly linguistic nuance has significant fiqh consequences.

Key Terms
متى (mata): A particle of time that always retains its temporal meaning even in conditional usage; the conditional meaning is metaphorical and inherent.
إذا (idha): A particle of time, but in conditional usage it can imply condition metaphorically; its conditional meaning can drop in non-conditional contexts.
المجاز (al-majaz): Metaphorical usage; here, the use of a time particle for conditional meaning is considered metaphorical.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the divorce example using 'idha', why does Abu Hanifa rule that divorce does not occur immediately?
A) Because the condition is not explicitly stated.
B) Because 'idha' drops the temporal meaning, making the condition indefinite.
C) Because the wife must agree to the divorce.
D) Because 'idha' always indicates future time.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi distinction between 'mata' (always temporal) and 'idha' (conditional meaning metaphorical) directly affects whether a suspended divorce occurs immediately or only upon death.
